THE RED PAGE.
One imagines that when Daley’s book of verses
is published—it is promised in a week or three—
“A Sunset Fantasy” will remain the most
fragrant of all the fragrant poems he has
penned. This opinion, of course, is subject to
revision ; for Daley’s Bulletin contributions have
covered such a space of time that perforce one
must have unwillingly forgotten some and the
value of some—and, in any case, relationship
in a book puts scattered fragments in different
lights and perspective. But even Daley’s book
will doubtfully hold anything finer than the finest
thing in the way of picturesque poetry yet done
in Australia. “ A Sunset Fantasy ”is only the
glorification of an old sentiment, but what a
sweet and tender sentiment, and what a glow of
glory ! The images!—the sun sinking like a
peony drowning in wine-and this is surpassed for
idea, though not for picture, by the next-verse
metaphor. And the phrases—the ‘‘gold wave-
way,” the “sea-paradise,” the “ wind-harp in the
lilac- tree ” ! And those three splendid stanzas—
Our ships shall be of sandal built,
Like ships in old-world tales,
Carven with cunning art and gilt,
And winged with scented sails
Of silver silk, whereon the red
Great gladioli burn,
A rainbow-flag at her mast-head,
A rose-flag at her stem.
And, perching on the point above
Whereon the pennon blows,
The figure of a flying dove,
And in her beak a rose.
has vision ever been swathed in more luxurious
verbiage? It almost makes one doubt the
supremacy of the Idea, such aesthetic delight
comes to a lover of words in this idealess, effort-
less presentation of Beauty.
* * * *
oes not depreciate our prized bush-
baliadists in saying that the publication of Daley’s
book will be the most m niorable Australian
literary event since the publication of Kendall
and Brunton Stephens. Daley has not the rugged
force of Lawson or Boake ; but he has the sense
of form which permits to write nothing bad or
ungraceful he is our classic, our dexterous master
of poetical arts. Less Australian, he is more
literary—appeals not to stronger, but to subtler
emotions—peacocking it exquisitely in a garden of
enamelled roses. For the color of verse, we have
I,v ' n .g on,y . ■ lwo . to rival him—Brunton Stephens
R°deric Quinn —the first with more intellect,
the second with more glamour, neither singing
with the rich, honeyed tone which Daley shares
with Kendall. To each his honour: we have
honours for all of them. And this paragraph
closes in an attitude of happy expectancy.
It seems that there are actually persons who
look to this Page for literary guidance, and one
of them complains that no opinion has been for-
mulated regarding a recent Australian publica-
tion, “Dead Men’s Tales,” by Charles Junor
(Geo. Robertson & Co.). This spurs the writer’s
confession of grief that he hasn’t any opinion.
His attitude to said reliques is that of Gilead P.
Beck to Browning’s poems—he can’t read ’em.
Three attempts—one hopeful, one hesitating, and
one desperate—have hardly been able to take him
further than the “lariat” by which the Wes-
tralian explorer on page 1 “retains control of his
horse.” This is more to be regretted because the
tales are “rattling good’’—Robert Barr, who is
called a “ popular author ” in the introduction,
says so. One can only apologise for one’s incapa-
city—and make inadequate amends by expressing
frank admiration at “.Aguerra’s Bride,” an easily-
read poem which drops in casually later on.
* Aguerra’s Bride ’ is a legend of South America,
where*
Plaintive the plover’s call, warning
Mustang and wild deer, the keen
Puma prowls. So breaks the morning
Light, on the plains Argentine.
Two verses of excellent atmosphere bring us to
the characters—
Dusk descends. Moans the wind eerie;
Riders twain, pass through the waste ;
Dark-faced the man spurring weary
Horses. Beside him, in haste,
Presses a fair girl. Her laughter
Stirs no response. Looking round
Something meaning thrills—-After,
Both ride in silence profound.
Here the fine staccato movement of the verse
cleverly reflects the emotion of hero and heroine.
Carina loves Jos4 ; but a traitor has betrayed
their plot to Aguerra—
“ Death to yourself. For Garina
Wedlock with Josi ; and she—
Back returned safe to arena,
Loved since her childhood—will be
Happy and free with the traitor,
Sworn his past misdeeds to mend."
So wrote an ally. No straighter
Word he, Aguerra, could send.
So Aguerra takes Carina out riding, and lyrically
quagmires her—
Turned she her head, as he beckoned,
But for an instant. Drew back
Aguerra,, swift. In a second,
Reined in her horse on its track,
Hurled her down in the pit ghastly J
Stood he then by its edge close ;
Lit cigarillo ; and lastly
Hissed, with an oath, “ Adios I”
This has, as we say, the true singing note. Then
Carina’s agonies and entreaties are depicted in
five impetuous stanzas till
Birds on the wing stay their flight ;
Forth sombre forms, fear instilling,
Glide. The sun sets. It is night.
The end is a glimpse of Aguerra rushing on his-
road ad inferos —
Shivers a moment Aguerra ;
Slowly remounts then his steed;
Mutters aloud ; then in terror
Gallops forth. Dire is his need
Past recollection to sever,
Whispers a voice in the gloom
“ Satan’s beside you for ever
Torments of Hell are your doom."
This is “ rattling good.”
“ Compton ” commentators—
Alex. M. : “Compton” complains that aesthetic dis-
putants start “in the middle of things”—and himself
starts “in the middle of things,” according to his own
showing! But, as I contend, he has commenced where,
by the very nature of the cas>', he couldn’t help com-
mencing-not in the middle of things, but right at the
beginning of them; for the simple reason that, since no
man ciin transcend his own intellect, every man’s particu-
lar point of view is, for hint , tho virtual all-there-isof visu-
alitv. Nevertheless, it is upon the majority-basis that we
are obliged to depend for our most nearly absolute (though
logically inconclusive) criteria of conviction. “ What
all men think,” ay Aristotle, “ that, we say, is.” In
the last analysis “fact” is “opinion.” Yet we can
practically differentiate “ matter-of-fact ” from matter-
of-opinion.” If I say a certain wooden-door is made of
iron, I shall have in no way altered the facts of the case.
The material of the door will still possess the nature and
properties which to everybody else are connoted by the
term “ wood ; ” and, since this nature is a matter ob-
jectively demonstrable to the material senses, my setting-
up for myself of a private nomenclature will be
in no way pertinent to the real issue. But
take, on the - other hand, a man born, say,
in France, of German-descended, English-born par-
ents. He would be, in one opinion, German (extrac
tion); in another, English (immediate parentage); and,
in a third, French (nativity). And since, outside an
arbitrary legal or political status, there would be no
tangible basis of determination to the three discordant,
aforesaid, he would continue to be—with all the be-ness
possible in the case—French, German, or English, re-
spectively. With the critic in .(Esthetics it is the same.
His personal standard is the only standard by which he
can judge. There is a tangible mathematic—‘a discretive
and formulable logic—but, were the .Esthetic to be no
longer the irreducible and elusive, it would also be no
longer the Esthetic.
Ambrose Bierce (in The Anti-Philistine) : “ I say,
you ; ’ bawled a fat ox in a stall to a lusty young ass
%ho was braying outside ; “ the like of that is not in
good taste.” “In whose good taste, my adipose cen-
sor?” inquired the ass, not too respectfully. “ Why—
h’m—ah, I mean it does not suit ME. You ought to
bellow.” “ May I enquire how it happens to be any of
your business whether I bellow or bray, or do both or
do neither?” “ I cannot tell you,” answered the critic,
shaking his head despondingly ; “ I do not at all under-
stand it. I can only say that I have been accustomed to
censure all discourse that differs from my own.” “Ex-
actly,” said the ass ; “you have sought to make an art of
impertinence by mistaking prejudices for principles. In
‘taste’you have invented a word incapable of defini-
tion, to denote an idea impossible of expression ; and by
employing in connection therewith the words ‘ good ’
and‘bad,’ you indicate a merely'subjective process in
terms of an objective quality. Such presumption trans-
cends the limit of the merely impudent, and passes into
the boundless empyrean of pure cheek." At the close of
this remarkable harangue, the bovine critic was at a loss
for language to express his disapproval. So he said the
speech was in bad taste.
T.D.: Red Page slips with the remark that ‘a man
stone-deaf might be a master-composer ’ if sound vibra-
tions were not external to the musician. Beethoven teas
stone-deaf during a fruitful period of his career, and
never heard some of his own most glorious aggregations
of sound— i.e., by auricular demonstration. The musician
glances a composition through, and mentally hears the
sounds. But a ‘Moonlight ’ Sonata as the soul’s outpour-
ing of a man entirely deaf from birth is no less beyond
conception than a five-thousand-guinea Madonna out-
lined by Kipling’s sightless battle-* dabster.’ At the same
time, Musical Art and Pictorial ditto are not such distant
cousins as ‘ Compton ’ would believe, and says he does be-
lieve. Without an arbitrary scale of tones and semitones
or an arbitrary set of primary and secondary colors neither
would be possible. With infinitesimal gradations of
color, sound and form at her disposal, Nature herself—
grandest of all artists—oannot, apparently, evade set
rules and limitations. ' From the innumerable sounds
existent in nature twelve . . . form the entire material
of the composer.’ Exactly, and ‘of the six hundred
billions of colors in the spectrum ’ we have a chromatic
soale of seven to work upon. For the rest, the painter
mixes his colors, and the musician mixes his chords—and
each occasionally begets the imaginative ‘ light that
never was on sea or land ; ’ or imagines he does.”

The nearly-finished Sydney City Markets are
an admirable lesson in literary criticism. This because
Art has only one face, and Literature, Architecture, and
the rest are mere facets. Note how an author-architect
can waste fine material and a fine opportunity. The
Markets architect had so much space to fill up, and the
beginning and middle of his storey are good enough—he
opens with a run of ordinary shop-windows, and passes to
a decorative facade of pillars which are sufficiently orna-
mental if not particularly novel or original—as we say
locally. Then came the climax—the roof—the olinching
assault on the skies of emotion ; and the architect failed
miserably he hadn’t any climax. So he covered his
shortcomings with the squattest, grotesquest, ugliest
pepper-pot-tops of little domes imaginable, and—down
goes his tale to the waste-basket? Alas, no! he has
written in stone, or its equivalent—l’iramortel malgrd lui!
The antics of Sydney chess-experts surprise
people who have read of the solemn dignity of players of
older countries and the old school, and people who have
become acquainted with the fine chesterfieldy politeness
which the better-than-royal game is traditionally calcu-
lated to nourish. Here, if a player has any dignity at
all, he invariably stands on it. The other day N.S.VV.
played Victoria, and the N.S.VV. champion was invited to
play second fiddle and didn’t. If he had played first
fiddle he would have had to place his men in order of
merit and battle; and the men were apparently so
frightened that their No. 3 might be his No. 5, or vice
versa, that they carefully clipped his wings beforehand.
(The metaphor is mixed to suit the state of things.)
Very obviously what is wanted is the suggested N.S.VV.
Chess Association, in which the good sense of the strong
clubs at Broken Hill, Wagga, and elsewhere can settle the
silly squabbles of the Sydney coteries—some of whose
best players play with the worst grace.

Colorist. If he paints the sky black and the grass <c

N. : “ With all kindly respect for the memory
of Mrs. Oliphant, a unique woman in the four-in-hanc
style of book-making, I dislike her last, work, on the
Blackwoods. It is sycophantic. The whole moral is the
superiority of the publisher to the author. A publisher
ought to have written it, not an author; but then a pub-
lisher couldn’t, and here comes in the primal eldest curse
of the author. Now, I will be bound Mrs. O. had her
grudges against the B’s. I will take my davy they didn’t
treat her as she thought she ought to be treated. Yet
she crawls-for a big-money book. Oh dear, what rush-
lights were Christopher North, Lockhart, Maginn, De
Quincey—especially—Bulwer, Hamlev, George Eliot—and
Mrs. Oliphant— to the God-gifted Blackwoods 1 ”
Bernard Shaw comments on the Shakespeare-
Ibsen comparison—“ Judged by intellectual force and
dramatic insight, apart from beauty of expression. Ibsen
comes out with a double first-class; Shakespeare comes
out hardly anywhere. In any language of the world
‘Brand,’ ‘Peer Gynt,’ and ‘ Emperor or Galilean ’ prove
their author a thinker of extraordinary penetration, and
a moralist of international influence. Turn from them to
‘To be or not to be,’ or ‘The seven ages of man,’ and
imagine, if you can, anybody more critical than a village
Schoolmaster being imposed on by such platitudinous
fudge. The comparison does not honor Ibsen : it makes
Shakespeare ridiculous.”

Au temps des mftres ils ont chant 6
Mes lfevres qui cfedent
Et mes longs cheveux, tifedes
Commeune pluie d’4tA
Au temps des vignes ils ont chantfi
Mes yeux entreclos qui rayonnent

CHARLES VAN LERBERGHE.
[Translations may be sent.]
A SUNSET FANTASY.
Spell-bound by a sweet fantasy
At evenglow I stand
Beside a strange sardonyx sea
That rings a sunset land.
The rich lights fade out one by one :
Like to a peony
Drowning in wine, the round, red sun
Sinks down in that strange sea.
With red clouds has he chapleted
His brows, like him who tied
Of old a rose-wreath round his head,
And drank to Death, and died.
His wake across Ihe ocean-floor
In a long glory lies,
Like a gold wave-way to the shore
Of some sea-paradise.
My dream flies after him, and I
Am in another land ;
The sun sets in another sky,
And we sit hand in hand.
Grey eyes look into mine: such eyes
I think the angels’ are —
Soft as the soft light in the skies
When shines the morning star ;
And tremulous as morn when thin
Gold lights begin to glow,
Revealing the bright soul within
As dawns the sun below.
So, hand in hand, we watch the sun
Burn down the Western deeps,
Dreaming a charmed dream as one
Who in enchantment sleeps.
A dream of how we twain some day,
Careless of map or chart,
Will both take ship and sail away
Into the sunset’s heart.
Our ship shall be of sandal built,
Like ships in old-world tales,
Carven with cunning art and gilt,
And winged with scented sails
Of silver silk, whereon the red
Great gladioli burn,
A rainbow-flag at her mast-head,
A rose-flag at her stern.
And, perching on the point above
Whereon the pennon blows,
The figure of a flying dove,
And in her beak a rose.
And from the fading land the breeze
Shall bring us, blowing low’,
Old odours and old memories
And airs of long ago —
A melody that has no words
Which are of speech a part,
Yet touching all the deepest chords
That tremble in the heart.
A scented song blown oversea,
As though, from bow’rs of bloom,
A wind-harp in a lilac-tree
Breathed music and perfume.
And we, no more with longing pale,
Will hearken to its blow—
I, in the shadow of the sail,
You, in the sunset glow.
For with the fading land our fond
Old fears shall all fade out,
Paled by the light from shores beyond
The dread of Death or Doubt.
And when Death from a cloud above
His awful shadow flings,
The Spirit of Immortal Love
Will shield us with his wings.
He is the Lord of dreams divine,
And lures us with his smiles
Along the splendour opaline
Unto the Blessed Isles.
VIOTOI J. DALEY.

YOUNG MEN
MAN !
Will you allow yourself to fall into

When your manhood begins

Following are some of the symp-

Do your facil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?

Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitehings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble ?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy?
Do you have ringing in the ears ?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim ?

Has'the brightness left your eyes?

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the General Election following the
dissolution of Parliament in ’6B Gladstone was en-
thusiastically returned by Greenwich. In ’7B he
was compelled to notify that he would not be able
to represent the place after next election, as so
many electors were dissatisfied with his neglect
of local requirements. They were proud of having
as their representative one of the greatest orators
of the century, but when in his wide-eyed im-
perial enthusiasm he forgot the Greenwich Town
Pump, the said Pump made ready to biff him
severely next time he turned its handle. Which
shows what a powerful factor the Town Pump is
even in the Higher Politics.
The Grey Road to the River.
We were riding down the grey road to the
river
When the peeping stars played hide-and-seek
above ;
In the warm air, with the moonbeams all a-quiver,
We were jesting on the fallacy of Love ;
Till, where a darksome gully throws its mantle
o’er the track,
I whispered—what till then I never knew —
And the merry forest echoes laughing rang her
answer back:
“ Love ? Love l Oh l I like you—yes, Ido / ”
I felt my fondling hand flung off as I came near,
And saw the brown horse fly the moonlit rise ;
And all the way to Bland’s he kept that distance
clear;
Then I gained ! He flung the flint-stones in my
eyes.
And the bay horse lengthened grandly, and I felt
my pulses tingle
As slowly to her bridle-hand I drew,
And the shod hoofs on the ironstone rang out a
merry jingle—
Lov-ing — Love —Oh ! —I like you — Yes, I do l
And I rode beside her, held her hand and chaffed
her
Till 1 saw the drooping lashes’ glistening dew—
On the sweet, grave face no sign of bygone
laughter;
Then we stopped—suggestive home-lights just
in view.
And now, when stars are peeping through the
leaf-wrack up above
And I ask her—“ After all can Love be true ? ”
You should hear the low, rich laughter and the
merry answer —“ Love ?
Why — l — love — you. No, I like you. Oh, I
do l”
W.A.
L.M.D.
Makin’ Tucker.
When you’re only “ makin’ tucker,” then you get
a taste of luck
Of the very worst description, but it ain’t no use
to buck.
You’ve got to grin and bear it, to sing and see it
through,
And wait for times that’s coinin’ to make a better
do.
Makin’ tucker, only tucker, eatin’ all you get,
Takin’ blocks and pillars out, and drivin’ in the
wet;
Pannin’ off the tailin’ heaps that other fellows
tubs,
Battlin’ with bad luck and feelin’ hard luck’s
rubs.
You wouldn’t like to boast about the kit of tools
you use;
Your pannin’ dish is full of holes (Lord knows
what gold you lose);
But a pennyweight or so a week won’t buy
another one,
So it’s do the best you can until you strike a
payin’ run,
For you’re only makin’ tucker, eatin’ all you get;
Every hole’s a duffer while misfortune’s got you
set.
But it kinder seems consolin’ rememberin’ other
chaps
Is feelin’ just the same as you, p’raps gettin’
harder raps.
Your clothes is none too good to wear; they’re
patched about a bit.
You smoke a pipe that’s hard to draw, some days
it’s scarcely lit—
The tucker that you’re makin’ is the poorest
people knows ;
And your leavin’s is so little that it won’t attract
the crows.
Makin’ tucker, barely tucker, it'll never make you
fat;
Fossickin’old gullies out and sinkin’ on the flat;
Turnin’ over worked-out ground, and round the
fire o’ nights,
Discussin’ with your comrades the price of miners’
rights.
But it ain’t no use of growlin ’cause the worst of
times you’ve got,
It’s all in life and diggin’, and you ’ave to take
the lot.
You’ve got to go on sinkin’ holes, and hopin’ for a
patch,
Knowin’ that you’re only one out of a biggish
batch.
Makin’ tucker, earnin’ tucker, eatin’ all you earn.
Diggin’ hard and waitin’ round until your luck’ll
turn ;
Takin’ blocks and pillars out, and pannin’ tailin’-
heaps ;
Battlin’ with misfortune, and misfortune playin’
keeps.
So it’s shoulder gear and move around to other
fields afar ;
There’s sure to be a dip somewhere—Life ain’t all
midlock bar;
And, averagin’ year and year, you’ll mostly al-
ways find
You’ve made your tucker and a bit above ; so
never mind !
If you’re only makin’ tucker, keep on diggin’,
don’t be beat;
You’ll strike a gutter some day that ’ll put you on
your feet.
Try the likely places first, then pick into them
that’s not —
It’s all in life and workin’, and you’re bound to
work the lot.

SATURDAY, JUNE 11, 1898.
The Buie of the Minority.
Last week Yietoria, S.A. and Tasmania all
accepted the Federation Bill by large
majorities. New South Wales also voted by
a large majority in favor of the Bill; and as
the cry of Want, Lyne, Heydon, Norton,
Rosa, Tommy Dodd and the Telegraph and
Star has all along been that they desire
above all things to preserve the sacred right
of majority rule, a fine chance here presents
itself. At present, because the 80,000
minimum, fixed by the Amending Act at the
time when New South Wales broke its
agreement with the other provinces, was not
reached, the small crowd bosses the situation
and the large crowd goes for nothing. An
anti-Federal minority is allowed, by a fraudu-
lent misuse of the powers of Parliament, to
overrule a Federal majority ; and, strangely
enough, the party which has all along been
demanding majority rule at the top of its
dissonant voice is now the minority which
rules. Also it seems quite satisfied with the
situation.
* * * *
The Federation Bill was stranded in New
South Wales, as it was intended it should be,
on the 80,000 minimum. When the original
agreement was made it was decided that to
accept the Bill there must be a majority
in its favor —only that and nothing more.
'ldle conference of Premiers drafted the
Enabling Bill on these lines, Premier Reid
being the leading member of the conference.
Then the Reid Government straightway
broke its word and inserted the first mini-
mum of 50,000. Three other provinces
agreed to follow her lead, the minimum
fixed being 50,000 in Victoria, and 6000 each
m Tasmania and Westralia. South Australia
alone went on the straight, Democratic prin-
ciple of majority rule pure and simple. The
Convention was elected on this arrangement;
it was tacitly accepted by the people ; and
each province went into the movement upon
the understanding that the others really
meant to give Federation a fair show. Had
IN ew South Wales fixed a practically impos-
sible minimum at the beginning, the other
provinces would probably have recognised
that the whole affair was intended to
be a mere waste of time and money
and stationery, and have stood out.
Then, when the work was half - way
through, the soulless and conscienceless
parochial gang which forms the bulk of
New South Wales Parliament broke its
agreement, and would have sacrificed its
honor, only it had none, by raising the
minimum to 80,000 —well knowing that the
tired Cornstalk never voted with sufficient
energy to give 80,000 votes to any one party.
The people were not consulted in any
way about the matter. They were dis-
franchised wholesale by their hired servants
in the Legislature without even the pretence
that they had assented to their disfranchise-
ment. There was no reason assigned—
except that Parliament said it considered its
act of treachery advisable. The measure
which enacted this deed of repudiation
was introduced and advocated by a party
which had always been bitterly anti-Federal
and whose object was almost admittedly to
wreck all possibilities of union. It was
accepted by the turn-coat Ministry of Eight
Gon-feanng Iscariots ; the Reid Cabinet
did not call on its party majority to vote
against this gross breach of faith, and in
addition to that negative act of treachery it
gave a good many individual votes in favor
of it. And the result is that a minority
bossed the situation on Friday, and a
majority was nowdiere.
* * * •
Obviously, the coming New' South Wales
general election must be fought on this same
question of Majority Rule. The one issue
to be put before the country is Federation
or Parochialism. The country has already
done its share of the work about as
well as the tired Cornstalk ever does
it. It elected a Parliament with a majority
of professed Federationists. It accepted
the agreement that it had to raise a
50,000 minimum and a majority before union
could be accomplished. It elected its ten
Convention deleg ites on that understmding.
It supplied both the majority and the 50,000
minimum with a big margin in each case,
and it did this in the face of the loudest,
wildest blast of misrepresentation and hard
lying that ever blew in New South Wales.
For a chronically tired community, New
South Wales did its part of the work reason-
ably well. The Cornstalk people kept faith
all through with the people of Victoria,
Tasmania, and South Australia, and these
three provinces kept faith with them m re-
turn. And now the only difficulty in the
way is that the Legislature (half of it acting
witji no mandate from the people, and the
other half—the nominee half—being abso-
lutely irresponsible and having no connection
with the people at all) has chosen to decree
that the majority shall not rule, and that
the bargain upon which the people
first went into the movement shall
be broken. Therefore, the question to be
put to the nation at the next election is
whether or not New South Wales shall
repair that mean act of treachery, and go
back to the 50,000 minimum which was the
basis of its agreement with the other pro-
vinces and with its own people ; and de-
clare by Act of Parliament that the Federal
Bill was carried by the vote of 3rd June,
seeing that the vote conformed to all the
conditions of the original compact. There is
no other question which is worth dealing
with at present, except the incidental neces-
sity of casting out the politicians, and parti-
cularly the Ministry of Eight GoD-fearing
Iscariots, whose breach of faith handed over
New' South Wales last Friday to the rule of
the minority. Also, considering that the
New South Wales nominee Council is mainly
anti-Federal, and will probably resist any
attempt at enforcing majority rule, and
further considering that Premier Reid now
states that he isn’t going to abolish the
nominee principle, and proposes to keep the
Council very much as it is, another inciden-
tal necessity is that of getting a new Govern-
ment which will really reform the Council
when that body gets in the road.
* * * *
The Sydney Telegraph, the great anti-
Federal organ of New South Wales, already
announces that the province must set to
work and get a more acceptable scheme pre-
pared, and generally start the struggle
afresh. But it is best to be off' with the old
struggle before commencing the new one,
and in this matter the other provinces have
also to be reckoned with. Their position in
the matter is as follows : They have twice
accepted invitations from New South Wales
to federate, and each time New South Wales
has deliberately broken her bargain and
wrecked the movement at the last moment.
In the present case they were led into the
movement at the urgent request of the New
South Wales Government. They agreed, at
the invitation of the New South Wales
Premier, to an Enabling Bill which left the
final decision in the hands of a popular
majority. When the New South Wales
Government smashed its brittle promise and
decided to give the cause of disunion 50,000
start, Victoria, Tasmania, and W.A. gave way
toherforthe sake of uniformity. Theyallowed
a New South Wales politician to lead the Con-
vention. They left the drafting of the Bill
to a committee of three, in which New
South Wales had two representatives and
S.A. one, while Victoria, Tasmania and
Westralia were wholly unrepresented.
They gave way to New South Wales on
almost every point, and it was largely
to oblige that province that the rivers, debts
and railways remain under provincial con-
trol. The New South Wales delegates voted
strongly for equal State representation in
the Senate and for the financial settlement,
and got their way on these as on most other
matters. Their hungry grab to secure the
Federal capital for Sydney, and thus gather
in extra population and force up land values
and rents, was the only grab of any import-
ance which didn’t come off. The other pro-
vinces loyally accepted the Bill which New
South Wales had mostly framed, and gave it
a vast majority. New South Wales also
accepted it by a majority, and gave it much
more than the minimum number of votes
provided in the original bargain—the bargain
on which the other provinces went into the
movement. If the New South Wales Legis-
lature has chosen to enact that the majority
shall not rule, and that the minimum shall
not be adhered to, obviously the other pro-
vinces cannot help that. But, when
the New South Wales minority asks Vic-
toria and Tasmania and South Australia
to abandon the measure which has been
accepted by the majority, not only in Vic-
toria and Tasmania and South Australia, but
in New South Wales also, and, instead, to
accept some measure which will satisfy the
aforesaid New South Wales minority, they
will probably reply to that unspeakably im-
pudent request in a way that is hardly polite.
If there is any faith in the Government or
Parliament of New South Wales, it must go
back to the 50,000 minimum—the basis on
which New South Wales induced the other
States to join the latest Federal crusade.
There was never any reason for departing
from it except the loud, coarse insolence of
the Cabbage-Tree Mob, bent on showing
that New South Wales was so far the boss of
the movement that it could make or break or
alter the whole basis of negotiation at its own
sole pleasure. And if all the howling of
Want and Lyne and Heydon and Reid and
their newspaper barrackers about the neces-
sity of preserving majority rule isn’t sheer
hypocrisy, then they must vote that the
Federal Bill, having the support of the
majority, shall be accepted. But they won’t
vote that way all the same. Majority rule is
the very last thing that the Want crowd is
looking for.
The Parochial Pecksniff Paper.
On Saturday morning, when it was definitely
known that the N.S.W. Federationists, though
in a large majority, had not got the 80,000
minimum, Sydney Telegraph came out with
an eloquent article calling on the Federal
majority to keep quiet while the anti-Federal
minority ran the show :
To eliminate the minimum vote provision by retrospec-
tive legislation would be a distinct violation of the whole
agreement under which yesterday’s battle was fought and
won. To “ validate ” the unsuccessful referendum in that
way would be as unjustifiable, even were there a majority
•to do it, as to invalidate the Convention. The Conven-
tion and the limit principle are both creations of the same
Parliamentary Act, and if one were tampered with the
whole might as well be treated in the same wav. When
it was proposed to make the limit 80,000, this
journal opposed it. We fought against it, fairly and
strenuously, as we did against several other details of the
Enabling Act. We were defeated, and loyally accepted
our defeat. Had the Commonwealth Bill been carried
yesterday, we would have been prepared to accept the con-
stitutionally-recorded verdict of the people with equal
grace. If the Conventionists had won, it would have
remained in the power of Parliament to refuse to submit
this Bill, to which a majority of its members are opposed,
for the approval of the Imperial Government. JSIo pro-
posal of that kind, however, would have received a
moment's consideration. This Bill, as a whole, is dead ;
the country has stamped its heel upon it; it cannot be
resurrected. The duty of all true Federationists is,
therefore, to join together with the view of producing one
that will be more acceptable. In doing this all the good
points of the rejected measure are available for incorpora-
tion in the one that is to take its place. Yesterday there
were two Federal parties in New South Wales,' which
nullified each other. To-day there is but one, and out of
their union, with the light and experience that the con-
test has brought, should come a Bill worthy of Federation,
worthy of Australia, and thoroughly adequate to the
needs of a free, fraternal, and progressive people.
* * * *
This is the Telegraph, of June 4, when, by
means of the “ faked ” minimum, it had been
decided that 71,000 Federalists should knuckle
down to less than 66,000 anti-Federalists.
Therefore the Telegraph points out enthusias-
tically that, if the majority takes steps to enforce
its will by repealing the 80,000 minimum, and
going back to the original agreement on -which
N.S.W. first asked the other provinces to join
it, and objects to be jumped on by the minority,
it will be a very dreadful crime indeed. And
the Telegraph says magnanimously that it
would never have attempted such a thing.
But, beforehand, when the same mendacious
organ expected to be on the wrong side, it
simply howled to Parliament to stand by and
be ready to squash the Bill if the minimum was
forthcoming. For instance, this was the same
Telegraph, of May 17 :
It will be quite within the province of Parliament by a
meeting of the two Houses or by the two Houses sitting
separately, as was the case before the Convention of 1891,
to appoint delegates to a Parliamentary Convention to
reconsider and revise the Bill. If it should be necessary,
an amendment of the Federal Enabling Act might be
made for the purpose. The Bill, as redrafted, could then
be either submitted to another and final popular referen-
dum or left for the acceptance of Parliament in the form
of adopting the address to the Queen. Mr. Lyne is one of
those who think the proposal an eminently safe one. As
the Bill has been prepared, and as there is so much of it
that can be adopted with scarcely any alteration, it is
unnecessary, he thinks, to have a second elected Con-
vention.
Therefore, on May 17, the Telegraph, when it
expected its minority to be the bottom dog,
was sooling on the N.S.W. Parliament to re-
fuse to recognise the popular majority, and to
disregard the referendum, and to disfranchise
the nation bodily, and throw the people’s Bill
overboard, and make a new one without giving
the people any voice in the matter. And now
it says that it would never have dreamt of
doing the thing it was lately yelling for with
all its might. Also, if the Federal majority
does anything even approaching to what the
Telegraph was lately urging the anti-Federal
minority to do, the proceeding will be very,
very sinful indeed. And it tries to demonstrate
the sacredness of the 80,000 minimum by saying
that it and the Convention were created by
“ the same Parliamentary Act.” As a matter
of fact, the Convention and the 50,000 minimum
were created by the same sacred Act—the
Enabling Act of 1896. The 80,000 minimum
was created by an Amending Act late in 1897.
And the Telegraph didn’t fight against the
80,000 minimum and several other details of the
Enabling Act, for that detail was never in the
Enabling Act. Thus does the parochial Peck-
sniff organ pour oil in the ear of its fatted
minion, Reid, the politician who is on both
sides at once and does no credit to either.
* * # * £
Even this, however, is only a small part o
the anomaly. The Telegraph now rises gladly
to say that the Bill which got a big majority
has been stamped on (by the minority), and the
majority is dead because the minority has
jumped on it, and the majority’s Bill is past
resurrection because the minority says so. And
the minority paper therefore reports that its
party in N.S.W. will combine with the other
party in N.S.W. and all work together to pro-
duce a measure with which both parties in
N.S.W. will be satisfied. Considering that the
Telegraph faction wants a Federation which
will include the debts, railways, posts, tele-
graphs, customs, and excise; and the Reid
faction wants the debts and railways left out;
and the Heydon faction wants the customs,
excise, posts, and telegraphs also left out, and
says, in addition, that it would be unwise to
federate at all; and the Want faction desires a
Federation in which N.S.W. won’t be “out-
voted ” —in other words, apparently, one in
which N.S.W. will be allowed to outvote all
the other provinces ; and the Slattery crowd
and most of the Sydney property-owners are
against any Federation which doesn’t make
Sydney the capital, the idea of this ragged
regiment working together for any pur-
pose is very “beautiful indeed. * Then,
again, the Lynk gang, led by the most
wooden and incapable person who ever
ran a party in any of the eastern provinces,
wants to make sure that the Commonwealth
shall be Protectionist, and Reid wants to make
sure that it shall be Freetrade, and that the
policy of a little over half a province shall be
accepted without demur by 4tj provinces. And
they all kicked at an arrangement by which
every province and every inhabitant was to be
taxed on the same basis, and produced strange
arithmetical contortions to prove that the policy
of taxing everybody on the same basis was a
hideous injustice to N.S.W. Yet no one ever
suggested how anything could be done except
by taxing everybody on the same basis.
* * * *
And even if all this incongruous minority
could be unanimous it would lead to nothing.
The Telegraph’s proposal simply is that the
N.S.W. minority—its minority—shall combine
with the N.S.W. . majority to make a Bill
which will be perfectly satisfactory to N.S.W.
No doubt that could be done; even Tas-
mania could do as much any morning before
breakfast. The Bill which would perfectly
satisfy any province would be one which
gave that province all the power and left the
others to pay all the taxes. If N.S.W. was
going to federate all by itself the scheme would
be admirable ; as it isn’t, the scheme doesn’t
amount to anything. What N.S.W. has got
now is a measure that satisfies the majority of
its own voters and the majority of the voters in
three other provinces. It isn’t good enough to
let that go until something better comes along.
If something better is coming along it had better
come quickly. Therefore, it is hereby suggested
that a conference be immediately held consist-
ing of the directors of the Freetrade Telegraph
and the Protectionist Star, also Want, Reid,
Lyne, Gould, Sydney Smith, Heydon, Nor-
ton, Crick, the Labor-party, Salomons,
Pilcher, Coghlan, Sunday Brunker, Slat-
tery and John Haynes, and that the police be
in attendance to prevent free fights, and an
ambulance to remove the wounded to the
nearest hospital. And it is further proposed
that all these harmonious Kilkenny cats should
come to an agreement as to the kind of Federal
Constitution they really do want, and that the
survivors publish the results, so that the
N.S.W. majority will know what kind of thing
the N.S.W. minority is offering it.
PLAIN ENGLISH
A Tight Place.
The fact that S.A., Victoria and Tasmania
have accepted the Federation Bill by large
majorities, and that it has only been lost in
N.S.W. because the majority has been annulled
by Act of Parliament, leaves tilings in a com-
plicated situation. There is no chance of
any new Bill ; the present one has been
accepted by four majorities, and for four
majorities to abandon their work at the dicta-
tion of one minority is wildly absurd. The
only question is whether, as soon as the
Westralian Parliament meets and settles its part
of the business, the accepting provinces will go'
straight ahead with the formation of the Com-
monwealth and let N.S.W. come in when it has
had time to squash the Ministry of the Eight
GoD-fearing Iscariots, or whether they will
wait. It probably matters very little ; either
way the final result will be the same. The
only reason why the other provinces should
wait is that to do so would be an act of courtesy
to the N.S.W. majority which is out of the
movement through no fault of its own but solely
through the base treachery of its Ministry. The
only reason why they shouldn’t wait is that
the surest way to get the Commonwealth
started is to start it with what materials are
available. So far as the figures are to hand
at time of writing, Victoria has accepted the
Bill by 99,000 votes against 22,000 ; S.A. by
26,000 to 14,000; Tasmania by 11,000 against
less than 3000. Therefore Victoria, one of the
populous provinces, which suffers to almost
exactly the same extent as N.S.W. by the
principle of equal State representation in the
Senate, gave a majority of nearly sto 1; and


S.A., one of the three small provinces which
are supposed to be greatly advantaged, could
only raise a majority of 2 to 1. The fact that
Victoria, whose unfraternal border tariff has
been so much denounced, gave the biggest
majority of the lot for the abolition of border
tariffs, and for throwing her markets open
to all the people of Australia, is also remark-
able ; so is the fact that allegedly Freetrade
N.S.W. gave the smallest majority for Free-
trade throughout Australia. Probably the
biggest mystery of the lot is what will happen
when N.S.W. Parliament meets. Reid will
have to face the Labor crowd after his recent
admission at Bathurst that he doesn’t mean to
make any serious reform of the Council, after
all. Thirty-seven anti-Billious members, in-
cluding the Premier, two other Ministers and
the leader of the Opposition, all have to con-
front the painful circumstance that their con-
stituencies have gone strongly against them.
Also, the whole crowd which voted for the
80,000 minimum will have the awful fact in its
eye that the country is against it. And it is a
very long time since any Parliament has been in
such a tight spot as that.

Want and Misery.
Some of Jack Want’s gorgeous oration the
night when the Federation party in N.S.W.
got 5000 majority : ,
I knew as well as you knew that the people of this
province would not fail to rise to the occasion [they rose
and left Want’s party 5000 in the rear] and that they
would not fail with their intelligence to recognise the
faults in this Convention Bill when it came to the question
of voting. [The 5000 majority for the Bill stood up in
great letters just across the street.] . . . When I started
out on this campaign I had few supporters, but as I went
along I found that they grew and grew in number and
that there was to be a great triumph of Democracy. To-
night shows a triumph which I hardly expected. [The
triumphant gentleman had just been licked by 5000
votes]. ... You have been told by those people who
supported this Bill that you cannot have another Conven-
tion, but I want you to watch these gentlemen ;
I say again, watch them. The very men who are
now telling you that you cannot have another
Convention will be among the first to start out and
boom another Bill. You will find that these men who
have been booming this fraud and sham will be in the
front ranks of those who will be starting out to-morrow
to try and run another monkey-show ; but they will find
themselves in the same position as they do now. (5000
ahead.) I cannot tell you how deeply I thank you from
the bottom of my heart for the position in which you have
placed this Bill (at the top of the poll). I have never done
anything from first to last but fight the battle of the people
of this country. I have had no axe to grind, and I have
had nothing to gain. I have had everything to lose, but
I am proud to know that, sacrificing everything as I have,
I have won the acquiescence of the people of this colony.
[The people of N.S.W. had just decided by 5000 votes that
Jack’s gospel was a sham and a fraud]. The vote to-night
tells me that I have done what is right. [A 5000 majority
had just up-ended Want’s provincial superstition in the
gutter.] . . . The approbation of this mighty audience
is the wildest possible delight which any public man can
store up in his memory or in his heart.
Apropos of which, The Bulletin is still wait-
ing to hear Jack Want’s manly retraction of
the—well—inaccuracies with which he helped
to work up that minority against the Bill of
which he is so proud. For instance, he is re-
ported as having told a Sydney audience, by
way of rousing its parochial frenzy against the
other provinces, that the delegates of these
other provinces “ kicked the Referendum out
of the Bill ” at the Melbourne Convention, when
there never was any Referendum in it; and
that Victoria, by means of duties, permitted
“ hardly an ounce” of N.S.W. coal to get into
that province, whereas there is no duty and
Victoria is N.S.W.’s biggest coal customer.
And, when the 80,000 minimum was proposed
in the Leg. Council, Want, M.L.C., assured
the House, as an evidence of the absurdity of
the original 50,000 minimum, that there were
nearly 350,000 voters on the N.S.W. roll —
which was some 80,000 to 100,000 more than
the truth. As it is a low and degrading and
crawlsome thing to bolster up any cause by
gross misrepresentation, The Bulletin still
waits to hear Want deny the accuracy of the
newspapers and the Hansard which ascribed
these statements to him, or else acknowledge,
like an honest though reckless bigot, that he
didn’t know what he was talking about.
Trot Out the New Bill.
Now that the Convention Bill has not got the
required minimum in N.S.W., and the boss
howlers of the provincial minority are boasting
that they are the apostles of genuine Federa-
tion, and that they have rescued the province
from the clutches of a purely bogus article, it
is about time these same gentlemen explained
the kind of Federation they do want. So far
they have only talked about the kind they
don’t want and about their desire for Federa-
tion in general. Therefore, The Bulletin
proposes that the Review of Reviews, as a
kind of peaceful bottle-holder between parties,
should send copies of the Bill to Reid, Want,
Lyne, Heydon, Brunker, Gould, Cook, the
smaller Piddington, M‘Gowen, John Norton,
and the editor of Sydney Telegraph, and ask
each to strike out the clauses he objects to
and fill in the new clauses required to produce
what he regards as a fair and workable scheme
—one which he will cordially support. The
trouble with all these loud smashers is that
they have constructed nothing, while they
threw bricks persistently at the work of other
people. They all say they want a Bill, also
that they don’t want this Bill. Will they now'
produce in concrete form the Bill they do want ?
They have all promised that if this Bill was
rejected a far better one would be forthcoming.
They never produced the better Bill which they
had in their mental vision so that the public
nrght judge, before it threw away this one,
how much better the next one was likely to be,
but they said there would be another one, and
that it would be better. If there is any truth
in the promises of Want, Lyne, Reid, Heydon
and co., and if they are not mere destruction-
ists like the other famous lunatic who burnt the
temple of Ephesus, they must have some kind
of improved Bill in view. And the country
wants to see it. Until they produce it, it is
impossible to tell whether there is any hope of
a compromise between the constructive party,
led by Barton, and the party, hitherto only
known as an Anarchistic crowd of breakers and
smashers, captained by Reid and Want and
Heydon, and any chance of agreement between
them and the Federationists of the other pro-
vinces. So far as The Bulletin knows, it is
the only paper, or the only person, who pub-
lished in detail its idea of what the Federal
Constitution ought to be (see article headed
“ The Bulletin’s Federal Constitution,” pub-
lished 23rd January, 1897). It is now waiting
to hear from some of the other people.
When Lawyers Pall Out.
The Victorian Law Commission now sitting
will probably do more in the way of legal reform
than has been before accomplished during the
history of the province. At the outset it
seemed likely that the Commission would fail
to penetrate the mysteries of legal practice as
the Supreme Court itself has failed up to now.
But, fortunately, the old jealousy between the
two varieties of shark gave rise to some nasty
remarks by one barrister re “amalgams.” This
resulted in retaliation by the next “ amalgam”-
witness, and since then the two branches have
been cordially exposing each other for all
they’re worth. Every witness finished with
at the early stages of the sitting is coming
forward again to ask: “If the last witness
complains of my charging a client £2O for
writing a letter, what should be said of his
charging £3O for serving a writ ? ” It is simply
the old detective game of going to one member
of a gang and “ drawing ” him by telling him
that his pals have “ split,” only in this case the
sharks have “ given away ” each other. Sample
of evidence given by an amalgam, who is
referring to one of his bills of costs, exhibited
by another solicitor during his evidence :
The Chairman (Mr. Higgins, (M.L.A.). —In that case
your charge for “ instructions for brief ” amounted to
£289 165., and £164 16s. was taxed off?—Yes. The pro-
portion taxed off was greatly less than the average, which
is about two-thirds.
You charge £l4 Is. for drawing up brief? —That is so.
We are perfectly entitled to that.
And £8 4s. 7d. for engrossing the brief?—Yes; we all
do that.
And you marked your fee on brief at 50 guineas?—Yes.
Do you know how much was paid to the opposing coun-
sel ? —Not of my own knowledge.
Mr. says the fee was only 17 guineas. Can you
explain that? —Yes. I recognised the gravity of the case,
and they did not.
Your bill of costs amounted altogether to £585, and of
that £233 was taxed off? —Yes ; but, as regards Mr. ,
I would like to say that four days before he gave his
evidence one of his own bills of costs was taxed in a case
where he acted for one of the defendants, and as solicitor
only. His bill was £450 11s. 2d., and of that £203 15s.
was taxed off. “Instructions for brief” was charged
£2OO, and £125 was taxed off—a considerably greater pro-
portion than in my case.
Can you point to any item in your bill of costs where
your client saved anything through your acting as bar-
rister as well as solicitor ? —I have been told by a taxing-
officer that the amount allowed to me for “ instructions
for brief” is less than it would be if I instructed other
counsel. I cannot remember who the taxing-officer was.
Can you mention any specific items? I was allowed
nothing for attendance on myself with brief, or for attend-
ing with refresher, or for solicitor’s attendance in court.
The fact that this man charged nothing for call-
ing on himself with his brief, or for paying him-
self the money he was owed, or for attending
the court as solicitor to give any passing hints
to himself as barrister, is indeed touching tes-
timony to the hardness of a lawyer’s lot and
cheek !

Bull’s Brother Jonathan.
Few things make The Bulletin smile a wider
smile than the tale about the “blood-brother-
hood ” between Britain and the United States,
and the proposed union of Bull and Jonathan
in a great Anglo-Saxon Federation. When the
last census was taken, in 1890, the population
of the United States consisted of 45| million
white Americans, 9£ million white or partially
white foreigners,and 7| million negroes. The
million negroes were plain, ordinary, black nig-
gers with no Anglo-Saxon yearnings or relation-
ships, though a lot of them may have been named
Johnson. Of the million foreigners, rather
less than 1£ millions were British ; 1,871,000
Irish (the Irish are not generally supposed to
be yearning for any alliance with Bull) ;
2,784,000 were Germans, mostly named Hans ;
and 31j millions were miscellaneous. As regards
the Americans, what is now the United States
began with a British element in New England
and in some of the southern districts; a
strong Dutch element in New York and
its surroundings ; a French settlement in
Louisiana, and Spanish ones in Florida,
New Mexico, Texas, Arizona, and California.
But the mass of the Americans are the children
and grand-children and great-grandchildren of
the immigrants who swarmed into the United
States after 1820, the year when immigration
seems to have fairly commenced. There were
15,334,000 of these arrivals between 1821 and
1889. The official records show that 2,281,000
of them were British ; 4,140,000 were Irish
(the Irish are not exactly Anglo-Saxon) ;
4,475,000 were German ; and over 4,000,000
were of other nationalities. From 1890 to 1897
the U.S. received 3,295,000 more immigrants, of
whom English, Scotch, Welsh, and Irish put
together were only 740,000. Therefore, the
beginning of the United States was largely non-
British. The contributed material of the great
immigration period, which has since been
assimilated and is now called American, was
less than one-sixth British, and much less than
half British and Irish combined. And
of the undigested foreign population —
the part which is still called “ foreign ”
—the British element is less than one-
sixth, and the British and Irish com-
bined are only about one-third. Moreover,
according to U.S. census returns there are on
the average 144 children to every 100 German
adults, and only 122 children to every 100
British adults, therefore the German element
is not only stronger than the purely British
one now, but keeps getting further ahead. For
these reasons this paper smiles a good deal at
the shriek about the “ blood-brotherhood ”
between Bull and his alleged brother Jona-
than. Pretty nearly the only thing about the
States which is British is the language, and the
Americans speak a good deal of English merely
because it was the official language of the
old ante-WASHiNGTON days—otherwise German,
spoken with a strong Irish accent, might have
crowded it out. If this motley nation allies
itself with Britain, there will be a great deal
more of policy than brotherhood in the business.

The So-called Vic. Charities.
Years ago—somewhere back in the last cen-
tury —a Charities Commission was appointed
in Melbourne. It had for secretary the dead
“ Vagabond,” and made sundry well-con-
sidered recommendations which were duly
hung up and left to solidify ; and the whole
matter was shelved in the characteristic way of
Australian people and Australian Governments.
But the objectionable practice of poor people
being ill and sometimes even being so incon-
siderate as to die in the streets, has become so
frequent that at last the police authorities are
stirring the municipal ditto to “ take steps.”
The incident of a sick person being rushed around
to sundry hospitals and finally driven to the gaol
for medical attention (possibly dying in the cab
en route) has become so common as to pass
without notice. Out-patients at hospitals are
treated as no man who had any regard for
their life and health would dream of treating
his cattle. Yet the State, which admits its
liability and enforces its claim upon the life
of the citizen by prosecuting him if he
fail when attempting suicide, shirks the
question by shoving it on to the shoulders
of that chill and barren thing—private charily
—the charity of Melb. Hospital, or the Bene-
volent Asylum, or the Salvation Army Home.
The other week, writer saw five small children
brought to Melb. Hospital, suffering from
diphtheria ; two were admitted as in-patients,
the other three were compelled, with the
mother, to wait for over two hours in a chilly
room, among a crowd of other sufferers, to get
their medicine. A day or two afterwards, a
woman came to the police-station, saying her
daughter was dying of diphtheria, in Carlton.
The police tried in vain to get her into a hos-
pital ; some refused that class of disease,
others had no room; and finally the girl
was taken, in a state of collapse, to the gaol !
In Victoria, for want of hospital accommoda-
tion, the gaol is the destination of a large pro-
portion of the poor, the sick, the aged, the
imbecile, the miscellaneous vagrants —in fact,
all the derelicts slop over into it when the
scanty infirmary accommodation gives out.
Yet it is just as cheap to treat patients in a
properly-managed hospital as in a gaol. There
is no saving of cash in the present system ; it
is even questionable if the province doesn’t pay
extra for the disgrace. Premier Turner now
promises to increase the charitable vote, but
that is not enough. The whole system needs
re-organisation.
In its issue of 26th February The Bulletin
published an eloquent article in favor of Feder-
ation. It was entitled “ A Word for Aus-
tralia,” and the writer spoke pityingly of
“ these good Englishmen and Irishmen and
Scotchmen who are arranging our Federal
destiny.” He was an Australian himself, and
he was sorry for these imported people because
they couldn’t understand the yearning of the
real Australian after a nation of his own. He
pointed out that it vvas the Australian who had
to do the work, and supply the fire and enthu-
siasm, and put a big bony shoulder to the wheel
and shove the big car of progress along. And,
to The Bulletin of June 4th, the same
patriot supplied another glowing article headed
“ For Australia ” with this peroration :
The appeal of the present is to Australians, not to
aliens. The appeal is to Australians, not to the men of a
province. The appeal is to Australians, not to calculat-
ing self-seekers. And it is to Australians who are worthy
of their opportunity and of their country that the chal-
lenge conies to stand up for the promise of a nation, for
the indissolubility of a race —for Australia.
The Bulletin only mentions these things now
because the burning patriot in question is an
Australian native and an enthusiastic believer
in the Bill which didn’t get on so well as it
should have done last Friday—and he was too
darned tired even to take out an electoral right
and vote. Which is an indication of the
reasons why the national cause didn’t get on
quite so well as it should have done last Fri-
day. The present writer, being a Scot with a
streak of the Frenchman in him, did manage
to vote. But then he comes of a race which
has rather less poetry and not nearly so much
tiredness.

“ Once accuse a man of a crime and people will
be ready to believe him guilty. Place the most
honest and pleasing face behind the rails of a
dock and it at once becomes a face that ex-
presses every passion,” said the late W. B.
Dalley, Q.C., at the trial of Sussex-street
murderer Scott. A few r days ago a man was
charged with having raised a cry of “Fire ! ”
at Melb. Opera House to avoid getting arrested
for the theft of somebody’s purse. The first
time he came to court he wore a dull, vacant
expression of countenance, which almost con-
vinced the indignant onlookers that he must
be “ shamming.” Under the circumstances he
was regarded as an artful monster of the
deepest dye. After a week’s remand he
toed the mark again with an excellent char-
acter from his employer, w’ho explained that
the accused was an absent-minded and timid
young man, very apt to get hali-scared out of
his wits when sharply spoken to. Whereupon
the magistrate and the newspaper reporters
and all the other critics in court perceived his
innocence sticking out a yard. The hypo-
critical thief and false fire-alarm-raiser van-
ished like a passing cloud of suspicion, and an
estimable, unlucky, industrious mechanic stood
in the phantom’s boots. Which suggests that,
notwithstanding the Law’s endeavor to believe
every prisoner innocent until he is found guilty,
the average juryman naturally jumps to the
very opposite conclusion.

Charles Edward writes from Brisbane re Old
Age Pensions.—
Dear Bulletin.— Re an article which appeared some
time ago in your journal on old age pensions, in which
the writer inferred that no scheme could be just unless
every one received a pension, irrespective of his or her
position. This at first sight appeared lo me to be a self-
evident proposition, but on reflection I arrived at a
different conclusion. Let us consider the subject as a
problem in life-assurance. A pension is proposed to be
granted, contingent on certain conditions to which all
are liable. In this case the condition is poverty, which
we will suppose to mean not having an income arising
from any source (except wages) exceeding 10s. per week!
But, to encourage thrift, should a citizen’s income exceed
this limit by Is., 25., or 3s. and so on per week, the
pension might be reduced to 95., Bs., or 7s. and so on to
correspond. Of course this is contingent on his reaching
the age specified in the Act. No man can possibly foresee
what his income may be on reaching 65 years of age 1
therefore, no one can reasonably object to contribute to
the necessary taxation. But as the majority of contri-
butors Will die before reaching the age at which the
pension becomes payable, it is a duty owing to them to
make the burthen as light as possible, and this is done
under the present scheme. In short, my theory is that
it is not necessary to give every aged inhabitant a pen-
sion in order to relieve the recipients from the stigma of
pauperism. It is simply a question of life-assurance,
every inhabitant being compulsorily assured against two
contingencies—that he will live beyond a certain age, and
that he will be in a certain financial condition at that
age. The man who has to collect his insurance money in
his old age will be no more a pauper than the man who
collects his fire-insurance when the contingency of a fire
comes his way.

“Nil ” (a medico) talks right out :
Dear Bulletin.—Your par. re Sydney D.T. on “ age-of-
consent ” mentions “ Surgeon’s ” “ view of the tender age
at which a great number of girls become tainted with
‘ immorality.’ ” Here you touch upon a fact which under-
lies a pestilential morass of side-issue, half-truth, cant,
prurience, and many kindred social weeds. Into the
morass Ido not wish to plunge. The fact is, in spite of
statements to the contrary, that creole children become
sexually mature at least two years (average) earlier than
those born in the colder old country. Then come some
subsidiary factlets : (1) Boys and girls are allowed to
sleep together to a later age amongst the half-educated
of Australia (especially in the country) than in similar
grades “at home.” (2) They foregather with “grown-
ups” to a greater extent at the same time. (3) At an age
when a spurious precocity reacts on early subsexual
awakenings they are permitted to see and hear connubial
interchangeson.the parents' part which most savages would
draw a veil upon. (4) They are sent to school (“ State ”
and especially “ Free ”) in many cases merely to “ keep
them out of the way.” (5) It is the invariable custom (in my
province) to have the boys’ and girls’ latrines either semi-
detached or but a few paces apart. Almost all teachers
of “mixed” government schools know the results of this
train of circumstances. A few will admit as much confi-
dentially to—well, perhaps the local doctor. One may
(once in a blue moon) inform the parents. Of course it is
then a case of “ Not my Tommy ! ” That teacher is very
apt to find it convenient to ask fora “shift.” The local
doctor may (and nearly always does) know all about it, but
“privileged statement” and “professional ethics,” and,
particularly, bread and butter, bar the way to exposure.
The writer knows a case of a female (child ?) of eleven who
for the last three years has been leading a “ gay ” life
with boys of about her own age. She still attends the
public school. He has also seen letters, intercepted by a
teacier, from mere children (in years) making
assignations for this purpose with others “after
school.” . . . These serve merely as instances
in no wise singular enough to be startling —to
the writer. And then we have to listen patiently to all
this perverted eroticism from the Gimlet person about
age-of-consent! Age of I want to know what, in
the circumstances, may be called the age of refusal ? The
whole subject is so hideously nasty that thinking people
flinch from dragging it into daylight and dealing with it.
The average parent being merely an unthinking breeding-
machine with that tired feeling and a confidence born of
stupendous conceit refuses to recognise its existence.
The wonder is not that the “ certain cure, with or with-
out medicines” is so profitable a trade, but that there
appears, at least, to be a fair proportion of the population
attaining adult age who have anything left worth “ con-
senting ” to.
■ ♦
In the projected fever hospital, Melbournians
are adding, at a cost of ,£17,000, one more huge
white-elephant to their already over-elephanted
city. The desire for multiplication of charit-
able institutions badly gives away the “charity”
of many of the supporters thereof, because it
shows plainly that humanity is of small account
beside advt. -Thus, while the subscriber of
£lOO to a brand-new institution has his name
extensively paragraphed, his speeches listened
to at preliminary meetings, and his beneficence
even engraved on the foundation-stone—the
giver of a like amount to a going concern has
nothing but his own feelings and the secre-
tary’s receipt to reward him. This fever-hospi-
tal project is the outcome of the turning away
of dozens of contagious-disease cases from the
existing hospitals, the only one of which—
the Melbourne Hospital—which will admit
contagious cases being in a condition
of chronic congestion. An unthinking press
hereupon took up the cry for a special fever-
hospital ; a few well-meaning people and a host
of advertisers set about getting a scheme into
shape, and a soft-headed Government was
“ kidded ” into granting land for a site. The
result is that the building will probably be
commenced in six months or so, and the utter
idiocy of the whole thing will not begin to
dawn on anybody until the roof is being put on.
With only £17,000 at its disposal, Melbourne
—an ill-drained, red-hot city with 450,000
inhabitants—proposes to build a fever-hospital,
while Edinburgh, with a decent sewerage sys-
tem, a cold climate, and only 240,000 inhabi-
tants, has just expended £llO,OOO over a similar
project, its ambulance arrangements alone hav-
ing absorbed £16,000, or nearly as much as Mel-
bourne proposes to spend altogether. On the
other hand, were the money in hand applied to
the maintenance of the existing hospitals, the
whole of the contagious disease of Melbourne
could be adequately dealt with. The Alfred
Hospital has 80 beds in an unused ward—un-
used because there is no money available to
work it; and the Melbourne Hospital, as has
been said, has its accommodation completely
occupied. The “way out” lies with that
£17,000. Were the £17,000 applied to the
endowment of that extra Alfred Hospital ward,
the Melbourne Hospital might be continuously
eased of 80 cases, and the whole of the city’s
contagious disease concentrated in one section
of the metropolitan institution, where it could
be safely and effectually treated. No new
building, no additional nursing-staff, no com-
missions—(everybody connected with the new
project has the cheek to demand his “cut.”)
, Nothing but provision for the suffering poor in
a manner so economical and unostentatious
that, as it wouldn’t make cheap advertisement
for anybody, nobody takes the trouble to con-
sider it.
An Australian Premier possesses the main, and
often the sole, influence in determining the pro-
fessional fate of the lawyers about him. He
can give the barrister odd jobs, such as draft-
ing bills, annotating opinions and appearing in
occasional cases; he can appoint him Crown
Prosecutor, and make him a Q.C. ; he can give
him a seat, temporary or permanent, on the
District Court Bench ; he can grant him holi-
days on half or full pay, can agree to his
request for retirement on full pension, and can
obtain for him any of a number of Imperial
titles and distinctions. These things being so‘
is it seemly or congruous to have a Premier
appearing as an advocate in ordinary court
cases in the guise of a barrister who is on an
equal footing with the barristers about him ?
Should he not, while in office, refrain from
ordinary competitive work ?
