Seen through a rich haze of scenic magni-
ficence, there appears to be some kind of a story in
Bland Holt’s latest effort, “Straight Brom the
Heart,” but it is a tale that has burst in various
places through being crammed too full of incident.
It contains just about everything barring a giraffe.
The incidents are past enumeration, but they in-
clude the murder of a red-headed man in act 1, a
robbery, a blazing ship, an execution which is
interrupted by anarchists exploding bombs under
the guillotine, and a bad egg in the desert. There
are three eggs as the sole provender of four people
lost in the blazing Sahara, and as one of them is
very bad indeed Bland Holt reserves it for Jack-
son’s breakfast, Jackson being the villain of the
party and any kind of egg being good enough for
him. Also a small boy who had already been
falsely accused of robbery, and partially fried in
the burning ship, is strangled in the same desert
after being 26 days or thereabouts without food;
and Norman is shot there by Baker, only he turns
up in the next act as good as new to assist at Baker’s
execution, and seduce his wife, and if the lady's
long-drowned father, who was last seen sinking in
the Atlantic many miles from land, didn't step in
just then and pound Norman something awful
with his walking-stick, it is impossible to say what
might not have happened. The drama doesn’t
say on what evidence Baker was going to be
executed ; but the murder of the red-headed man
is laid at his door, and there is a very realistic
guillotine with a large knife that comes down im-
pressively, and but for the fact that the anarchists’
bomb knocks the machine sideways Baker would
be dead before Bland Holt has time to arrive on
horseback with the reprieve. Just a few minutes
before this Norman, whom his fellow anarchists
have discovered to be a traitor, has been beguiled
away by two other anarchists to have a drink,
and The Bulletin presumes that they murder
him with the bottle.
* * * *
As this paper has remarked on several
previnous occasions, the magnificence of Biand
Holt’s scenery mostly covers up the defects of his
dramas, and in this case the drama is wilder
than usual and the scenery even stronger. The
palace vvhere the coarse, red-headed man wallows
in his ill-gotten wealth and his lost “h” is a
dream of magnificence, and the desert and the
burning ship and the execution are all big efforts
of stage art. Cosgrove gets out of his usual groove
this time. As his own self in a top-hat and a long
coat Cosgrove is wearisome, but as the red-headed
millionaire he is a very gratifying and interesting
person to meet, and he makes old Pry the best
feature in the play. Bland Holt temporarily falls
away into the character of a Spanish duke and he
is also refreshing. The rest of the excellent co. is
of varying degrees of excellence as usual. One
feature of the drama which calls for loud cheers is
that there is no long fupny scene between Mr. and
Mrs. Bland —in fact there is no scene and no part
which are specially labelled as being funny. The
red headed man is cheerfully and unobtrusively
grotesque till he dies. Also Bland and his
estimable wife don’t stop to be funny ; they are
simply funny as they go along, which is far better.
#* * *
The present Sydney Lyceum drama is a
very large affair, and Diver pursues bis blood-
stained road to ruin till a late hour. Said drama
bears the harrowing title of “ The Coroner and
the Coiner’s Gang,’’ and the wickedness of it
passes description. Compared to the boss Coiner,
even Nero was a tame emperor who would feed
out of the hand. The Coroner begin.? gentlv by
casting off his wife and slaughtering Old "Dad
Goodman for the sake of his money. Then he
sets up as a respectable country squire, and gives
his wife poison* and wants to throw her in the
river also* only there are too many people about,
so he locks her up instead. In between times he
hires old Mike to murder George, and he also
charges George with murder and robbery, and
gets him locked up for 12 months, and he sools
Mike on to rob old Corbin, and hires Mike’s son
to steal Corbin’s little boy. By way of casual re-
freshment he makes bad coin, and engages Mike
and his son to defraud simple-minded German
emigrants with it, and while he is waiting outside
a public-house so that he can betray Mike and his
son to the police he shakes his abandoned wife
and threatens to knock her head against the quay.
Likewise he brutally assaults George who is now
out of prison and starving; and he hires Jake to
personate a parson and perform a bogus marriage
with Nora. There is hardly anything in the way
of sin that he doesn’t either do himself, or incite
Mike to do, or persuade Charles to lay Mike on to,
or perpetrate with the assistance of Jake ; and if it
wasn’t that the policeman to whom he is always
betraying his associates is really a noble but dis-
guised character named Dick his career would
probably end with the slaughter of everybody.
Dick is übiquitous, and turns up everywhere.
When Mike and his son break silently into Cor-
bin’s house, and burst open the chest where his
money is concealed, Dick, disguised as a very deaf
patriarch, rises out of the chest. Then the two
silent burglars naturally yell in his ear for ten
minutes so as to avoid observation and not waken
anybody.
* • • •
TheMacMahonfirm is nowputting on anew
drama every week, and “The Field of Ice” is due
next Saturday. Till then “ The Coroner and the
Coiner’s Gang ” should do first-rate business—at
least it deserves to. When Tins Bulletin's
staggered representative resolved to give up won-
dering about the motive of anything and take the
show as it came he enjoyed it amazingly. It is
well-staged and is choke-full of situations and sur-
prises, and it never flags for an instant; before the
first suspicion of dulness can descend on it Mike
always breaks out into a new crime and gets £5O
from the “Coroner "for doing it. Among thepeople
who hustle the gay show along, Harry Dher calls
for kindly mention as the boss fiend ; Shine, as
the comedy ruffian in the white bell-topper ; and
Charles Field, as Mike, who is a truculent monster
in depressed spirits. Laurence Dunbar is five
different people, and shows a remarkable capacity
for disguise, and bright Inez Bensusan hovers
generally on the outskirts of the drama. One of
her appearances is in the capacity of a suddenly-
awakened girl in a long and elegantly-frilled white
garment, and in this capacity she takes well and
is universally popular.
** # *
The worm of change has begun to creep
into the co. which keeps up the “Sign of the
Cross ” at Her Majesty’s, Sydney. The same
Marcus still giv r es his noble limbs to the lion for
the sake of the same pale Christian girl, and
Berenis, Tigellinus, Poppcea, Dacia, and the
noble old Roman drunk are unchanged. But
Caleb Porter has quitted the co. and a new Csesar
has fits in ancient Rome in his stead. Mr. H. .T.
Carvill makes a respectable Caesar, but it is diffi-
cult to replace the jewelled party who lately filled
the imperial bill, and the show suffers a little in
consequence. On the other hand, Miss Annie
Cubitt, as the smallest Christian with the least
quantity of clothing on, is an improvement on her
predecessor, so, perhaps, things are about even.
Cyril Keightley now fills the massive sandals of
Metellus with very good results, and the “Sign”
still prospers and sheds a mellow radiance over
the days when the Early Fathers were done brown
for the sake of the Faith.
** * *
The Tivoli is now Rickards’ only show in
Sydney, the Cri. having closed up and the deserv-
ing Cartwright co. moved north. Miss Peggy
Pryde still grows in favor, and that bright and
clever little she-humorist seems good for a lengthy
run. Also John Coleman has produced a number
of perfectly new jokes, and the other people, who
are many, remain in good health and condition.
*# * *
Old “Fun on the Bristol ’’ has risen from
the oblivion of years, and is doing a short season
at Sydney Royal Standard, with Miss Billie
Howard as Dora. This ancient joke has grown
and altered so much in the course of time, and so
many new jokes have grown on to it and old ones
dropped off, that it is hardly recognisable as the
Widow of a dozen years ago. Still, it remains a
very respectable joke to the very last, and Sydney
should be able to stand a week of it, anyhow.
** * *
“The Little Minister,” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, is a quaint, pretty little play that doubtless
improves on acquaintance, but will probably seem
rather thin and fictitious to the average non-
Scottish person, ignorant of J. M. Barrie’s novel.
And the average person will be in a majority after
the first night. The meenister runs an Auld
Licht Kirk in the one-horse town o’ Thrums
where an alleged insurrection is expected when
the curtain rises. A vague, mysterious insurrec-
tion. The meenister, having aroused it by blow-
ing a horn to oblige a strange girl, rushes down
the hillside, and puts it out, and returns to his
bare-legged charmer. She is an “ Egyptian
wumtnan ” (otherwise gipsy), as far as the Auld
Lichter knows, swooping round the district in
search of chickens and things, and she simply
paralyses him at her first fell swoop. A casual
bcotch youth meanwhile has recognised her as
Lady Babbie for the information of the audience,
but the youth goes away and doesn’t tell anybody
else.
* * • •
Miss Pattie Browne, joyfully received by
her old admirers, makes a charming Egyptian
wumman—an incomprehensible attraction shining
in the middle of a misty narrative. She meets the
meenister again in the cottage of an aged Nanny,
and the amorous evangelist loses his head more
than ever. He takes to writing godless verses,
ending with “bo, her boy am I,” when he ought
to be preparing Auld Licht sermons. His thoughts
are quite diverted from religion. The elders of
the kirk bewail the meenister’s bewitchment, also a
converted drunk, Rob Dow, relapses into liquor
and tries to suppress the extraordinary damsel,
consequently the meenister ignores a prayer-meet-
ing in order to rescue his unaccountable girl, and
follows her to the castle of the noble earl, her
father, and proposes to her in the hall, under the
impression that she is a trespasser, escaping from
justice, without a pair of boots or stockings to her
name. The original horn-blowing episode supplies
the play with a semblance of plot at this crisis.
Miss Pattie’s noble father comes to thank the
meenister for having put the insurrection out,
whereupon the lady runs into the next room,
and presently reappears with her title and
declares her love. But a painfully upright
captain, in an uncomfortable uniform, wants to
arrest the meenister for blowing the aforesaid
horn, likewise he holds a warrant for the arrest of
the Egyptian woman, who is now Lady Babbie
1 urthermore, the plot is complicated by the dis-
covery that Lady B. married herself by Scotch
law to her adorer in the first act, when she made
him call her his wife before witnesses. In the
ordinary course of things this young- Scotch couple
must have known all about the Scotch marriage-
law from the start, but the play allows them not
to know it until they are informed by a third
party. The meenister returns to his forgotten
prayer meeting, and tlie Auld Lieht congregation
arises in a body from behind a garden wall to see
him hug his bride, and the noble earl gives his
blessing, whilst the uncomfortable captain, who
had come to arrest the criminal, goes away to hide
his diminished hat. A queer plot. The interest
is centred in two unconvincing characters, and a
number of small convincing circumstances help to
give the play a charm of its own.
* * * *
Miss Pattie Browne, as the Egyptian fly-
by-night, is the main cause for whatever success>
great or medium-sized, the show may be achiev"
ing. Her part suits her, and she performs it in a
little better than her best saucy manner of the old
B. and B. days. Cecil Ward puts all the sincerity
possible into the name part, and sundry natives of
Thrums—-unimportant characters in their drama-
tised capacities—are well done by J. W. Sweeney,
Oily Deering, Miss Athena Claudius, Fred Cam-
bourne, and F. Macviears, a new-comer. Another
nevv-comer, Miss Beatrice Day, scores as a French
maid, and a particularly natural bit of comedy is
supplied by Miss Eloise Juno, who puts herself
into the place of Nanny, and defies one to tell
t’other from which.
** * *
Malevolent Johanna, at Melb. Royal, has
removed a portion of the original chalk from her
countenance, and “ Shall We Forgive Her?” is
progressing favorably. The Bulletin took kindly
to the Johanna’s nightmare aspect, but perhaps
it was wise to wash away some of her ghastliness,
thereby giving the domestic side of the drama a
chance to be taken seriously. The penitent
heroine (Miss Gresham) attracts attention to her
bursts of feeling now that Johanna seems less
inhuman, and the oftener Miss G. is regarded, the
more she seems likely to shine as an emotional
actress if she can manage to get her head
and shoulders under stronger control. Perhaps
parson Titheradge, who is her friend and adviser
in this tearful story, will tell the heroine how
he cultivated repose long ago by standing motion-
less against a wall, or a bed-post, whilst he hurled
fiery harangues into space. If he found any part
of himself throwing off the restraint, he stopped
his elocution and made a fresh start, and the re-
sult is seen in the quietly-impressive Titheradge,
who leavens a large lump of drama and makes it
fairly digestible after the prologue has passed
away. Nothing could mitigate the awfulness of
the prologue, which happens in Queensland at a
time when the local water-supply is reduced to
half a pint. Hans Phillips staggers upon the
scene in the last stage of drought, and the heroine
gives him the half-pint, also she takes him into
her hut, and places her bed at his disposal.
Then she wakes him up with the news that a
mob of desperate men are about to murder him for
his gold, and bids him fly ere it is too late. So
he flies, but returns immediately and stands lis-
tening round the corner whilst the desperate
diggers hear the lady confess that she isn't law-
fully married to their ringleader, and has other-
wise sinned in advising the droughty capitalist to
save his money and his life. Then the infuriated
mob, having come for the purpose of murdering
somebody, start to slay the unmarried female, but
the capitalist whom they meant to annihilate in
the first instance, and who had begun to bolt from
his doom ere he changed his mind, stands forth
and subdues them with a rifle. The prologue,
however, only serves to provide the sinner with a
Past to pave her road to Hingland and Johanna.
#* # *
“Dr. Bill ’’is struggling through a third
week at the Bijou, and if there is a Lady Doctors’
Association in Melbourne it might as well give
patronage to this riotous moral lesson. “Dr Bill”
is a joke that tends to set Adolphus ruminating
every time his best girl consults a masculine
specialist in “nerves,” and leaves her future hus-
band cooling his heels on the doorstep whilst the
other fellow sounds her in sundry places with an
artful little trumpet. On Saturday (11th) Miss
Myra Kemble will set up “ The Alhambra Girl.”
** * *
At the Alexandra, this week, a more
coherent melodrama than the previous effort is
calculated to improve business and urge the Wil-
fred Hayes co. to further acts of violence. On
Saturday, the co. will begin “Breaking the
News,” and Miss Hattie Hayes is to assist the
proceedings.
* * at *
Also, on Saturday the seventh or Penulti-
mate “ Pop ” will transpire at the Town Hall,
and an announcement that Phil Newbury and
Madame Spada intend to sail for London shortly
is printed on the penultimate bill. Meanwhile
Harry Cogill is preparing to re-open the Gaiety
with a “combination of novelty stars ” and the
strictest regard for Refinement. We are whales
for Refinement in our present state of advanced
civilisation.
** * *
The Tiller Quartette, who have been much
missed in Melbourne since Rickards’ pantomime
went away, returned to the Opera House on
Saturday, and shook their wild, spontaneous
understandings at an appreciative crowd. The
Poluski Bros, are still getting routed seven times
a week by one of their gallant boys in red. and the
Kellino Family are even more remarkable acro-
bats than ever, but neither of these strong turns
is anyway wicked. The city was crying out for a
little refined wickedness, when the Tiller Quar-
tette came along with the desired pinch of salt-
god blessem !
** * #
Florence Schmidt has fairly captured her
countrymen in Queensland, and, what is a far
more difficult feat, her countrywomen. Rock-
hampton fairly excelled itself in its reception of
the cantatrice, and no doubt the fact that Premier
Byrnes brought her up-in the Govt, yacht Lucinda
and gushed over her will mean a majority for the
Government next time. Luckily, the gifted
Florence has a very level head, and will hardly be
spoiled by success. She still has the musical world
to face, and though The Bulletin puts its money
on her, it hopes she will not stop up too late o’
nights, or dance too many dances, or shower her
pearly notes around, without adequate reward, in
the salons of Rockhampton millionaires.
** * *
Dear Bulletin —Your remark, “ The
Firm is now the only institution in Sydney which
retains the prescriptive right to charge ss. for ad-
mission,’’may be applied, with equal truth, to Mel-
bourne. Bland Holt, by supplying handsome and
well-acted spectacular plays at “ popular prices,”
has practically prohibited all managers, save \V.
and M., from making money at the old rates. B.
and B. oouldn’t flourish at Molb. Bijou of late
years, and were forced to appear at the Princess’s
by arrangement with the Firm. They, like
Rickards with his Cartwright Comedy Co., would
doubtless have done better had they knuckled-
down to the public demand for a lower tariff,
nevertheless the 5s. maximum was no more than a
reasonable charge in each case. And the Firm is
not really a gainer by this failure of rival manage-
ments to maintain a competition on even lines.
A show at the Princess’s must be large
and glittering, or have a particular “ boom ”
element, otherwise it doesn’t go to any great ex-
tent. “ Prisoner of Zenda,” for instance, couldn’t
have been a very profitable venture, but when
Julius Knight and co. were put to “ The Sign of
the Cross ” the exceptional notoriety of the play
gave them a “boom” which accounted for the
subsequent success of “A Royal Divorce.”
Notoriety scored again for a season with Wilson
Barrett. Yet it is undeniable that the Wilson
Barrett and co. of to-day would have been a more
durable attraction ten years ago, when popular
dramas ran for eight or ten weeks at the Royal on
terms which the modern public would consider too
hierh. The Firm doesn’t gain anything from being
left in a position of “ splendid isolation.” As the
competition among popular-price managers in-
creases, the public will demand more and more
value for their money at the more expensive
theatre. And the public’s idea of extra value is,
unfortunately, not High Art. — Old Melboukne.

The Review of Reviews for May repro-
duces a batch of Bulletin pictures, and credits
most of them to other papers. One is acknow-
ledged to the Queenslander and two to Melbourne
Punch.
The Commonwealth Bill has been dis-
honored in N.S.W., bya timid, selfish, provineially-
minded minority. It will be presented again in
due course, and met at maturity.
As the late returns crawl slowly in, the
N.S.W. majority in favor of the Bill grows larger.
At time of writing, the majority is 5430, and it
will probably get close up to 6000 before all is
finished.
For the billet of inspector of evil smells at
Kanowna (Westralia), the place where all the gold
is being got at present, there were 116 applicants.
And, strange to say, the job went, not to a Yarra-
side man, but to one from Sydney.
Bananaland is great in the field of scientific
experiment. Recently, that province has been
dosing the rabbit with chicken cholera, but with
no more serious result than an alleged slight in-

It is authoritatively announced that none
of ex-Premier Nelson’s colleagues knew anything
of the agreement entered into between the Pre-

Isn’t it about time that Lyne and his ally,
Sydney Telegraph, furnished the names of those
imaginary gentlemen who, they say, sent along
large sums of money from Victoria to help along
the Billious interest in N.S.W. ? If any such
money was sent, there is absolutely nothing libel-
lous or dangerous in mentioning the fact that
certain Victorians subscribed to the N.S.W.
Federation fund. Therefore, the darkly-mysteri-
ous attitude of the anti-Billious can’t arise through
any need for caution. The only possible explana-
tion is that these murky Victorian conspirators
are mere inventions. Besides, owing to the dread-
ful curse of Protection, Victorians can’t have any
money.
Premier Reid, says his own special press
organ, “is cogitating. He is very desirous not to
make a mistake about his next step. He is
anxious to do the correct thing. He is carefully
considering the position, and will not commit
hiinself hurriedly to any announcement of the
future course of action. The Premier’s position is
one of some difficulty. He absolutely declines at
present to commit himself to any statement. All
that Mr. Reid now says is that he will consult his
colleagues at an early date, probably during the
present week.’’ In a word, the Man on the Fence
is now astride of a weathercock on the top of the
fence. Also, before saying anything, he will con-
sult his colleagues, the seven other God-fearing
Iscariots.
The mistake which led to the announce-
ment on the S.M. Herald’s notice-board that
over 80,000 votes had been recorded for the
Federal Bill was the saddest event of that cold
Friday night. Barton was humped round the
room in the Empire Hotel by his cheering friends,
and after that he got into the middle of an enthu-
siastic speech from the balcony where the Federal
flag was —and just then the 80,000 came down and
a bleak and disastrous 68,000 went up in its stead.
It was about the saddest pint of cold water which
ever fell on an enthusiastic politician in N.S.W.
The other side of the case was that dozens of the
enraged anti-Billious sloped violently for home
when the 80,000 went up, and gnashed their best
store teeth all night, and drank more than was good
for them, and raised Cain generally. Anybody who
saw a thoroughly wretched-looking cat next morn-
ing might safely bet that it was the cat of an anti-
Billious gentleman who had thrown it out by the
tail the night before while laboring under serious
misapprehension. Or else it was the cat of a
Billious gentleman who had thrown it out by the
tail while not laboring under any misapprehension
at all.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Maori kind ended its financial year at 31st March
with a surplus of £160,858, and the accumulation of
£354,286 brought forward from previous years made a
total surplus of £521,144. This is the tenth successive
surplus which Maoriland has produced, and considering
that it is the only province which has a war-debt, and the
only one which never had any great land-revenue, be-
cause Maoriland alone of all the seven states had to buy
much of its lands from the natives instead of getting them
all by plain robbery, this is a remarkable result. Out of
the surplus, £300,000 is paid into the Public Works Fund
for the purpose of building railways, &c., without piling
all the liability on posterity, and 221,144 is carried
forward. Maoriland is also the only province, bar Wes-
tralia, which has no accumulated deficit to work off, and
altogether, so far as fi lances go, it is distinctly ahead of
all the other six Australias.
» • * *
The N.S.W. revenue for May boomed suddenly
and violently up to £1,003,210, which was £110,406 more
than the revenue for May, 1897. The expenditure was
£643,710, and Treasurer Reid solemnly announces that of
this sum £602,079 is chargeable to this year, so apparently
he is still debiting large hunks of present expenditure
back to that dreary myth called the “ Dibbs deficit.” It
is now close on three years since the famous Cash Basis
started, and the finances don’t seem to be any nearer get-
ting on to it than they were before. Also, about a year
ago it was officially announced that only £7OOO or so re-
mained to be charged hack to previous years, and when
that was finished the Cash Basis would really get com-
menced. As far as can be gathered from the monthly re-
ports, about seven times that amount of expenditure has
since then been gently removed out of this year’s accounts
by putting it back into the expenditure of previous years,
and the Cash Basis is still being waited for.
For the 11 months of the financial year that
have now elapsed, the N.S.W. revenue is £8,661,189, or
£227,961 more than was realised for the corresponding
11 months of 1897. The land-tax supplies £216,942 of the
increase. Expenditure is nominally £8,647,452, so that,
there is a visible surplus of £13,737. But for the shady
device of still charging back expenditure to ’95 and ’94,
and previous years, there would still be a considerable
deficit. All the same, the improvement is very great,
and it looks as if Reid’s accounts might show a small, real
surplus for the first time —always allowing for the fact
that all N.S.W. surpluses are made by charging the repairs
to old punts, and the new roof to the museum, and the
new crockery at Jenolan accommodation-house, and about
£500,000 to £BOO,OOO of other little jobs to loan account as
“ permanent and reproductive works.”
* ♦ * *
N.S.W. Government gathered in ,£858,523 of
borrowed money during May. Seemingly, 1898 is going
to be a real good borrowing year.
• * * »
Queensland’s revenue for May was £291,594, an
increase of over £39,000 on May, ’97. For the eleven
months the revenue (£3,417,000) is £113,000 better than
for the first eleven months of last financial year, and, so
far, the receipts are £625,000 ahead of expenditure.
m * * *
Westralia’s revenue for May was £215,631, as
compared with £221,501 for May, 1897—a decrease of
£5870. For the eleven months that have expired of the
financial year the revenue is £2,473,495, so, with only one
month’s revenue to come in, there is no earthly hope of
reaching the estimated £3,008,000. At the end of April
the expenditure was already 11326,381 in advance of re-
venue, and the gap keeps widening. At the same time,
Forrest’s finances are not to be judged by N.S.W. stan-
dards. Westralia for a good while has charged to revenue
all the small accounts for repairs to public works, half of
which go into loan charges in N.S.W., and has also paid
out of revenue for a great many new works, all of which
N.S.W. charges to loans. In Australia, as a rule, the man
who makes the public expenditure large by paying all
legitimate charges out of revenue is cursed as a spend-
thrift by people who never look at details, and is thrown
out; the one who keeps expenditure down by charging
things to loans, and running into debt, and leaving
posterity to pay the bill, is called an economist. The
great mistake consists in mixing up ordinary and public
works expenditure in one budget. The latter should be
paid, as in Maoriland, out of a separate Public Works
Fund. Then the ordinary expenditure would show how
much each Government spent on merely running the
State departments, and the honest Treasurer who fed the
Public Works Fund out of revenue wouldn’t he regarded
as a spendthrift, and the prodigal who supplied it out of
borrowed money wouldn’t be called an economist.
As a case in point, Dibbs, in 1892, was a spend-
thrift because he spent £9,931,411 in all, while the econo-
mist, G. Reid, spent only £9,523,057 in 1896—difference,
£408,354. The other difference was that Dibbs, in ’92,
paid for 61,387,203 worth of public works out of revenue,
while Reid, in ’96, paid only £978,497 difference,
£408,706. Dibbs, the spendthrift, actually ran the
country on an expenditure of £352 less than the sham
economist who came after him ; his offence was that he
charged to revenue the works which Reid charges to
posterity. If there was a separate Public Works Fund,
and all expenditure on works came out. of it, then the
bogus economy which consists of running into debt would
be publicly recognised and called by its right name.
Tasmania’s May revenue, £78,788 ;an increase
of £12,038 as compared with May, ’97. The Raspberry Isle
begins its financial year on Ist January, and the four
months that are gone show an increase of £47,126 as com-
pared with the figures this time last year. Treasurer
Fysh already reckons that he will be able, during 1898, to
pay £120,000 off the old deficit. In proportion to popula-
tion, Tasmania shows the biggest increase in revenue and
the biggest increase (barring Westralia’s) in the number
of its people ; and Treasurer Fysh is certainly wiping off
the back debts left him by his predecessors much more
rapidly than any other Australian financier.
Victoria’s revenue for May was £535,798—an
increase of £28,578 as compared with May, 1897. For the
11 months which have elapsed the revenue is £6,309,696,
an increase of £233,094 as compared with the receipts for
the corresponding 11 months last year. Turner now
reckons that, even after paying his regular annual
£250,000 off the old deficit, a surplue is assured for 30th
June. ,
S.A. has something like £3,000,000 of public
debt falling due next year, and, by floating a renewal loan
at 3 per cent., it hopes to save £30,000 or £40,000 per
annum interest. Which is very cheering—only, unhappily,
the prices of the commodities which Australia sends to
Europe in payment of the interest keep falling much
faster than the interest itself. And, considering the pre-
sent value of money, the interest payable on the old debts
which the large, lavish, drinky Fathers of the Country left
behind them is truly awful. In ’96, these provinces were
still paying 6 per cent, on nearly £2,000,090 of debt
(£849,400 of this belongs to luckless S.A., and some of it
isn’t redeemable till 1918), 5 per cent, on 4A millions, 4£
percent, on 7 millions, and 4 per cent, on 129 millions.
Considering that money is now worth only 3 per cent., that
last item alone is equal to a dead loss of £1,290,000 a year.
* * * »
The Standard Bank (Melbourne), which recon-
structed in 1891, 1893 and 1895, will apparently either
have to reconstruct again this year or perish. It
owes £729,445 to depositors ; and its assets, set down in
the balance-sheet at about £840,000, are estimated by a
committee of experts, which has lately subjected them to
careful overhaul, as likely to realise about £ 85,000. The
first instalment of the reconstructed deposits falls due in
August, and the whole institution would hardly suffice to
pay that one small instalment. Therefore, it seems about
certain that the Saddest Bank on Earth will go out of
business this year.
* • • *
Ten years ago there were over 300,000 sheep in
the S.A. districts west of Port Augusta. Now there are!
probably 8000. The long series of droughts has eaten up
the rest.
• • * •
The directors of the Overflow claim that their
cyanide experiment was not a failure, but a success,
though some additions, which it was hoped might be dis-
pensed with, must now be made to complete the plant.
Pending the perfecting of cyanide arrangements, expendi-
ture has been reduced to a minimum by the dismissal of
nearly all hands.
« * * *
Sydney D.T. mining-man has revisited Peak
Hiil’mine, and reports the striking of a vein of mineral-
ised ore at 600 ft., very rich in gold, assays up to 2500 z.
per ton having been made. Here we have the explana-
tion of the enquiry started for these shares some two or
three months ago when they could be got for fourpence.
They are now more than ten times the price. Next to
nothing has ever been published about this new develop-
ment —made, the writer is told, in March—and the reason
is said to be that the mine authorities have had so many
disappointments they thought it better to await develop-
ments. All very well, hut who are the people that have
been buying shares meanwhile ?

Mt. David (N.S.W.) : “403 tons ore mined;
415 tons crushed, yielding 4510 z. smelted gold, worth
£4 os. 5Ad. per ounce ; average, loz. ldwt. 18gr. per
ton ; total value, £lBl7 3s. 6d. ; total cost., £687 Bs. ;
net profit, £1129 15s. 6d.” This is concise, and reads
well. But there is something else the writer would like
to be clear about. It is alleged that the mine is in no
condition to keep going the new battery, now almost
finished ; that to get these good crushings the ore has been
won, more or less, at the expense of development ; that
the time is nearing when this money, instead of going to
clear-off a big debt, will have to go back into the mine to
put It in shape ; and that, meanwhile, some knowing
people are getting out. Whatever the value of these
rumors, the mine has been worked in so curious a fashion
that time had better he allowed to show its real value.

Dear B. —Re Lake George mines. There is no
difficulty in raising and smelting a ton of sulphide, ore at
18s. Perhaps you are not aware that this coarse matte
on its first appearance runs about 13 per cent, copper,
and that it has to be again put through the smelter. To
what percentage of product does the “ three per cent, coni
consumption ” refer ? Are you aware that it takes fully
80 tons of raw ore to produce a hundred units of copper
from this famous mineral at a cost of fully £77 ? (See late
report.) 1.27 p.c.—a little over 281 b. of copper per ton cf
raw ore—where is the div. part to come in ? The eminent
metallurgist now in charge will perhaps explain to—Scrip.

The prophetic Bottomley “He would refer to
his old love, the Northern Territories Goldfields, which he
had taken an active part in bringing before the investing
public. He took every opportunity of accepting respon-
sibility for that business, and he held out to those directly
or indirectly concerned the expectation of quite a
phenomenal result at no distant date. On their intrinsic
merits they were worth £lO a share, and he hoped before
long they would be seen at that price. He was not a
sporting man, that was to savin the city—(laughter)—but
he wished he were as sure of the winner of the Derby as
he was of that fact. (More laughter.) At any rate, when
he came to think of it, he was not certain that he was not
equally sure of both—(laughter, this time with cheers).”
Bottomley’s “ old love ” proved very fickle. Within two
weeks of that speech, instead of going from £4 to £lO,
they went back to 305., and threaten to go down to 10s.
Wonder if Bottomley is talking of taking ail the responsi-
bility now ! He promised the shareholders something
sensational, and they have got it with a vengeance—at no
distant date, as he said. Probably his certainty of the
Derby winner was equally misplaced.

Of course the recent fluctuations don’t necessarily mean
anything. For instance, in December, ’94, the output
was worth £104,000, and it didn’t again get within gun-
shot of that figure till October, ’95, when it reached
£105,000. Then it dropped about one-half, and until
June, ’96, when it reached £106,000, Westralian gold-
mining seemed as if it was dying a natural death.

The new co. formed in Adelaide to takeover
the General Gordon has begun by making a call of 6d. per
share. Meanwhile the mine enjoys exemption, and the
splendid winding and battery engines (among the best in
W.A.) are being rapidly destroyed by rust. Prof. Krause,
the late manager, is located at Kalgoorlie, and, according
to advt., is prepared to act as geological expert, &c.
Paddington Consols, a show near Broad Arrow
(W.A.), from which great things were expected, has toed
the mark at lid wt. to the ton. As the reef is some 12ft.
wide it. is possible that, if well managed, she may even-
tually reach the div.-list.
m m * *
Hit or Miss, Mulgarrie, again shut down. The
co. pulled itself together a little while ago, and having
secured the services of Jimmy Henderson, a veteran
manager from Charters Towers, evidently determined to
make a final effort to test the value of the property.
Contracts were let for the deepening of two shafts to
300 ft., and the battery was set going on stone taken from
an open cut on the famous Black Reef, which, at clean-
up, proved worth a shade over 2dwt. This and other
circumstances probably proved too much for the long-
suffering shareholders.

many cases, the mines, too, are as good as ever—which
means that they were never any good at all. The want
of a market is caused by the want of gold in the mines,
the prospectuses of which are now good for brooding pur-
poses.
* * * •
So far, the filter-presses treating slimes on Lake
View Consols mine are showing an extraction of about 86
per cent. The cost of handling and treatment and the
exact percentage of recovery are not available, but should
be known in a week or two. Should these come out all
rigid this system will attract big attention in other parts
of the world.

Great Boulder annual report: —The average
yield works out at 2oz. lO.jdwt., as compared with 3oz.
7dwt. for the previous year. By the operation of 30
stamps, instead of a varying number never exceeding 20,
the ore crushed last year was increased by 12,744 tons,
while the gold recovered has grown to the extent of
27,3430 z., the total being 83,2920 z. Dividends, £264,022,
representing 155 per cent, of its capital, against £223,705.
Cost per ton—opening up, 6s. 6d. per ton ; stoping, 13s. ;
battery expenses, 10s. Bd. ; general charges, 6s. sd. ; sun-
dries, sd.—total, £1 17s. 6d. per ton ; the principal saving
in battery expenses, which have been reduced more than
half. The assay value of the reserves of oxidised ore, loz.
14dwt., against 2oz. lldwt. given in ’96. The reserves of
sulphide and telluride ores— i.e., ore in sight, are 10,000
tons, the value of which is stated at loz. 16dwt. What
newspaper lies we have had from the camp about this !
Here it is clearly and officially admitted that the grade of
the ore is coining down rather too rapidly to be pleasant.
Attention, however, is directed to the concluding passage
of the manager’s report:—“ The deepest workings on the
lodes show the ore to be of high grade, having a width
equal to the average of that in the upper levels, and bear-
ing more evidence of permanence. Everything points to
the year 1898 being a prosperous one.” The directors add
their encouragement: “ They feel justified in saying that,
in their opinion, never in the history of the mine (lid the
prospects of the future appear more favorable than at the
present time.” No doubt for years to come the Boulder
will be a big producer, but its gold and divs. will come
from ore not half as rich as that crushed by it during
nearly three years past.
* • * *
Some weeks back the Great Boulder was suffer-
ing from “ rumors,” in London. Shares fell from 245. to
16s. —a drop of nearly in value. Daily Mail
gave it out that the high-grade ore was failing, that the
mine would soon have to depend on its low-grade ore, that
the eyes had been all picked out, and the days of highly
profitable returns were over. The management replied
denying it all. The Critic showed clearly that the
returns had come down in easy stages, from an average
of 3oz. per ton to 2oz. Where is the mine in
God’s green earth—as Kurnel Bell would say—that didn’t?
It was that way at Mt. Morgan, Broken Hill, and the
Rand mines. As stamps and tonnage go up averages go
down. Tonnage enables the Daily Mail to turn out a
good paper lor a half-penny, and tonnage will no doubt
enable the Great Boulder to turn out the same divs. at
2oz. as it did when the average was 3oz.

Dear Bulletin. —Re a boomish-looking para-
graph lately printed by you. As an outsider, profoundly
ignorant of mining history, I would like to offer my prob-
ably stupid remarks on the Chiilagoe Proprietary spec.
Assuming that I am fairly correct in my figures, this is
how the present situation strikes me. Some time ago a
number of Melbourne speculators decided that a copper-
boom would fill a long felt want, to which end they
formed a syndicate—2oo shares of £lOO each. They
selected the old, not to say abandoned, copper district
of Chiilagoe, in N. Queensland, and the 200 original
shares were mostly no doubt taken up with the hope that
whatever the real worth of the field in copper produce, it
would at least prove a lucrative boom “pitch.” The in-
vestors were mostly “good” men i.e., silk - hatted
mortals who have waxed plump and important on the first
proceeds of previous booms—yet it was a significant fact
that none of them cared to run a great risk in this
matter. They put down sums ranging from the
minimum £lOO to £5OO or £6OO apiece mere
trifles to men who fare sumptuously every day.
The Chiilagoe scheme was practically a railway scheme,
based on the supposition that Chiilagoe is a gorgeous
greeny tract of solid copper, waiting all these years for a
means of transmitting its green stuff to market, and that
N. Queensland has been waiting for strangers to come
forward and seize an easy opportunity to develope the
“ vast mineral resources of the country.” The formation
of the syndicate was the signal for a whisper that the
Copper Boom had started. Melbourne rises at the word
“ Boom ” like a trout at a fly. Chiilagoe shares advanced
rapidly from the £lOO par. until it seemed advisable to
water them, and after some judicious watering they
sprouted more vigorously than ever. To-day they are
quoted at £lBOO apiece, which represents, I am told, a
paper value of nearly £BOO,OOO for the property. Genuine
buyers for holding (if there were any such) would be nowa-
days counting on the certainty of Chiilagoe yielding—
well, how much value in copper should Chiilagoe
yield before the net profits amounted to £BOO,OOO,
plus handsome interest on capital, during the
years spent in effecting this glorious result ?
The £lBOO quotation has been arrived at through the
persistent whispering of the aforesaid “ good ” men and
their myrmidons. Chiilagoe Proprietary shares, says the
Whisper, are to touch £lO,OOO, £20,000, £40,000 a-piece,
You just buy, and sell at a fine profit next month. Take
£lO,OOO as the figure adopted by the more “ representa-
tive” boomsters among these dear, “good” men. It means
that Chiilagoe profits, after paying for a railway and
mining exes., must amount to, say, six or seven millions
sterling ere the field is worked out. But this is not the
full strength of the Chillagoeic allegation. There are
bands of minor performers, taking up adjacent properties,
and playing variations on the main Whisper theme. To
the visionary wealth of these green fields there is no end.
And N. Queensland, we are to believe, has allowed its
noble possession to be pegged out by noisy pilgrims from
foreign parts, who arrived like so many Excelsiorians,
bearing each a banner with the sweet device, “The
Copper Boom is Coming.” Don’t you think it has already
come as far as it ought to be allowed to get without pro-
test from—A Copper-Headed Juggins?
• * * »
Within a few weeks Chiilagoe copper-camp will
no doubt have its own newspaper—its own mining boom-
ster by wire. It will import a few up-to-date men from
Westralia —in which case we shall have the same lying
business over again per medium of the innocent Sydney
press. Meanwhile, there is a man standing to the wire in
Brisbane. He proclaims that there is a large influx of
Southern syndicates, speculators, investors, and the pros-
pects of Chiilagoe w»ere never brighter. There is reason
to believe that Q. Govt. Geologist Jack is on his way to
the field. Jumpers have arrived already and started biz.
Thousands of acres have been pegged. N.S.W. is repre-
sented by the “veteran mining-expert” W. S. M'Dowell 1
who has pegged a large area of ground. This is tew much !
—but there is some consolation in knowing that, for the
present, Dowell has ceased pegging out N.S. W. Chiilagoe
Proprietary shares still in the neighborhood of £lBOO.
• • * •
Re Chiilagoe by wire “ Since Stewart’s
examination, the Chiilagoe Proprietary has taken up 940
additional acres. This is regarded here as significant
proof of the glowing nature of his report. Frew, the
C.P. railway engineer, is leaving for Melb. next week to
consult director, and it is expected that the building of
the Mareeba-Chillagoe line will start as soon as he returns.
A report to the C.P. on its water-power scheme at the
Barron falls for smelters will shortly be available. New
finds are reported from the junction of the Einasleigh
and Copperfield Rivers, and a story is current here that
Stewart himself has secured an option on the Noonday, a
big copper lode located by M'Lennan, eight miles south of
Bedford. Speculators are arriving daily, and amongst
the experts are G. A. Stockfeld and Herbert Daley.” First
meeting of Chiilagoe Proprietary shareholders held
in Melb. on Monday. Chapman, the man who has
taken the most prominent part in the inaugura-
tion and promotion of the whole scheme, pre-
sided, and did some tall talk for a man of his pe simistic
proclivities. Total area, 3009 acres, embracing seven dis-
tinct groups of mines. Cast of railway to be known in a
month. Believed with tolerable certainty that £500,000
capital w’ould be sufficient to place the undertaking on a
profit-earning basis. The move now’ is that the pre-
sent holders provide £lOO,OOO of this amount to show
their confidence, and to put the directors in a good posi-
tion to raise the balance. New co. to be 900,000 shares of
£2—£1,800,000 ; present holders for their rights and sub-
scription of £lOO,OOO to have 580,000 shares fully paid.
With £lOO,OOO in hand, it is thought the balance can
be raised by a debenture-issue suitably secured, with right
to convert into shares within a certain time. Chapman
remarked that Stewart had examined the whole of the
mines as well as the railway-route. Though it would be
some time before his complete report w'as available, he
had admitted that the properties justified the building of
the line w'ithout delay, providing facilities for treatment
of the ores and generally w’orking the mines on a scale
commensurate with their magnitude. The directors con-
gratulated the holders, and expressed their unanimous
and unhesitating conviction concerning the great possi-
bilities of the mines. The scheme was passed w'ithout a
murmur, and everybodj went home well pleased—some,
no doubt, to dream of the immeasurable wealth Chapman,
Reid, Knox, Stewart, Moffatt, and other mining philan-
thropists had in store for them.

wich, and Mt. Perry, near Bundaberg. The lat' er was a
MTlvvraithian venture, and, in the good old days, had a
railway built to its owm cheek.

* • * *
One of the (at present) best paying claims on
Croydon was, some time ago, being financed by a local
bank. Things looking blue, the manager of latter insti-
tution declined to subsidise the show any further, when
the Q.N.B. stepped in and took over the business. The
Q.N.B.’s luck this time was in the ascendant, and the con-
cern is now one of the most flourishing in the North.
* * * m
Aitkin and Grant’s find at Tara go River, Gipps-
land, created a regular bush-fire sensation in the dis-
trict, and the city mining men sickened with a fever of
longing at the figures blazoned forth in the morning
dailies—“lB77oz. Bdwt. 22gr. fine gold to the ton.” “ Fine
gold ” seems altogether superfluous and uncalled-for.
Alas ! what a slump in public interest followed
when, a day or two later, it became known that
only a sample about as large as a piece of cheese had been
sent from the dyke at Tarago, and the assay of this gave
an average of “18770 z. Bdwt. 22gr. fine gold to the ton.”
Oh, those assays ! It’s like sending a man’s gold-stopped
molar to the assayer, and hearing a week later that the
auriferous citizen would go “ 18770 z. Bdwt. 22gr.” pure
gold to the ton. We are all of us of “ average ” wealth,
according to the assayer’s fairy system.

Twenty-five years ago, a party of Sydney com-
mercial men, some of whom still survive, formed a com-
pany to purchase and colonise about half of the then
known Papua. The Governor of the day communicated
with the British officials, and the issue was a summary
snuff-out of the daring enterprise.

Society
The full published particulars of the gilded
Mr. Jim Crotty’s will show it to be just about as
disreputable as early rumors represented it.
Crotty’s wife gets =£loo a year for life out of the
old man’s glittering pile ; his four sisters and two
brothers get .£lOO a year each ; one executor gets
<£3oo a year and another <£loo, and a R.C. priest
.£3OO a year. Then all the rest of the pile—
alleged to be .£150,000 —goes to the church. Old
Crotty was benevolent after the usual fashion.
He hung on to the money while he had any use
for it himself, and, when he had no further use for
it, he gave it to the cause of religion, in
the hope that the gift would be of use
to himself in the next world. He didn’t
deny himself for the sake of his faith, but he
denied his bereaved widow like a hero, so Mrs.
Crotty has to fall from affluence and live on
.£llBs. sd. a week in order that Crotty may be a
remarkably good man after his death. It is a
miserably mean kind of piety which offers
up the old woman on the altar of its
faith—a kind of piety which wants to be
enthusiastically kicked by every decent person in
the community. All the Seven Provinces badly
require a law which will give the old woman a
lien on the old man’s money when he dies, so that
he can’t rob her by way. of paying a big fire-
insurance premium on his own scared soul. For,
mark you, the old woman is the old man’s full
partner, by all that’s fair and square ; the old man
deliberately took her into partnership and signed,
sealed and delivered the contract, and even if he
did not promise in writing to go her fair halves,
there was at least an honorable understanding to
that effect. He endowed her, not the Church,
with “all his worldlv goods ” in the marriage
service. Is Holy Church going to act as re-
ceiver to Mr. Jim Crotty’s theft from his wife, or
is it going to offer her the square deal which was
denied her by the old hunks? Receivers are as
had as thieves ; also, no man should be allowed to
leave money to any church without having first
provided for his own wife in accordance with the
financial station at which they had ar rived at the
time of his death.
• * • *
A droll incident occurred at a certain
Government House on Queen’s Birthday.
Amongst the mob who came to shake His Ex-
cellency’s fist was an aged person of High-
land descent, sporting the original kilt, plaid, and
bonnet in which he tramped his native heather 40
years ago. The kilt was held together in places
by wire and pieces of string. The eagle’s feather
no longer waved defiantly, but hung limp, broken
in two places. The weather being bitterly cold,
the old warrior wore under his kilt a pair of
flannel pants. He declined to change anything in
the retiring-room, but proceeded into the recep-
tion-room ; in sight of all, he pulled off his pants,
and, throwing them on a seat, was presented to
the Governor, who shook him warmly by the
hand.

“ Scot ” :
Be loafing in N.Q. hospitals. The same holds good in
N.S.VV. and Vic. I have known “forty-fives" and a
concertina going in the big ward when a man was next to
the gates of life. The eoncertinisfc had a little sore in his
leg, kept open by scratching round the edge. At grape-
picking and lamb-marking time it healed up. With a
determined doctor, or even wardsman, the best prescrip-
tion to. clear such trash out of the hospital is floor-scrub-
bing, window-cleaning and, above all, low diet. There is
a certain hospital that has eight or ten derelicts always
there, and they own the shanty. One man, after six
weeks, really got fever, and there was found on him a
bank deposit receipt for £llO. He had entered as a
pauper, but the secretary is Scotch, and knew how to
deal with the banking account.

“Convert” sends a poser to The Bul-
letin :
Knowing you as a theological authority, can you inform
me what the Anglican Blessing is as practised between
dear brethren and sisters. My curiosity has been aroused
by this. Going down street at 10.30 this morning I wit-
nessed a meeting between an Anglican bishop, who is no
longer very young, and a rather pleasant-featured young
lady. There was a cordial yum-yum sort of handshake ;
then the bishop, after a rapid glance round, put his gloved
hand to his lips, osculated thereon, and laid it swiftly on
those of his companion, who dittoed pianissimo. Not
much in this, but there was a sort of wouldn’t-I-like-to
twinkle in the eye of the bishop, reciprocated by the
lady, which lent much glowful color to the incident.
Hence my thirst for knowledge as aforesaid, previous t 0
making a beeline to join the Anglican community Don’t
dash my hopes by telling me this blessed privilege is con-
ne(lto bishops.

S.O’B. : The
ruling passion.
In a lane off
George-st., Syd-
ney, is a dossers’
penny lodging-
house. Stopping
to watch a “fa-
kir,” who had
“pitched” in the
lane, and was offer-
ing the odds on the
vari-colored wheel —I saw
one of the dossers put
down a penny on the 4 to 1
chance, and lose it. Then he
began to whine —no bed ; have
to sleep out, and so on. The fakir
took pity on him, and gave him back
his penny. The dosser stood watching
the game for awhile ; then, despite the
fakir’s caution that there would be no
giving back this time, he staked again 4 to 1—
lost, and went off up the street without a word.”
A rorty spectacle was provided in a fashion-
able bar, up North, the other night, when a cleric
(in costume), a well-known jockey, and half-a-
dozen of the latter’s friends held high revel. The
horseman had exchanged hats with the parson
early in the evening, and saw the whole of the fun
through in a broad-brimmed tasselled affair, which
harmonised with his adamantine face no better
than his own perky-felt did with the monkey-beard
of the cleric. The latter was in no way disturbed,
however, and, when the racing-man tired of the
fun, he made things willing enough “ on his own.”
Ultimately, the whole crowd drove off in hansoms
for —supper.

“ To- enjoy a thing exclusively is com monly
to exclude yourself from the true enjoyment of it,”
says Thoreau. A stab at marriage ?
Melb. : “I pass daily by the Out-
Patient Department of Melb. Hospital.
It is positively the most dismal, horrid
place in the city. Why ? When poor crea-
tures are possessed by the blue-devils, they
have to go to a damp-looking, wretched
den, more melancholy than any about the
gaol. Watch the crowd of men and
women in the waiting-room, an ingenious
compound of a Methodist conventicle and
the ante-chamber of Hell, the walls a sug-
gestion of a leprous lazaretto, while the
benches look as if made out of all the old
court Bibles. I claim that the department
should be a pavilion in the pleasant garden
of the front, just as cheerful as at the Alfred
Hospital.”
* * * *|
Into the casualty-room was carried
the form of a young woman with unusually
luxuriant hair. Her head had been badly
damaged in a street accident; one Sin. gash
running round the base of the skull. Sur-
geon (to assistant) : “ Get the shears—all
this hair 11 have to come off.” The poor
girl, hovering between life and death, heard,
and, with one supreme effort, feebly ex-
claimed : “No! no! Not mv hair!
Please don’t.’’

Holdar M. : “Have noticed an in
increasing tendency on woman’s part to en-
croach on the restaurant accommodation
intended specially for males. The change
does not seem to be appreciated by the trou-
sered tyrant, who wants to wolf bis food and
mash the waitress apart from hostile obser-
vation. The waitress, too, jibs against
serving her haughty sister, who, she says,
is fond of making complaints, and is harder
to please than a tableful of men. In any
case, eating being such a dreadfully unro-
mantic business, the whiskered sex should
be protected from lady intruders. The
inured waitress doesn’t count.
No sense of humor in the daily press.
The other day, a Melb. man found an ominous-
looking brown-paper parcel, labelled “infernal
machines,” on the doorstep of his office. He
cautiously shifted it to a vacant allotment, where
it could explode with safety, so to speak, and then
discovered the contents to be 11 second-hand
Bibles. And this was what the papers called “ a
stupid practical joke.” Why not “a brilliant
topical satire ?
* * » *
Melbourne has been antagonistic to its
tramway men ever since they “ struck ” for higher
wages at a time when they were getting about
10s. a week more than their present pay. The
strike didn’t seem to be justified, and a lot of
people expressed their feelings by raising a sub-
scription for the “blacklegs ” who had prevented
a sudden stoppage of the. tramway service. A
strike which inconveniences the public is accursed
for evermore if it doesn’t get a vote of sympathy
at the outset.

regal blundering records.

* * * *
On a recent Sunday evening Melb. Con-
gregational Dr. Bevan told his congregation that
there were pickpockets present, but that he
hoped God would change their hearts. Where-
upon the godly glared enquiringly at each other,
and dropped their prayer-books, and clutched
their purses, and the spirit of devotion went down
badly before the devils of suspicion and greed.
And, when the collection came on, few people
dared withdraw their purses from their pockets.
So that the plate suffered and the curly Bevan
probably was sorry he spoke.

The Snodgrass-Gold tragedy is a pretty
large field for Westralian speculation. Snodgrass
(aged 55) and the late Captain Gold (aged nearly
70) were chummy. On the death of the captain,
a year ago, his comparatively young widow (aged
33, or 46, or something in between) found consola-
tion for a while in the bosom of the Snodgrass
family, and, when the family opened dining-
rooms in Hunt-street, Coolgardie, the widow went
with them. The restaurant soon collapsed, where-
upon Mrs. Gold obtained a place as nurse at the
hospital, and Snodgrass visited her once a week.
Having a daughter and another lady relative
among the staff, his frequent visits looked harm-
less, and his little attentions to his old friend’s
widow passed as properly sympathetic under the
circs. The nurses, who are credited with a know-
ledge of most things that happen in Coolgardie,
didn’t blush over the Snodgrass-Gold “friendship,”
and, when that friendship could crawl through the
needle’s eye of their rigid inspection and come out
uninjured at the other side, it may easily be re-
garded as absolutely sinless. Perhaps Snodgrass
shot the lady in despair of her ever going astray.
* < • *
A Bulletin scribe re the Coolgardie
tragedy
Nurse Gold’s murder by Kenneth Snodgrass, a much-
married man of 55—who at once shot himself dead—re-
moves from that wicked willy-willy-stricken town a bright
little lady ; her sad death will bring tears to the eyes of
many patients who were ministered to by her dainty
hands and cheery smile. Snodgrass is not the first man
by a long chaik to make a fool of himself over a
local hospital nurse. Two doctors rendered them-
selves objects of ridicule for many days by their
jealous bickerings, of which the nurse whom they adored
was, womanlike, totally unconscious. Also, a fever
patient in the convalescent stage developed love symptoms
in alarming degree, which eventually broke out in the
shape of declaration and an impassioned request that
the nurse would say “ Yes ” (writer occupied
next stretcher). Much to the writer’s relief, the nurse’s
reply was beautifully evasive, whereupon came the deter-
mined rejoinder, “If you don’t promise to marry me
I’ll die. I’ll make myself die.” And he did die. Went
on a wild jamboree immediately he was discharged, was
brought in to exactly same cot in a state of collapse, and
soon threw in his cards almost in his goddess’ arms.

The A.N.A. uoom for Federation at Melb.
Town Hall was quite a triumph of stage-manage-
ment and dramatic effect. Before the talking had
begun, and just when the “No” minority was
meditating a demonstration against the bootnsters,
the organ struck up “Yankee Doodle.” Roars
of applause. Then “ Rule Britannia ” and an in-
creased volume of roar. After which the chair-
man read a telegram from Barton, and announced
that each speaker would be limited to a ten-
minutes’ oration. More enthusiasm at the news
that no performer would get a chance to grow
wearisome. The discovery that each man had
been appointed to furnish his own particular sec-
tion of subject matter was another glad surprise,

SHE: “ Yes ; probably put it on just before she came out! ”
and what with the rapid succession of spouters
and sundry musical turns to vary the proceedings
the “Noes” were hopelessly demoralised. They
got no openings for interruption. A brisk and
bright entertainment (as political meetings go)
concluded with the best. imitation of patriotic
enthusiasm that Melbourne has ever produced.

“ Melb.” ; Fawkner’s house, the first built
in this city, was reproduced at the Old Colonists’
carnival. I saw the real one, just before it was
knocked down, only about ten years ago.
Fawkner’s house, afterwards used as his print-
ing office, and subsequently as a sort of old
rag-and-bone appendage to a warehouse, stood
behind the Union Club Hotel, formerly the Shakes-
peare, corner of Collins and Market-streets.

“ Yiator ”: I especially like the Old
Colonists’ Homes, because there is not the faintest
suggestion of the workhouse about them. They
are charming brick villas, hard to equal amid
places of their stamp, with pleasant, well-kept
gardens, whereas the attempted gardens at other
charitable places get like the neglected graves in
cemeteries, with the broken, rotten railings em-
blematic of grief. Mr. Coppin is justifiably ner-
vous lest his evaporation should be followed by
that of his two noble charities, the O.C. and the
Decayed Actors’ Homes, from which dear old
Gladstone emerges, to do the Napoleonic veteran,
in “ A Royal Divorce.”

Story of a giddy little Tottie and
a great Australian daily. One even-
ing lately Tottie strolled into the office
and handed in this advt. :
Miss Flossie Goodenough begs to notify
her numerous friends that she has removed
from 348 J Denuire-street, Carlton, to 902 Se-
lect-street, Richmond, and trusts to receive
a continuance of their favors.
The advt. was duly set-up, queried by
the printer’s “reader,’’and finally came
under notice of the sub-editor. The
latter eyed it doubtfully ; it looked so-
so, but then, again, it might emanate
from a respectable dressmaker, or a
most virtuous white - worker. The
members of the staff were called upon,
but they one and all appeared to be
afflicted with utter ignorance, or tem-
porary lapse of memory, or denseness
of intellect. Finally the sporting-edi-
tor arrived. Now, the s.e. is a godly
young man of the Brunker brand, and
when he had read the advt. he con-
demned it at once. Then the sub-edi-
tor was satisfied, and said he was so
glad that at least someone on the staff
knew Miss Goodenough. Whereupon
a wicked laugh went round, and the
sporting-editor went out and kicked
himself.
* * ♦ *
Dear B.—You might take this
on as a puzzle : Where do the servants
sleep in Sydney ? In places I have
lodged, the problem has in nearly
every case baffled me. In the only
instances in which I have solved it, I
found one sleeping in a cubby-house,
between the dog’s-kennel and the
fowlhouse ; another, under the coun-
ter ; and still another, under a stair-
case that led into the yard. I had no
other motive than curiosity, and don’t
walk in my sleep. How about a law
for the protection of indoor servants ?
—Maria.
* * * *
Again the guileless nigger !
At Bundaberg, t’other day, an abor-
iginal committed for trial on a charge
of killing his gin by an undue enforce-
ment of club-law took objection to his
wife's giving evidence against him.
The possession of a gin is, of course,
native custom ; the holding of a wife
as well is a concession to civilised cus-
tom ; the belting to death with a nulla is aboriginal
tradition again ; the raising of aforesaid objection
in Court is civilisation with a vengeance !

The two Sydney lady-doctors have settled
down quietly to work at Adelaide Hospital,
although, it is said, they did not receive a very
cordial reception from the male medicine - men
previously in sole possession. The trouble which
for years lias raged round the institution has now
subsided, and, for the first time since the row
began, there is professional peace in the farina-
ceous village.

In a recent S.M. Herald appeared a
learned disquisition on the “mystic number,’’
which was alleged to be 9, because so many per-
sons of greater or less fame {e.g., Gladstone and
F. W Haddon ?) were born in the years ending
’O9, T 9, ’29, and so on. But this sort of argument
would apply to almost any number—what’s the
matter with 15, anyhow ? Magna Charta was
signed in 1215, Agincourt was won in 1415, Water,
100 in 1815. But, after all, as a certain cynic onc e
THE NEW THEATRE HAT—AT SYDNEY CRITERION
observed, “ The magio number is Number One,
and it doesn’t do to forget it.”
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.—l was last week a drunk
passenger on board “ the drunks’ train ” —11.25
p.m. on Saturday, Sydney to Richmond. I’m
invariably maddened by the check-notice —
“Break the glass and press the button to stop the
train ” ; for the penalty-warning underneath seems
to dare you to break, with reason or without. On
previous occasions I haven’t been drunk enough to
defy the notice ; but on Saturday I was. I broke
the glass with a stirrup-iron, pressed the button,
and waited for the deluge. It didn’t come—
nothing caine. The train didn’t even stop; and
all my bravery was wasted. Now, just how much
security to passengers does this inefficient check
offer ?—B.

Adelaide sometimes takes an intensely
practical fit, as it did lately when the relics of the
Calvert Exploring Expedition were sold at auction.
Spite of puffs preliminary in which sand-drives
and treeless deserts and weary waiting and
watc’.iing figured largely, Johnny All-sorts had
most of the lines to himself; and it is recorded
with much bathos that, instead of Adelaide
flocking to get souvenirs of the disastrous jaunt,
“a riding saddle on which the renowned and
deeply-lamented explorers jogged thro’ the thirsty
wilderness went to an unpoetic general dealer for
a sovereign.” So the “ few melancholy memo-
rials left of the latest explorers are scattered
and the hobbles that once adorned the great Cal-
vert’s camels now prevent Smith’s cow from
wandering too far outside Smith’s back yard.

The Chinaman sent to S.A. quarantine-
station as a leper has substantial cause of action
against S.A. health authorities. It turns out that
he is only “ suffering from the effects of a Chinese
plaster applied to the leg four months ago,” and
the yellow man is now in Adelaide Hospital.
Wah Lee will now probably be worried by an
army of Adelaide lawyers anxious to take up his
case on spec.

Cheerful chunk from Nelson (M.L.) Even-
ing Mail’s Quee’bir’day loyal snort:
Not many more times will it be given us to hail her
natal day. A few more years—perhaps months —and the
joy-bells will be muffled, the crimson flags will be draped
in black —even as to-day the flags mount to the truck, to
fall to the half-mast again to-morrow, telling of him who
has gone before his Queen, her senior by but 10 years.
In view of this kind of rejoicement, perhaps it’s
as well that the Mail’s gruesome congratulations
are not in much danger of reaching the poor old
lady’s ear.

“ Creeve Roe ” on the Melb. bomb-ouf>
rage :
The party whom the criminal idiot responsible for the
deed seemed to have intended to send—with all his
household—to Kingdom Come, is an obscure, inoffensive,
retired publican named Maloney. I can understand
dynamiting anybody named Romanoff—but why blow up
Maloney? If the crime was really—as the misspelt word
“Anachy” on the bomb would seem to indicate—
planned by Anarchists, they must have pitched on their
proposed victim in a fashion similar to that attributed to
the N.S.W. Govt.-billet dispensers who, it is said,
used to settle all conflicting claims with the oracular
sentence— “ Appint Maloney ! ” The Age, in its report
of the affair, says : “ Between 10 and 12 years ago there
was a weak-kneed brotherhood in the city prone to give
way to extreme talk ; and sparsely-attended meetings
were held ; but these, small at the outset, dwindled away
to nothing.” Quite true. J discovered that brotherhood
and described it for the Age in an article Entitled
“ Terrible Fellows.” When I attended one of their meet-
ings—which were held in an upstairs room in one of the
small two-storey shops that used to occupy the present
site of the Alexandra Theatre—I felt that I was carrying
my life in my hands, and would probably not carry it far.
But after I had ascended the dingy stairs, and, passing
the janitor, who had eyes of the color of weak soup,
had entered the meeting-room and taken my place beside
a wraith-like female with scanty hair parted on one
side of the head, and had glanced over the gathering
generally—l was no more afraid. An orator with a feeble,
wispy beard, and pigmentless eyes, and a consumptive-
looking chest, and a small, black tie fixed in front of a
paper collar, was informing the audience that the only
way of achieving anarchic freedom was by wading
through Seas of Blood. And he uttered the dreadful
word “ blood ” in a tone which the ordinary man would
have used in referring to milk-and-soda. But the
audience didn’t seem to get excited. This sanguinary
style of speech was, evidently, de rigueur in the Anar-
chists’ Club. Then several other terrific dynamitards—
who looked as if they had been casually crumpled up and
A MEDICAL ITEM.
In plain wrappers, secure from observation.
kept, by an oversight, under the counter of a pawnshop
for some months—arose, and, still in the same scrannel-
pipe key, proclaimed that what was wanted for the
regeneration of the world—and Melbourne particularly—
was Lots of Gore. Finally, someone came round with a
collection-plate and we dropped threepences into it and
I went away disgusted. I had been present at Dorcas
meetings that were far more lively. As for doing any-
thing—there wasn’t one of those sheep-faced, wholesale-
murder advocates who would have been able to summon
up sufficient courage, even when hungry, to decapitate a
fat fowl.

Evidently the grey mare wasthe better horse.
The foe—a furious hungry horde —
Had swept the land with fire and sword ;
And now, all bloated with renown,
Had come to pillage Sydney Town.
“ All’s lost! ” through desp’rate teeth a-clench
Hissed madly Major-General French.
But Colonel Burns said : “ Sir, restrain
Your grief—the Lancers yet remain 1”
It was a gay and gallant sight
To see them prancing out to fight
In scarlet coats, with haughty air,
The idols of the Sydney Fair.
The hostile General, when he spied
These splendid warriors, loudly cried,
With many dreadful foreign oaths—
“ Take them alive ! Don’t spoil their clothes !"
The enemy broke rank and chased
The Lancers o’er allotments waste;
Then poured the people from the town
And cut the scattered foemen down.
A cup now to the Lancers fill,
The warriors weird who dress to kill!
And let this truth be here conveyed—
Much Virtue dwells in Gold Brocade!

All Drapers Everywhere.
“Home, Sweet Home.”
Witat a beautiful place home is when the father
is bilious, the mother is suffering from female
weakness, and the baby has the croup; father
frowns and swears, mother scolds and grumbles,
and baby yells ! And yet this husband and wife
may be two of the best-natured and reasonable
people on the globe when they are in good health.
It may seem ridiculous to say that nearly all
domestic disagreements are caused by the liver,
but a careful study of the problem reveals the fact
that such an assertion is quite within the bounds
of reason. When persons are in ill-health they
are impatient, unreasonable and quarrelsome;
what more natural then than a family “tiff”?
Few people are cynical enough to believe that
married couples don’t wish for peace and harmony,
and some reason must, therefore, be found for
their all too common differences. Bile Beans come,
therefore, in the guise of a peacemaker ; they put
the liver in good order, and invigorate the whole
system, giving new life, as it were; then every-
thing goes as smoothly as a freshly-risen honey-
moon. A word to the wise: Marriage need not
be a failure when Bile Beans cure Indigestion,
Liver Troubles, and various other ailments,
thereby making husbands and wives happy com-
panions. Bile Beans are made in one of the
largest and host-equipped laboratories in the world
at Detroit, Michigan, U.S.A. They are an un-
doubted specific for Constipation, Biliousness,
Headache, Indigestion, Debility, Female Weak-
ness, and all Liver Troubles. For sale at all
chemists; price, 9Jd. per small box, large box
Is. lid., or six for Gs. Get the genuine ; take no
worthless substitutes.*
