A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, June 7, 1898.
Mr Dear Mtek—
A large but quiet crowd thronged Govt.
House drawing-rooms last week, and stood about
from 10 till 12 in the belief that it was having a
deliriously exciting time. The skim of the Cream
was net present, but there was plenty of that good
milk that goes thick if you keep it long enough,
and any quantity of the plain, skimmed material.
A few old-fashioned souls pined for the photo-
graphs and picture-books that used to lie round
under other regimes, but the majority wanted
nothing but to be There. Just inside the door
Lady Hampden and the Governor shook seven
hundred hands and smiled seven hundred smiles,
and careful consideration decided that Lady
Hampden has the most effective smile of all our
Governors’ wives. Hers is a gentle, motherly
radiance that reveals no sign of string-pulling.
The fashion of the evening was a sort of fancy-
mixed. Long sleeves and short sleeves, trains
and ankle-revealers, high neck and no neck, dress-
blouses and ball-costumes, betrayed the fact that
dressing for an at-hcme is a puzzle to those who
don’t know everything. Somewhere out of sight
a band played fitfully, the supper-room was open
early, and—well, I think that was about all. A
stirring definition of this class of entertainment
came my way the other day. “ You just ask
people—that’s an ‘ At-Home.’ ”
The Fresh Air League held its annual ball at
the Town Hall next evening. The committee is a
specially hard-working one, but there was that air
of isolation about the dancers that has charac-
terised several public balls this season. Indeed,
the year has witnessed a perceptible falling-off in
this particular industry. St. Vincent’s, the first
charity ball of the season, was the best patronised.
Prince Alfred Hospital made a good second, but
neither the Factory Girls’ not the Fresh Air
League was sought after according to its deserts.
It is lucky that this falling-off means just what
you would think it did not mean. It points to the
fact that Charity is on the increase, not on the
wane. Every little stowed-away suburb now hops
for its own poor, has committee-meetings over its
own washerwomen, and is so busy with its own
deaf, dumb and blind bazaars, that it cannot join
in general charities so largely as it used to. Under
this new system the poor are the gainers.
To go back to the Fresh Air Ball. One of the
handsomest dresses in the room was black tulle
over rose pink, with rose velvet revers worked
with pale appliques. Another nice black dress
was worn by Mrs. Rennie. There’s always a
peculiar costume at every public entertainment,
and you may be interested to hear that, this time,
it was a poplin of a hue that came out of a bottle.
Judson’s Magenta is the briefest name I can give
it. A puce-tinted-scarlet-that-has-been-through-
the-chastening-waters-of-the-blue-bag is longer,
but yields a more definite idea of the eccentric
tint. With this was worn, so help me Worth,
clumps of forget-me-nots and yellow peas, and a
spray of roses that had rubbed up against a boiled
salmon. Every one knows that many people are
obliged to dye, but surely a good, homely, honest
black would have been kinder both to the «m a rer
and her floral decorations.
An interesting feminine crowd foregathered in
the Girls’ High School one night last week to
work up an Old Girls’ Union. Ethel Turner, who
edited a High School paper years ago, made an
affectionate little speech and was loudly cheered
by her old school-chums. The head-mistress, Mrs.
Garvin, who has seen the High School through
teeth, measles, exams., and every youthful ail-
ment, and is warmly remembered by hundreds of
girls all over Australasia, was elected President of
the Union. A vice-president was found in Miss
Florence Thompson, the first lady-graduate of
Sydney University. Dr. Iza Coghlan, Ethel
Turner, and others form the committee, and every
three months there are to be re-unions in the old
schoolroom, with buns and coffee and no mere
men at all, not even husbands, if any High School
girls have married. And here, on behalf of the
sex, I may remark that gatherings of this kind
are the only attempts women make at smoke-
concert effects. Frills are unnecessary adjuncts,
and conviviality is the one and only reason for so
meeting.
Ada Ferrar strained her ankle last week, and
the lovely Mrs. Maesmore-Morris hurried into
Mercia’s robes for one night only. The delicate
beauty of the new Christian was characterised by
a little note of worldliness that broke the heart of
the big audience, when the lions stretched their
jaws around the corner. It seems that there is
less pang and pathos in watching a really holy,
saintly statues* go out to be eaten, than in looking
on at the martyrdom of a lady who would be such
a dream of loveliness in a cotillion.
Federation Friday saw Cartwright’s last per-
formance in Sydney. In spite of the monster
opposition in the streets, there was a crowded
house, and a demonstration broke out at
the close. The house sat in its seats for
many minutes, and cheered hotly and waited
hungrily for the usual farewell speech. A
quiet bow said all that Cartwright cared to say.
Had he spoken there were several nasty remarks
he might have made about “ the colonies,” but on
the whole that bow said more than words.
Next day flowers and friends gathered lavishly
at the Brisbane boat to see the very last of the
company. Cartwright sat aloof in a deck-chair
with a gltini face that warned off casual devotees.
Miss Lamb took things calmly in a little red hat,
and nobody lost his temper when the boat was put
back three hours. By this time, these good people
are used to petty annoyances. They stoically
made their way up town again, and Miss Lamo,
M iss Stewart, and Miss Morley spent the after-
noon in a box at the Tivoli, while Charles Thursby
wandered round the dismal Saturday-afternuon
streets and looked in shop-wmdows fur want of
more exciting games.
By the way, the dreadful rumor that follows
this elegant young actor from province to province
has been dispelled at last. A few days ago, a jolly
afternoon tea-revel rose in a body, at the request
of the maligned one, and pinched him carefully
round the waist. The charge of whalebone was
then dismissed, for want of supporting evidence.
Tempe Ball comes off on June 22nd, and rumors
of fancy sets, and a lively Swedish dance in which
anyone may join, are lending briskness to the sale
of tickets.
A strong band of professionals and amateurs are
working up the “ Cloches de Corneville.” Three
charity performances are to be given next month
at the Criterion by a caste that includes Miss
Josephine Deakin, Madame Sul dene, and Mr.
Burns Walker.
Ihe Society of Artists, now presided over by
the boyish-looking Sid. Long, breaks into fes-
tivities and an exhibition early in September.
The fancy-dress ball is now fixed for Sept. 7th.
Artists' female folk ought to have a great pull
over the wives and daughters of the ordinary
business man, whose only suggestion is Red Rid-
ing Hood or 80-Peep, or some artless personage
that couldn't interfere with his wife’s reputation.
The weird, wild melancholy of the average
melodrama, is illuminated with a dazzling row of
novelties in Bland Holts latest production,
“ Straight from the Heart.” Thunder and light-
ning, and all those banalities, are driven into the
dark ages by the horrors of a fire at sea, shrieking
passengers, wreckage on a desert coast, Arabs,
Anarchists, and a great, red, greedy guillotine.
The gods shuddered when the guillotine was
screwed to the proper point, and the hero’s
hour appeared to have come. A fierce re-
bellion against inhumanity surged through
their elevated hearts, and every face was a picture
up there —a picture of pity! But, just then,
a little child coughed, somewhere away in a back
corner, and in a moment the gallery's noble feel-
ings fled. It turned on the mother like a savage,
and made fierce faces at her until she crept out
into the passage, where she probably relieved her
feelings by slapping the poor little cause of the
tender-hearted gallery’s severity.
It seems a waste of good material to make a
boy of Miss Fitzmaurioe Gill, who can play a
woman so delightfully. Her length of limb
evidently preys upon her mind ; she curls herself
together shrinkinulv wheuever a chance offers.
In her little death-scene, her manner of flop-
ping out of life is an excellent variation on the
average stage-death of linked horrors long drawn
out. Miss Watson works hard for her living
all through the piece, but her mouthy method
gets on the nerves after her first speech, and re-
mains there till gunpowder blows everything out
in the final act. In the proposal scene, this lady,
naturally enough, wears a gown of shimmering
gold. And, naturally enough, she wears nothing
but white in the wedding scene, even though it is
at sea, where you can’t get your clothes washed.
And, naturally, a dull, dark brown is donned when
grief is beyond words. These things have to be.
A little song of Sydney lloben’s is sung by an
unseen singer during the scene of the big, bour-
geois party, and MLs Coutts Duvalli touches her
harp gracefully in an ante-room. Bland Holt has
an excellent feeling for musical effects, and in his
last play he engaged four professional banjoists
for one little turn.
Which is vainer, the very vainest man or the
very vainest woman? Time was when vanity was a
purely feminine word, m-aningless if dissociated
from frills and bonnets, feathers and froufrous.
“ A vain man ” was an expression that no one
would dare to air in public life. But, la-la-la,
what do we hear this century ! A brave and
m inly naval man now brands himself complacently
with a little book of all the newspaper-cuttings
that have mentioned his name. He endeavors to
turn the serious side of the matter away by airily
remarking, as he hands the book to lady friends,
“My little Conceit Book, don’t you know.”
Within the covers innumerable small slips inform
you that Mr. So-and-So played the dashing lover
in Mrs. So-and-So’s private theatricals, and waxed
his moustache with a beautifully-scented pomade.
That the same being sang “ True Till Death ” with
a touch of loving pathos at Mrs. Otherwoman's
at-home. Or even such bald statements as
that the gentleman in question attended a vice-
regal hop last night, or “was present among
others ”at the latest society gambol. Now, do
you think the vainest woman in the world could
be as deliciously artless as that ? Anyhow, I feel
sure that she would have left out that “ was-pre-
sent-among-others ” remark. And if you think
that to leave that out would make for the greater
vanity on the woman’s side, let ine tell you that to
leave it in is a sublimity of self-satisfaction that
even a stone-blind woman could not achieve.
How’s that for the other side ?
Still, to appear to think a certain amount of
oneself is a necessary protector in this fitful fever
stage. The pretty wife of an elevated legal per-
sonage, who dresses in the simple frocks of a little
schoolgirl, was rubbed the wrong way the other
day by no less a person than a big Irish police-
man. He tried to stop her from entering one of
the courts, and was gruff beyond the bounds of
policy or policemen. “ But you don't know who
I am,” she said. And she told him. And he
laughed—laughed ironically. And if a friend had
not turned up just then she might have been wait-
ing admission still. When she had gone in, the
policeman turned to the friend and winked. “Do
you know who she said she was, sir? She said
she was Mrs. Top Rowe. I’ve heard that tale
before. But you can't have me” And he doesn’t
believe her yet. That's the worst of having con-
victions.
This month the streets are full of violets, full of
jonquils. But the little blue flower you buy for
threepence a bunch is as unlike the violet that
darts its arrowy odors through the brain till you
could faint with the delicious pain, as a turnip is
from a tuberose. Its only scent is of water, a
clean aroma certainly, but profoundly annoying
to some people, and never the scent one yearns for
in a flower. At the same time, anv one of any
class whatevah is heavily redolent of violet per-
fume at so much a bottle, and it’s possible that
the bottled perfume is chasing the natural one out
of the grasp of the senses. Down in Hobart this
flower is just reviving its popularity. Sometime
ago a murderer maliciously wore a big bunch in
his buttonhole, and carried another in his hand as
lie stepped on to the scaffold. The flower was
immediately as dead to the world as the man him-
self. For years Fashion would have nothing to
do with it. If Butler or someone of that sort
would carry a bunch of magnolias—the sickliest
flower for scent that blows—there would be virtue
iu his last act.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Spectacularly speaking, the Old Colonists’
Carnival was hotch-potch in patches. Watteau
flovver nymphs, Puritan girls, Salonite parlor-
maids, for the most part, adhered loyally to pre-
scribed inodes. But colors clashed, and stripes
ran mad on the star-spangled raiment of Buffalo
fals from Fitzroy, while, not far off, passe
luropean shoes put the com ether on the sequined
splendor of a flaxen-haired Zingari. And the jum-
ble of styles made onlookers blink when ‘Bohe-
mians ’ posed for a group-portrait in front of their
Oriental bazaar. Damsels in inartistic street-suits
and Indian slippers, or gorgeous ball-gowns and
coronets, rubbed shoulders with colored gentlemen
(genuine) in smug broadcloth and gold-embossed
caps. However, hurly-burly or not, that Eastern
depot excited outside feminine interest, thanks to
its brunette male contingent. Towards the end,
when tired vendors ceased from touting, neglected
she-customers gazed enviously into the shaded
booth where fatigued syrens basked recreatively
under their colleagues’ black Sphinx-like glances.
“ The cosiest corner of the * Carnival ’ was the
Gas Co.’s snuggery, warmed by such caloric con-
trivances as asbestos ‘ Yule-logs ’ glowing ruddily
on old fashioned ‘ dogs.’ Maybe, though, these
realistic brands, like the miraculous bush seen by
Moses (always burning, but never consumed),
might grow monotonous on further acquaintance.
lie the Carnival cookery displays : the pies and
sich turned out by a pretty young deinon-
stratrix seemed ship-shape enough and probably
tasted orthodox, but (considering all the pastry-
cutters and similar conveniences on hand) lacked
finish and coquettishness. Certain patties, too
(mushroom, most likely), with a soupjon of dark
ooziness pervading their seams and a singed freckle
or two on their upper crust, fell short of ehic-ness.
Then the eclat of the poultry was lessened by
their superabundance of long, ungirded leg. Per-
haps, when cold, they would be shorn of this shin-
superfluity and put into frilled paper pants—
though, when the public took its last long look at
the end of the display, they were still exposing
their spindle-shanks in a baking-dish.
“The Carnival has caused much wrath and heart-
burning in fashionable circles. There have been
quarrels, slanders, much calling of names, and even
nasty insinuations about where some of the receipts
went to. The esclandre is specially unpleasant for
those girls who gave their services with a laudable
desire of aiding a charity of the best kind, and
who now find their names bandied about with
those of the female ‘ push ’ against whom
the insinuations are made. The nasty remarks
flung around by a wealthy magnate in particular
rankle in many a virtuous bosom, and a general
shriek ariseth from the maidenhood of Toorak
and St. Kilda: * Never again, my dear—never
again ! ’
“ There were female jarrings indeed from the
very beginning of said Carnival ; bickerings almost
every day while it lasted, and a thundering big
row to" finish up with. An official endeavour is
being made to hush up the whole business,
especially in view of the financial success of the
undertaking, but it won’t work. There is not a
tea-drinking, character-worrying drawing-room in
Melbourne that is not agog with rumors—largely
malicious, probably ; but the feminine mind is
particularly resolved to believe that there is
fire where there’s smoke. It is openly stated that
someladies make a small pocket-money industry of
‘assisting ’ at bazaars and collecting for charities ;
and startling details are given. All of which, of
course, is delightful for the women who assist at
such functions with genuine desire to do a little
bit of good.
“By one of the grim ironies of Life the late
Baronet Clarke’s kindly widow was smilingly
making, at Melb. lown Hall, her first appearance
since her husband’s death, at the very moment
when her brother Kenneth Snodgrass, after shoot-
ing a lady whom he presumably loved to distrac-
tion, was ending his earthly perplexities with
a revolver at Coolgardie. Lady Clarke is,
during Lady Brassey’s absence, Acting-President
of the Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to
Children. A combination of her own interest
with the wishes of her friends induced her to
depart from her usual course, only to retire the
next day into deeper seclusion. It was not alone
the shock of the episode, but the feeling that her
effort had been in vain to keep family trouble
from public discussion. It is generally known
that she has tried to play guardian angel to her
family ever since her marriage; and everybody
equally well knows what a Herculean task it is to
make the inherently crooked go straight. The
most charitable explanation of Nurse Gold’s
murder and Snodgrass’s suicide is that he was
suddenly overcome by the mental malady that has
kept another member of his family so long an
inmate of a lunatic asylum.
“Like the dove out of the Ark, finding no-
where rest for the sole of his foot, Prof. Tucker,
with a MS. roll in his hand, migrated from floor
to stair, and from stair to floor again, at Melb.
Athenaeum, on the memorable night when the
mob of Federationists, finding the Town Hall
wouldn’t hold them, rushed his lecture-room, and
drove him with rude laughter from the platform.
But his feelings were not outraged or his nerves
disturbed as much as sympathetic reporters have
described. He was upheld by a subsidiary elation
that did not communicate itself to his audience —
waste of time was a small price to pay for the
dramatic fitness of the episode. For once in a
way he lost his identity; he was no longer the
classical lecturer in evening dress and eyeglasses ;
he was, for one brief half-hour, the unpopular
actor driven from the stage of that old Dionysian
theatre at Athens by the laughter and taunts of
the manyheaded Demos. And, in order that none
should miss the point, it was artistically woven
into the lecture. The real trouble came in with
the loss of more than half the audience, who
haying fled discomfited into the streets could
neither be cajoled back by Chairman Wrixon nor
rounded-in by under-Master of Ceremonies Leeper,
though he beat up the highways and byway® for
the lost seekers after classical information.
“ Florence Schmidt got back to her native
Rockhampton as the guest of Premier Byrnes in
the Govt, steam yacht, with her host’s sister and
a Parliamentary party. Returning thanks for his
welcome, the Govt, chief praised the tuneful
Rockhamptoness sky-high, saying that if the lyric
stage had not tempted her, Miss Schmidt would
have had a fine opening in Bananaland politics.
In addition, he stated that all along the coast
theirs had been a joint triumph, and at Cairns the
dual toast had been—‘The Premier in Politics
and the Premier in Song.’ At this stage Rock-
hampton's solitary Frenchman interrupted with
‘ Place aux dames,’ but in the absence of an inter*
preter few saw the point. Later on the unmarried
Mayor fluted so rapturously of ‘ Queensland’s
prima-donna,’ that interjectors prescribed for the
enthusiast a douche bath of matrimony.
“ Solicitor Bertram Lilley, fourth son of Banana-
land’s late C. J., is to join in the bonds with the
prettiest girl in Q. Central Division. The youth-
ful bride-elect is one of a family of sisters whose
faces have literally been their fortunes, .and whose
late aristocratic father and handsome mother had
their own romance in days gone by.
“ It is said that rich-man Crotty’s ill-used widow
intends contesting his will. Those who knew Crotty
best also know what a hopeless waste of money
such action would involve. When remonstrated with
by a friend who was present when the rough copy
was read over to Crotty, the latter shut the re-
monstrator up by asking: ‘ls this your will or
mine that’s being read? If it’s mine, kindly hold
your tongue. If it’s yours, I’ll hold mine!’
“That lucky priest at Kew, who benefits so
largely by the Crotty will, is a typical Paddy.
A kindly soul, ten times as watchful of his flock
as the average shepherd. Even the juvenile
stragglers, lagging on their way to morning cate-
chism, get their share of pastoral attention.
‘ Hurry up, Ambrose,’shouts his Reverence, ‘or
I'll be after seeing your father about yez. Nora,
aren’t ye ashamed to keep the Reverend Mother
waiting ? ’

The chief man of a Gulf town was lately
locked up for drunkenness and “screeching.” His
friends excuse him on the plea that “Jimmy was
only practising for Parliament.”
Said the president of a N.S.W. town
Progress Association to a member who asked that
a lamp be erected near his residence: “Perhaps
if you were to take the pledge it would serve the
purpose just as well.”
Pastor-pedagogue Fitchett finds that blood-
and-bullet writing pays. He is now engaged on a
“ History of the Angio-Freneh War, from 1793 to
1815.” Pastor F. is evidently bent on “ teaching
the young idea how to shoot.”
The pi’ophetic legend of the Ticlibornes
—setting forth that after one of them had had
seven sons and another seven daughters the
family would become extinct —hasbeen fulfilled to
the extent that there now remains alive only one
of the seven daughters.
Another ex-Governor of a certain Aus-
tralian province has separated from his wife.
Several other Governors of that province had
somewhat similar experiences, which suggests that
there is a microbe ot domestic infelicity in the
local vice-regal residence.
Died lately at Lake Nyassa, Africa, Dr.
A. W. R. Fletcher, most promising son of ex-
Adelaide University Professor Roby Fletcher.
Fletcher, jun., a Victorian boy, after qualifying
as a medico, went missionarying to Central Africa,
and was a corpse within three months.

Not long ago one of Alex. Montgomery’s
Bulletin yarns re-appeared in another Australian
paper, having been supplied, as original, by a
local man, who had cribbed it from an English
weekly, which had taken it from an American
paper which professed to have received the story
from the narrator viva voce and at first-hand.

In his eve-of-battle Provincialise whoop-
bigs at Parramatta on 2nd June, G. Republican
Dibbs described himself as “ an experienced states-
man of 40 years’ standing.” The man who oalls
himself ‘ a statesman ”is as bad as the bard who
labels his verses “poems.” Dibbs isn’t the first
creature of political circumstance who mistook
himself for a creator thereof.

Sam Jackson, Frank
Long, Carroll, Strong,
the Karlsens, the Bells,

hadn’t his luck. The
first intimation he re-

Field, whose rented land
extended to within a
short distance of the
Mount. The “stuff ” was

gravel,” and Smith went

from the Government;

Paragraphed that Rowland Rees is a can-
didate for Westralian Leg. Council. Rees was
formerly one of S.A.’s “eccentric” politicians,
and, like the brilliant Parsons, marred his splendid
talents. Rees, Parsons, and Ward (the latter is
still a S. A. M.L.C.) formed a trio unequalled in
their day as political orators. Rees has now a
good architect’s practice in Perth.
At the Sydney Town Hall anti-Federation
meeting last week ex-Attorney-General Want an-
nounced that he was indebted for the use of the
hall to Mr. John Norton, who had engaged it,
but had generously and loyally given it up in anti-
Federal interests. Anti-Federation does make

John Norton butter on a lordly dish !
Gov. Hampden’s distinction between Ang-
lican and R.C. bishops, for purposes of official
address, works out this way : The Anglican Bishop,
say, of Armidale, is addressed as “ The Bishop of
Armidale ” ; the R.C. Bishop is addressed “ The
Rt. Rev. Dr. Torreggiani, Bishop of Armidale.”
And the State pays some full-grown man to think
out and perpetuate such nice distinctions !
Obit the blind son of a well-known Austra-
lian Anglican clergyman. Deceased leaves children,
whom he never saw, for he had lost hissightwhen he
married a lady, by no means penniless, who had
known him in happier days. In darkness, too, he
successfully negotiated the first examination of
his intended law-course, but he got no further
owing to general break-down of his health.
The editor of a N.S.W. country paper
being ill, the proprietor asked a visiting Melb.
pressman fora “leader” —which, when supplied,
was found to be a rattling advocacy of Federation.
“Damn it all ! ” said the paper-owner, “I’m not
a blooming Federationist; but there’s no other
‘copy,’ and this will have to go in—so I suppose
I’ll have to be one from this out! ” And he is !
A very “long arm” this time! Last
year a Gundagai (N.S.W.) man was thrown from
his horse and died from the effects. About 20
years before he and four others were riding to-
gether when one of the party was killed similarly,
and since then this man has been present at the
killing, in the same way, of his other three com-
panions—finally meeting the same fate himself 1
Although Melbourne newspaper offices
have been simply inundated with Federation

Dyson's half-column in the Argus. The rest of
the slush would have ruined any but a good cause.

Gazetted that Tozer (Q.) is to be allowed
to retain for life the distinctive title of “Honor-
able.” And, by the way, hasn’t “ a Tozer ” come
to have in Queensland a certain well-ascertained
meaning? But it works round all right. An
ambassador is said to—er —romance abroad for the
good of his country, and an Agent-General is a
kind of ambassador. The Honorable Tozer,
Agent-General, may pass.
At the eleventh hour, financial editor Nash,
of Sydney D.T., was drawn into the deep sea of
anti-Federal oratory—but the man who had
loomed large in print emerged as a very small and
flustered Samson. Not one journalist in a
hundred but courts disaster when his newspaper
shell gets too small for him—and it is unnecessary,
anyway, to face unsympathetic hundreds if one
habitually spreads himself to thousands.
Mr. F. Barrow Morley, portrayed in pre-
sent Bui.i.KTlN-issue, was born in Sydney in ’7O.
and displayed such remarkably early musical
ability that at 15 years old lie was equal to Bach’s
G. minor organ-fugue. Young Morley’s special
aptitude, however, was for the piano, with which,
under Mr. Sydney Moss’s tuition, he made such
progress that in 1894 he was able to make in
Sydney a very successful first appearance. Since
then his growth in public favor has been such as
to give every promise of a successful career.

Albert Lucas tells an anecdote of Remenyi,
who was a great artist and a great boaster,
and particularly proud of being Hungarian.
Lucas was at an hotel in Johannesburg when
the violinist came in and introduced himself
thusly : “I, Remenyi, artist, musician, Hun-
garian.” Whereupon Lucas struck an attitude,
and exclaimed : “I, Lucas, actor, artist, thirsty.’
Remenyi took the hint, and they drank.
Facts about the Coming King. Name,
Albert Victor. Nickname, Tummy. Age, 57. Wife,
1. Children, 4. Grandchildren, 5. Height, sft. 6in.
Weight, 12st. 121 b. Eyes, light-gray; beard,
gray ; complexion, brown ; head, bald. Favorite
wine, champagne of ’B9 ; favorite liqueur, 40-year
cognac. Judge—of horses, dogs, and women.
Shoots, sets the fashions. Has 13 university
degrees. Is colonel of eight regiments, spends
£lOOO a year on telegrams, and won’t have a type-
writer in his house. Owns the deepest mine in
England, and was the first Christian to dine with
the Sultan of Turkey.
Years ago, in Sydney Water
Police Court, before the then
Police-magistrate Cloete, W. B.
Dailey and Windeyer got to say-
ing hard words about each other.
“Gentlemen ! ” expostulated the
P.M. “Why the plural num-
ber?” asked Windeyer, insolently.
“ Oh, well,” said Cloete mildly,
“if you have any objection, Mr.
Windeyer, I withdraw the word,
so far as your share in it is con-
cerned. But please let me know—
in order to facilitate matters—
what I am to call as—er—a
general thing—when it is neces-
sary to refer to you, or address
you, or speak of you, or —er—
think of you?” And Dailey
smiled large, and patted his
bingey, and Windeyer’s scowl
blistered the walls of the
court.
The Isis Standard, published in the tick-
and-kanaka infected districts of Bananaland,
winds up its obituary notioe on Gladstone :
He has laid down his lance, and the great and generous
deeds performed iu a well-spent life are now garnered
with the past which holds all things. During the closing
hours of the dying chieftain’s life the weakening brain
seems to have wandered to the Sunny South, as the cable
message states he occasionally spoke some words in the
French language. He was away among the golden glories
of Sunsets of Paradise, the scarlet blaze of a wilderness of
flowers, the sweetness of songs sad as death that was so
close at hand. . . . Then all pain vanished, and his soul
swooned, and was borne Heavenward amid the deep,
dreamy hush of white moons shining on silvery seas.
The constant repetition of the Tory legend
that M.L. Premier Seddon is a rich man who has
made much bloated affluence out of politics has at
last moved Richard of Ktunara to make a statement
of his financial position. The result is that, apart
from his political screw, along life of hard work
has leftSeddon with an income of rather under £4
a week. Seddon’s predecessors, Ballance and
Atkinson, both died practically penniless. So did
ex-Premier Whitaker. Ex-Treasurer Ward is
stone-broke, and another ex-Mimster has just
his schedule: assets £L3B9; liabilities,
•£3053. A politician can’t get enough tick to
make even a decent insolvency.
Purves, Q.C., to an E. Melbourne audi-
ence “I am against the Referendum. Can any-
one point me a single instance where it has ever
done any good?” Nobody had the presence of
mind to reply, “Yes ! when the people of Vic-
toria refused to elect James Liddell Purves as a
member of the Federal Convention.” Which re-
calls that when Laborist Sleuth appeared at that
memorable meeting of Broken Hill shareholders to
set forth the men’s case, Purves aforesaid attended
for the purpose of oratorically stoushing Sleath.
“ He calls himself a workman,” said the Q.C. ;
“ why, look at his hands—they are whiter than
mine!’’ “ Yes,” Sleath might have said, “they
are cleaner.” But he didn’t. Most people think
of these tilings afterwards.
The newspaper knight in Adelaide haa
discarded the name of John, and is to be known
as “ Sir Langdon.” His title, it is claimed, came
as the result of the energy of friends on the other
side of the ocean. Premier Kingston, it is averred,
had nothing to do with the matter, though dis-
affected persons in Adelaide pretend that it is a
reward for Bony’s services as journalistic champion
of the Govt, during the fierce Hospital fight, now
decided in Kingston’s favor.
The will of late W. H. Glen, music-
seller, who died six years ago, was dragged into
court by a crowd of lawyers last week, in order
that Judge a’Beckett might explain its meaning
on certain points. Any Melbourne man who dies
rich can pretty well depend on having his last will
and testament brought to court. It is beautiful
to see the learned brethren seated in front of his
Honor listening to an exposition of the Law at the
rate of 6s. Bd. per second, or thereabouts. Eleven
counsel and six legal firms had a picnic in the Glen
case.
Died in Ballarat on 29th May, Stephen
Cumming, aged 78. He was an old mate of the
late Peter Lalor, and sheltered him when he
escaped with a wound in the shoulder from the
fight at Eureka Stockade. After Lalor had
rested a little while in Cumming’s tent he wai
taken by the latter to the house of Rev. P. Smith,
where next day his arm was amputated by
Drs. Doyle and Stewart. Old man Cumming
was much esteemed in Ballarat, and most of the
surviving pioneers followed his remains to the
grave.
When Rev. Dr. Momerie (the “Corrup-
tion of the Church ” man) was out here, a Melb.
parson asked him whether he had called on
Bishop Goe. “Dear me, no,” exclaimed the
dandified heretic, hitching up his pants and dis-
playing his scarlet silk socks, “ when I was in
America the bishops all called on me. If the
Bishop of Melbourne likes to call on me at my
hotel I shall be willing to see him.” That clergy-
man, probably having an eye upon a better
vicarate, never dared to make the suggestion to
F. F. Melbourne.

Kenneth Snodgrass, the Coolgardie widow-
slayer and suicide, once made a valiant attempt to
oust F. C. Mason from his safe billet as member
for Gippsland South (Vic.), which was held by
father Snodgrass in the very early days of the pro-
vince. The new candidate hadn’t a ghost of a
show against the old campaigner, vvho has since
pushed his way into the Speakership. Bad luck
for Snodgrass. A comfortable seat in Parliament
would have been his salvation, and even the loss
of Mason’s services to Victoria might not have
proved a much sadder event than the murder of a
nice widow in evening dress.

A venerable Cobb's coach, after long cor-
duroy-road service, fitly adorned the Vie. Old
Colonists’ Carnival. This kind of vehicle goes by
other names in America. The CoSl> is Australian,
after Freeman Cobb, who introduced the coach
about 4(1 years ago. His associates were Robert-
son, Wagner, and especially Clapp, while the
Clapp of the next generation bosses the city train-
roads as manager. The Cobbs, the Melb. ’buses,
and the trams were all kept pretty well in the
same progressive Yankee hands.
Cable : “ Samuel Plimsoll, formerly M.P.
for Derby, is seriously ill.” [He has since died.]
Footsteps on the sands of time
Mark the men of stature great;
Sammy Plimsoll. in his prime
Serving loyalb t State,
Played on cran y b ig and barque
Just a small, in.mortal Mark.
Every merchant ship that sa : ls
Bearing Britain's b mting brave
Calls to mind the shameful tales
Of sailors sent to find a jrrave
Peep in ocean caverns dark.
Ere the birth of Plimsoll's Mark.
Hated by the wealthy few,
Callous in their creed for gold,
Every stormy wind that blew
Cursed him in the days of old.
Stormy wind and hungry shark
Cursed the sight of Plimsoll’s Mark.
Now, when weak and sick he lies,
Onetime sturdy Seamen's Friend,
Memories of his work arise
Glorifying Plimsoll’s end.

Aboriginalities.
K.: “Met an Australian to-day and asked
him the road to Tuppal. ‘Well, pard, can’t
’xactly direct; yer from yere. Better inquire at
the next ranch. Guess they'll be able to put yer
on the darned trail ! ’ said he. Which shows
what a singular hold the cheap Yankee dread-
ful ’is getting upon the gumleaf bloke. The boy
who shot the man at Mungadale ; the man who
lassooed the ‘galoot’ at Deniliquin; and the
crank who presented two revolvers at a citizen on
Narandera bridge and remarked that he * had the
drop on him,’ were all consistent followers of
Frank Reade, of ‘ The Land of Crimson Snow or
the Pirates of Sandy Hook.’ Common thing now
in the back country to meet men under different
degrees of the Dead wood influence, carrying the
indispensable volume in the billy with the pups,
or reclining on Matilda by the roadside, leading
the Outlaws of Nuggetty Gulch to victory—with
no socks on ! The hawkers are doing well. Met
a man recently carrying £1 worth of Deadwoods
in a bran-bag. This was his only swag. ‘ I’m
Tarantula Tom, the great Blood-Pudding of the
Norwest. I’m a bad ’un and I mean to kill! ’ he
remarked, dropping the pack and striking a blood-
curdling attitude. What price a 3d. purely-Aus-
tralian short-story library?
* * » *
Jim went broke at the pub. and drank his
dog. Got sober, took up his swag and walked.
Some years after he came across his dog. “ He’s
forgotten you,” said the owner ; and so it seemed
until Jim shammed drunk, rolled about, and fell.
Then the dog knew it was the same old Jim, and
lay alongside of him and defied one and all to
touch him. Beautiful moral somewhere.

“ Mac.” : “ Lazarus ” is too cocksure as to
the pleasures and benefits of sleeping out in the
open. Depends very much on the locality. He
should try Woolloomdoloo Bay wharf. I did
ouoe ; and, what with broken bricks and coal, and
smears of tar, and other stuff, it wasn’t just
heavenly. And, after all, the skipper of the
cutter 1 was shepherding for a billet told me in
the morning that lie didn’t want any more hands,
and when he did he knew where to get ’em and
he said more than that, too—damn him ! Of
course, in the country one can go to sleep with his
head on a fowl and, perhaps, a couple of potatoes,
and, maybe, with his tongue reasonably damp,
and needn’t trouble whether the adjacent farmer
Wcpits him to go to work in the morning or not.
Perhaps “Lazarus ” can always get tucker when-
ever he wants it, and so can fix all his mindon the
search for a residence. But if so, he ought to call
himself Dives.

“Groffin”: A witness before Vic. Law
Commission was questioned as to whether there
was such a thing as racial or class bias amongst
Australian natives. The answer Was “No, ex-
ceptiiig in the case of Chinamen and their child-
ren.” The witness, however, could never have
lived in a community where there was a mixture
of Irish-Australians and Cornish-Australians. If
you want to specially insult the former, call him
a “ Cousin Jack,” and, if the latter, coolly observe
“ Get out; you’re Irish ! ” I was one day riding
in a cab from Mt. Pleasant to Ballarat. r I he
legal fare is 2d., but anyone who doesn’t want to
be condemned as a miser always pays 3d. The
cabman was the son of an Irishman, and a China-
man, who was amongst the passengers, alighted
and tendered two coppers. The driver exhausted
his stock of Billingsgate, and, when it was all
used up and there wasn’t another curse in him,
scathingly added, “ Well, you’re a dashed Cousin
Jack.” He could say nothing worse.

f'Miell ; My friend from Gippsland
bad a cockatoo once. It was a most gifted
linguist, and could talk policies in five languages,
The bird was very fond of its kind master at first,
but, as its intellectual powers and its worldly ex-
perience increased, a coolness arose between them,
'ln fact the Gippslander was not class enough—-he
Miss PATTIE BROWNE, up to date.
Photo., Falk Studios , Melbourne.
lacked interesting matter and command of
language such as that bird demanded of a bosom
friend and confidant, and, as was to be expected,
the cockatoo went off one day in search of more
congenial society. Says the man from Gippsland
East : “ He was missin’ for about 14 months,
when one day while I was paddin’ it from Bruthen
t’ Sale, I heard a awful clatter in a ole white gum,
an’ dropped bluey to make enquiries. On a limb
I sees my old cookie perched, an’ lookin’ as old-
fashioned as a bloomin’ Judge. He had about
fifty million other cockatoos ranged around, on the
other branches in classes, an’ he was layin’ down
the blessed law t’ them like all what’s this. What
doin’? He was keepin’ school, teachin’ all the
other blanky cockatoos in the blanky country the
blanky English langwidge—that’s what he was
doin’.”
C.H.R. ; “Anybody wishing to be sea-
soned to Klondyke weather should try a winter
on the mid - tableland of Tasmania. The
present writer and a mate were fossicking
on the spurs of the Cradle Mountain
(5100ft.) one August. An afternoon
Bleet-storm came along rendering it
necessary to camp in a partiou-
larly cheerless spot just above
the timber line. Wood being
scarce, we were forced to turn
into damp rugs, and in the
morning my mate sang out
to know who had been pil
ing timber on to us (we
were in a field - bed).
Everything was frozen
stiff, including ourselves.
After violent exertion we
managed to crawl out.
Our shirts stood out like
sheets of bark, and we
had to prop our pants up
against rocks and lower
ourselves into ’em. Then
we overturned the tent and
upended the bedding, and
stood ’em up to thaw. An
old liutkeeper, who has resided
around the highlands for many
years, told me that his hat once
froze to his head and his old
woman only removed it by ap-
plying damper poultices every
two hours; and when it did
come it left his head as bare
as the palm of your hand. How-
ever, he was a bit of a liar.
The ‘ Big Freeze,’ some time in
the Forties, in the Lake Coun-
try, killed every pine for miles
and froze the entire surface of
the great lake 9in. thick. The re-
ports from the timber sounded like
the Battle of Waterloo, and thou-
sands of ducks and other birds died
on their perches. If the provinces want
re-naming after June 3, I propose the
Little Island’s name be changed to Klondob-
sondyke. ”
* * * *
It is told that when the first white man
who trod the soap-stone paths of Levuka, the
ancient capital of Fiji, struck a vesta on his
trousers, the whole of the scantily-clad population
followed in his wake for hours, repeating aloud to
each other, “Lo and behold the mighty white
man, who can bring forth fire from his trousers ! ”
* * * *
George : Met a woman-swaggie a few,
weeks ago camped outside a small western town.
Reckoned she was making for the city, where her
“ old man ” was supposed to be, and, if I “ didn’t
mind,” my company on the track would be very
acceptable. “I’ll find the tucker,” she said.
“ The blessed cookies always fork out to my little
yarn. You needn’t appear on the scene at all ;
or, if you do, remember you’re in the last stage of
consumption, and I’m your sister taking you to a
hospital in town. Weakly I assented, and after
many droll adventures reached the city, where I
had the devil’s own trouble to get rid of her. And
now that T am affluent and, of course, respectable,
I’m in deadly fear of running up against her and
being claimed as her long lost brother.
Ex-Queenslander Rawson
is giving some wonderful exhibi-
tions of boomerang throwing
at the Palace Theatre, London.
Alleged that Rawson can make
a boomerang describe three cir-
cles and return to his hand after
completing the third. Rawson
might return and give a. few
lessons to the aboriginals. There
are lots of districts where the
hapless remnant has almost for-
gotten what a boomerang is.

Bush Editor : Sample of
letters your brothers in the bush
have to bear up against when
they enter their offices on wash-
ing-morning (Monday):—
Dear Sir,—l threw into your letter-
box yesterday 7 pages of M.S. If
you publish them see to the autho-
graphy. That “y” in “said.” The
“s” in “prizes,” and “oar” for
“ o’er,” &e. When my mind is en-
gaged on any subject, spelling and
punctuations are of a second con-
sideration.

* ... *
On one western N.S.W. squattage, Pdola-
macea, the owner has more than average re-
gard for the aborigines’ well-being —besides, they
■come ill handy at shearing-time and for rabbiting.
Being shocked at the awful discomforts of
the “ camp,” made mostly of rotten bags and
forked sticks, lie built a big circular galvanised-
iron barn, with provision for a central fife. Then
he invited the “tribe” (a mixed lot, but mostly
deformed and diseased) to suggest improvements.
One or two things were done to the building as a
result ; and the “ tribe,” in great joy, trooped in.
In a week the first sneak-out occurred ; after that
THE SPREAD OF CIVILISATION.
VISITOR : “ You litre him cigarette, Billy?”
KING BILLY : “ Well, I dunno. What brand, ole
man?”
the disreputable old collection of bones and rags
and brawling things was gradually resorted to by
all the rest, the last to leave being the half-breeds.

Dear Bulletin. tie kangaroos using their
tails in jumping. A couple of years ago we got
an old-man in our paddock, which had a high,
barb-topped fence. Knew he couldn’t jump it, so
we pressed him into a corner. But when lie got
near the fence he jumped on to a stump some five
yards off, and from that cleared the fence easily.
Evidently an old game of his; but his tail could
not have been of the slightest service in jumping
from tho stump, which was about three feet luge.
-F. R.
THE M‘CAMLEY MIXTURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jaok M'Cainley,
Lank and long,
Ox-persuader,
Billabong.
Bluff and hearty
Sort o’ party,
Got the “ blanky ” habit strong !
Says the parson,
Bright old bird,
“ Whv’d you use that
Horrid word ?
(Jack looked grinful)—
Not say sinful,
But most vulgar and absurd ! ”
“ It’s the blanky
Church, betwixt
You and me, that
Got me fixed ! ”
Says M'Cainley,
“ In our fam’ly
Things is all so blanky mixed !
“ There’s me father —
Whoa back, Dick 1 —
Church o’ Blanky
England, stric !
There’s me mother
And one brother,
Roman —Blanky—Catholic !
“ But me sister —
Way, you Stan !
Don’t them bullocks
Rile a man ?
Kilts enticed her,
Went and spliced a
Presby—Blanky —terian ! ”
W. T. GOODGE.

Federalist: (1) Under the single electorate system a
man generally gets in because he is the big local store-
keeper. (2) The choice is narrowed. If two men like
lteid and Barton, who between them got 180,000 Conven-
tion election votes, stand for one electorate and two local
usurers for another, it follows that one usurer must
got in and one of the two most popular men in the
province must stay out. (3) The Fat Man can often put
up sham democratic candidates and get in by splitting
the Liberal vote. (4) The constituency system doesn’t
represent the people. N.S.W. in general was willing to
(4ve Barton 96,000 votes because he was the boss Federa-
tionist of Australia. And no single constituency wanted
Barton, because he didn’t look after the local culvert

don’t read too well and are beastly awkward things for

On the Road to Klondike.
“ C.H.M.” writes from Wrangel, Alaska, under
date April 16, ’9B : .
Editor Bulletin.—Australians are here mi good force,
and the wish expressed by one and re-echoed by all pie-
sent that there was a copy of The Bullet n to be had
suggests that perhaps you could, in the interests of in-
tending Klondikers, find a corner for reliable information
re the journey as far as Glenora, to which point I have
been The first trouble one is likely to have is choosing
the right kind of outfit, and where so many cater and
each swears that his ideas as to the needful articles are
rio-ht, it is just as well for those coming to know that
three parts of the things said to be requ l red are utterly
useless. The weather here in winter is cold—no doubt
about that; but the best clothing is heavy woollen. Let
anyone making the journey bring woollen clothing and
plenty of it, and that, with oilskin outside-wear, isi the
best winter covering for these parts. Furs of every kind
are simply useless, and skin garments are wotse still.
Provisions, of course, depend on the person choosing, but
the everlasting bacon and beans are easiest and best
to take when a journey right through is intended of
course with a variety of dried fruits and groceries. Those
three kinds comprise, principally, the old h ®-" d *
Concerning the points for outfitting there is not much to
choose, hut Victoria and Vancouver arid Seattle in
America seem the most used at present. For Australians
Victoria is the best, as it is more expensive getting to the.
other points mentioned. Of the towns of Victoria and
Vancouver one cannot say much. The streets are poor
and the buildings mediocre (except the Parliament house
at Victoria, w hich is really a pretty building), and the
whole shooting match” (favorite local phrase) is run by
Fat Man. The wharves are Fat Mans, the railways are
Fat Man’s, the telegraph wires, the land the people
mostly, and what there is left, are all Fat Man s. Toth e
average Australian this does not seem as it should be,
and comparing the two countries, Canada and Australia, as
far as the privileges enjoyed by the bulk of the popuia-
tion cro, our own land is far ahead. The Canadians
are running Klondike for all it is worth, and
the papers vie with each other in booming any-
thing and everything connected with the Yukon.
Each corporation, and there are many, generally has a
paper of its own running to advocate its interests first and
the country somewhere (generally a long way) after. The
various routes into the goldfields also have many and
varied advocates, but the route via Wrangel and Stickeen
River (for summer months) is the best, and Skagway for
winter travelling. There is an all-water route during
summer via St. Michael’s and the Yukon—but that is too
expensive. There is no doubt of gold being found over a
very large area, as the auriferous nature of the country is
very apparent, and all the old Australian miners I have
met are pleased with the look of the place ; hut it is a
country that will have to be enormously rich to pay the
miner, as the disabilities incidental to climatic conditions
and bad government are such that ordinary returns will
be no good. The rush is enormous—and still they come.
This place (Wrangel). three months ago was a small,
scattered hamlet; to-day it is a young city. Three-
storied hotels, replete with every modern comfort, stores,
warehouses, coal-yards, theatre, and everything else that
go to make an up-to-date town are here, and, owing to
the rush, all doing a thriving business—except, perhaps,
the restaurants, which are even more numerous here than
in Sydney and Melbourne put together. The weather is
terribly watery. Provisions are fairly cheap ; but busi-
ness of every kind is overdone. To those “over there
who like a spice of variety and have a few hundred
pounds to gamble, this country at present offers induce-
ments, but the ordinary working-man, with less amount
of capital, would be a fool to even dream of coining.
The worker of every grade is far better off in Australia.
Better wages, shorter hours, and better treatment.
A rather successful Eng-
lish marine painter of former
days is now in an Australian
city teaching the local society
girls. But his undisguised con-
tempt for his present locale has
somewhat handicapped him.
Some time age he was asked by
a local dignitary to prepare
rough studies of a subject-pic-
ture of the Mayor, a crockery-
shopkeeper, for presentation to
the city art-gallery. He did so,
investing his Worship with all
the dignity and circumstance of
office ; but in an idle moment he
also thoughtlessly hit off a per-
fect likeness of his Nibs, bal-
anced, circus-fashion, with one
foot on the seat of his bicycle
and juggling with two most pro-
saic articles of crockery ware.
By mistake, the caricature got
mixed up with the other studies,
and was sent to the patron.
The poor painter didn’t get the
order for that picture.

Fergus the beauty-championship

colleagues with regard to the

along the whole Ministerial
bench, added—he was a great
stickler for the proper Latin
plural was Dargaville—“ These
Polonii! ” This brought “Jack”
Kerr- to whom the allusion was
incomprehensible —to his gouty
feet with a point of order—
“ May I ast, Mr. Speaker,”
he croaked if me annerable

of the Government as polonies? ”

“ Wooster’’-sauce
Lee, recently deceased
worth .£1,700,000, might
have cut-up for three
times as much but for the
fact that, as there could
be no exclusive legal pro-
prietorship in the un-
coined appellation “Wor-
cestershire,” the well-
deserved vogue of the ori-
ginal condiment brought
out a host of “ Worcester-
shire ” sauces, nearly all
“prepared from the recipe
of a nobleman in the
county,” and mostly imi-
tating the famous trade-
mark as closely as they
dared. So much money,
in fact, was thus unjustly
diverted from the pocket
of the proprietor of the
original concoction, that
Th e Bulletin has never
been able to see any of
these pseudo- 11 Woosters
passed around without a
sense of almost personal
injury, and feels now inclined
—millionaire though Lea was
—to drop upon his grave a
gustatory tear. Apropos, there
is a legend setting forth how,
when a newly-engaged French
cook at a N.Q. hostelry wanted
to begin with a convincing
taste of his quality, he threw
the whole resources of his art
into the manufacture of a
cunningly devised soup, and
planted himself, when dinner
arrived, where he would be
able to observe the expected
surprise and delight. What
was his horror, then, to see
every hand in turn reached
out for the “Wooster,” a
liberal supply of which was
discharged into every plate
before even an experimental
sip had been taken at the
sacred liquid. Tearing his
hair, the outraged artist
rushed frantically from the room, and shot or
poisoned himself.
An Australian at Winnipeg, Manitoba,
sends this comfortable picture of the Durward
Lelys—man and wife. They are well wrapped up
against the season’s influence, and are said to be
also absolutely wrapped up in one another. The
happy pair, leaving their bairns at home,
have come to sing “the auld Scots sangs to
mixed audiences at the antipodes, not, of course,
without an eye to the bawbees. The man from
Arbroath found himself on his native heath at
Dunedin and in the wilds of Otago ; but when he
got among the Englishry of Wellington and Auck-
land, Adelina Patti’s tenor was equal to the occa-
sion,and the ancient Doric only cropped up ocea
sionally in the programmes. The Carl Kosa-
D’Oyley Carte singer is a robust tenor ; and the
Spanish-American trouble, with the talk about an
Anglo-Yankee alliance, gave him a splendid
chance for warbling the “ Death of Nelson,
“Tom Bowling,” and the “Star-spangled
Banner ” amid wild outbursts of jingoism. Shep-
herded byR. S. Smythe, who scores once more
the Lelys are on their way from Maoriland, and
will make their first appearance in Australia in
Sydney Centenary Hal), on Monday next. Lily
Fusch the girl violinist from Berlin, who is several
years younger, and, it is said, a few degrees belt, r
than Bessie Doyle, is on board the same steam .
and will probably perform at the Lely ent«rt
ments.

MELBOURNIANA.


Melbourne Items.
Mr. A. Johansen, of the well-known firm ot
Johansen, Castwood & Co., Bookbinders,
Paper Rulers Account Book and Manufacturing
Stationers, 303 Lonsdale-street, recommends War-
ner’s Safe Cure. In a letter dated January 29th,
1898, he says “Ten years ago I suffered from
pain in my back to such an extent I could not
stand to do my work, and had sometimes to be
taken home in a cab. I took Warner’s Safe Cure
—twelve bottles in all—and although I did not
keep to the prescribed diet, I have not had pain
in my back since. I consider Warner’s Safe Cure
cured me, and I have since recommended it to all
I know.”
Mr. Charles A. Jackson, 51 Toorak Road,
South Yarra, in a letter dated January 10th, 1898,
says:—“X feel myself in duty bound to express
my heartfelt thanks for the benefit I received
from the use of Warner’s Safe Cure. For about
four years I had been an intense sufferer from
severe headaches and pains across the loins—more
particularly my back. I was recommended by a
friend to give Warner’s Safe Cure a trial, with
the result that after taking three bottles X was
completely restored to my former good health.
Therefore I can, and always will, recommend
Warner’s Safe Cure to other sufferers.” *

At Poverty Point.
When a certain beauteous actress was starring in the
English provinces, an enterprising tailor, who rented a
s top adjoining a certain theatre, erected a few seats on
the roof and charged his friends a crown a head for a,
brief glimpse into her dressing-room. Two bricks picked l
out from under the ventilator revealed the flame of
princes getting into fresh harness after each act. One
night a full-blooded pup-fancier gave the show away : he
was crouching near the windy aperture, his hair stiffen-
ing, his face agog, enjoying his “ look in,” when sud-
denly he drew back and said, “Oh my ——!” The
strong men waiting under the lee of the chimney attri-
buted his emotion to the large wen they had observed on,
the lady’s left knee. He was severely reprimanded for-
making a noise. Four minutes later nine earnest supers..
appeared on the farthest waterspout, It was a pretty
m6Re, and the people in the street below wondered
where the flying slates and hats were coming from.
Alba D. : Years ago an English journalist played it low
■ n an Australian bard. The bard was living peacefully
in his slab and bark hut until the E. j. came limping down
ihe bush-track and asked for a cup of water. Thereat
I hey became friends. At night, they smoked under the
adjacent gum and swapped phrases about glinting billa-
■ bongs and, shimmering mirages. The E.j. found the
bard's damper palatable and the life full of ease ;- also
the innocent bard never pestered him for board-money
So he stayed on, and, by way of payment, sketched out.
the scenario and plot of a soul-grappling play, which he
presented to the bard, telling him to stick in a bit of
dialogue, as there was a fortune in it, &c. The bard read
it dubiously, but after the first few pages he became con-
vinced that he was harboring’a genius who could sit down
casually and vomit masterpieces. The E.j. stayed on, while
the bard grubbed and shore, and sweated in the interim
over the unfinished drama that was to shake the world.
At the end of a year the bard came to Sydney with his
precious MSS., and fell into my arms outside the Lyceum.
He was waiting for the manager. YVe had a drink, and
he explained how a poor but brilliant Englishman had
given him the backbone of a fine drama. He permitted
me to glance at the MSS., and, while turning over the
sheets, related how he had worked at it night after night
until the candle-light had left him half blind. It was a
good play. I read how YVilfred Denver thought he shot
Ware, and called on somebody to put back the clock of
the world ; and when I reached the Spider and old Jaikes,
I dropped the MSS. on the counter and shouted another
drink. I hadn’t the heart to tell him he had written the
“ Silver King ” with poe*” variati-ns. It we a ghastly
trick to play on a bard
It is well known to everybody, oar the pubno, that the
heroine hates the hero like rats. He is adored by the
ladies of the workshops and the medium classes, and she
has earned a respect that is almost reverence by main-
taining a nun-like attitude through several emotional
dramas. W T hen she leans up against the hero’s brisket
and breathes sedate rapture to his Adam’s apple, and he
holds her tenderly and sadly responds, everybody says,
“ How- nice—how inexpressibly touching ! ” But it does
not occur to the keen admirers that she is a “dead
mark” to him once they step behind the wings, and on
the street she passes him in the wake of an arctic depres-
sion that sends cold chills down his spine. If his name
is mentioned to her, she says, with the expression of a
woman who has just trodden on a frog: “Mr. Blank?
Mr. Blank ? I do not know the man ! ”
Viator : “ Have seen G. Rignold in Sydney, Melbourne
and Brisbane, and on each occasion something upset
the gravity of the audience just when a ‘ bit o’ fat ’
was being sliced off. In Sydney the coarse joke of a
gallery-boy broke the spell in the third act of ‘ Called
Back.’ In Melbourne a nervous limelight-man sent a
drunken disc of light wobbling up and down the o.p.
side, leaving George in gloom ; and recently in Brisbane
in ‘ Lights of London,’ when Bess Marks invites the
escaped hero to warm himself at the fire, a ‘property ’
hand came boldly down the stage-chimney, struck a
match audibly, arid lit the gas behind the asbestos stove.
No wonder G. Rex kicks the stage-furniture a bit viciously
at times.”
Kati-Kati: “Three hours in the pit, on a warm, wet
Saturday night. Eight p.m. —Steam from hundreds of
■damp, dirty suits of clothes. By 9 p.m. is added the odor
■of hundreds of perspiring bodies. By 10 p.m.—Smells of
oranges, peanuts, cabbage-smoke, paregoric, peppermint,
whisky, and much-breathed air are thrown in, and the
atmosphere is the Irish-stew of smells. By 11 p.m.—
Everybody sweltering in the heat; there have been more
•drinks, and no more fresh air, and the atmosphere is the
lerman-sausage of odors. The Bible knew what it was
il .liking about when it referred to the horrors of the Pit.”

Ed. Bulletin.— This being a country where the saint
is presumed to have no privileges beyond those held by
the common sinner, why do Sydney transit-officers and
police compel licensed vehicles to pass churches at walk-
ing-pace when services are in progress? With much
better reason could the rule be applied to concerts and
similar performances. During first-class musical enter-
tainments at the Town Hall many items are absolutely
spoilt by the spasmodic whirr of vehicular traffic outside,
whereas a passing fire-engine would, as a rule, afford wel-
come relief from the leaden monotony of proceedings in
Bishop Smith’s glory-house next door. —T.D.
Justin M‘Carthy, in his “ History of Our Own Times,”
either didn’t know, or didn’t wish to remember, the name
of the musical founder of the original Jingo Party in
Hingland. Says M'Carthy—“ Some Tyrtseus of the tap-
tub, some Korner of the music-halls, had composed a
ballad which was sung at one of those caves of harmony
every night. The refrain of this war-song contained the
spirit-stirring words —
‘ We don’t want to fight; but, by Jingo ! if we do,
We’ve got the men, we’ve got the ships, we’ve got the
money, too.’ ”
Is it possible that a M’Carthy has never heard that
glorious effusion associated with the personality of his
compatriot, Macdermott—the “ Great Macdermott ” —the
full-1 looded Irishman who drew his £2O a week (a fancy
price in ’7B) at the London Pavilion, and smaller sums at
three other halls, for the fervor with which he assured
his pasty-cheeked audiences that “the Russians shall not
have Constanti-no-ple ”? Never heard of the Mac. who
sang the Jingo song into popularity by sheer magnetic
brute force ? And this is Fame !
Says London To-Day, re Robert Buchanan’s protest
against prudish dramatic censorship: “It does not
matter to the smallest extent whether the Licensor of
Plays admits or rejects a problem play. His rejection
gives the writer a certain amount of advt. that the per-
formance of the play would never bring. Put a literary
play upon the stage, a piece remarkable for its fine lan
guage, and almost the only people to praise it are the en-
lightened, highly-educated literary men and women who
come in with free tickets.”
Nellie Farren is grand-daughter of William Farren,
one of the foremost comedians in English stage history.
He never acted anything but old men, having begun
brilliantly, in.that line, at 20 years old, with Sir Peter
Teazle. His son, Wm. Farren the second, uncle of
Nellie, replicated the performance, and there is Wm.
Farren the third in exactly the same line. He visited
Australia as a humble member of Garner's co.
Nellie Farren’s benefit, after paying all her debts, wi I
leave her with a safe income from capital of U 7 a week.
So the Rothschild guarantee of £5 a week for the invalid
will not be called upon.
“More than mild surprise was felt,” writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent, “at the published about-to-
be marriage of Ethel Hi.ydon to George Robey, of the
music halls. Robey, during the past year or so, has come
right to the front as an eccentric comique, and, barring
Dan Leno, is at present the most popular artist in
London. The usual margarine-headed public who have
never seen the man excepting on the stage in shabby,
much-rent, overgrown clothes never doubted that G. ft.
went about thus clad always. It is some of this section
of the public who, seeing Anson as Eccles in ‘Caste,
asserted that such a low-class drunkard should not. be
allowed on the Bijou stage ! Robey is, off his beat., a
quiet, gentlemanly fellow with a university education,
who is, fortunately or otherwise, irresistibly and cleverly
funny in anything but the kid-glove and enamelled shirt
line of comic fooling.”
Geo. Robey (Ethel Haydon’s fiance), shortly due in Aus-
tralia, is not, The Bulletin is informed, a ’barrister, as
reported. He is a civil engineer, who was educated in
Germany, took his degree in London, and made bis first
music-hall appearance in ’94.
Wilson Barrett, as is well-known, made bis first big hit
with “ The Silver King,” by Jones and Hermann. In bis
preliminary advts. W.B. inserted this ver-e'.of Tennyson :
I hold it. truth with him who sings
To one clear harp in divers tones
That men may rise on stepping-stones
Of their dead selves to higher things.
This W. S. Gilbert brightly parodied as follows :
I hold it spoof with other things
That men should sing in Barrett-tones
And raise themselves by aid of Jones
From starving “ potes ” to Silver Kings.
Equinox : “ Has anyone noticed how frequently Wil-
son Barrett’s voice is an exact reproduction of that of
veteran Edwin Kelly ? ”
Things looked at first very blue for the Broughs at
Shanghai, The Bulletin learns; and, though an effort
was made by the co’s well-wishers to make things “ hum ”
a little more audibly, business contrasted very unfavorably
with that done at Hong Kong. Races and other local
counter-attractions were partly responsible for the low-
theatrical temperature.
Cockaigne : “ Demise—French, the London theatrical
bookseller. He began as Lacy’s agent in N’York, then
bought out Lacy’s London concern. French developed
into an immensely rich old bachelor. He had a circle
of intense theatrical cronies, ladies and gentlemen, who
frequented his cosy table and had pros and cons among
themselves as to where the pile would go. French
settled it by marrying a delightful young lady, and the
poor pros, pirouetted elsewhere. ”
Wallace Brownlow, still hotel-keeping in the West, now
consents to sing at local charity and benefit concerts.
Siarred, of course. And business keeps brisk.

that reporter to make Maud Jeffries speak on her arrival
in Sydney. Indeed, at the time, the sweet Maud hinted
that her hesitancy was due to the boss s possible dis-
pleasure. However, when it was certain that, the boss
would be pleased, how the eo. did bay, and what a beauti-
fully unanimous howl it was !

“ Ready for the closing scene !”
Touch the bell ! Yes ! seventeen 1
Super-master, make ’em hurry 1
See each one is dressed correctly :
Curtain’s going up directly—
What’s the meaning of this flurry?
Fairy Greatheart’s lost her winglets !
“ Anybody got my ringlets? ”
Hush ! Confound you! Aren’t you ready ?
There’s the cue. Now, couples, steady !
In you go l Laugh ! Laugh ! Great Csesar ’.
Man alive, why don’t you squeeze her?
Ha ! ha! ha ! He !he ! Ho ! ho l/.
Slap your thigh and double—so !
That’s the ticket! Be vivacious 1
Don’t speak words, but look loquacious
Here’s.the hero. Crowd about him :
Look as if you want to “ shout ” him ;
Never mind if he gets twenty
Quids to your two. Big per canty
Is deducted. YVatch his hand.
“ Happy ever after.” Cue ! Now, band !
Squeal, you fiddles ! Cornet, blast you !
Ladies ! let each partner past you.
Tableau ! Double forte chorus !
Ring the curtain down before us !
What a great success it’s been !
’Specially that closing-scene. . .
Right, Orchestra ! ’Save the Queen !

Two returning Australian tourists lately
found themselves in Aden. They had been in
Aden before, and had had a guide, and the guide
had “had ” them. Got the same man this time,
went all over the ground with him, and, on return-
ing to the ship, tendered him 2s. 6d. apiece, which
he declined with scorn. “ All the gentlemen give
me sovereign. Mr. give me sovereign and
character, and Mr. Wack Jaunt he give me
sovereign and character. You read!’ they
read, and Mr. Wack Jaunt’s testimony ran—
“To all whom it may concern.. The bearer served
me as a guide, and I can confidently recommend
him as a blanky * thief and scoundrel.”
W.D. : “‘ It is only when Oscar Wilde
goes to gaol that he realises the misery and
brutality of gaol,’ remarks the Red Page, and, as
indicating his mental attitude on this point before
angered nineteenth-century morality cast him
from his artistic hothouse into personal contact
with the workings of the brutal British penal code,
take the following from his ‘ Decay of Lying :
I know nothing sadder than the artistic career of Uhas.
Reade. He wrote one beautiful book ‘The Cloister and
the Hearth ' . . . and \\asted the rest of. his life in a
foolish attempt to be modern, to draw public attention to
the state of our convict prisons and the management of
our private lunatic asylums.”

The instinct which makes whales adhere
to their migration routes is marvellous. Near the
Bay of Islands, M.L., is a deep strait not more
than 200yds. wide between an islet and the main-
land. The whales invariably go through the
strait, instead of outside the island ; the slaughter
of hundreds of them in the strait does not deter
those that escape from using the same dangerous
passage next trip. Up to 20 years ago whales
followed an immemorial custom of visiting the
shallow waters of the Firth of Thames and strand-
ing themselves on the bar of the Thames near the
township of Shortland. Once an 80-foot whale
went ashore on the bar, and a little 20-ton steamer
put off with a scratch crew, including the present
scribe, to attempt a capture. As the monster
seemed dead a hawser was made fast to its tail,
and, with the aid of the rising tide, it was towed
off ; the crew was just beginning to reckon up
its gains when the animal suddenly roused up,
and, making straight down the gulf, took the
steamer along, stern first, at a 25-mile gait. Ihe
engineer stood by the hawser with an axe, to cut
away in case the whale should “sound.” But the
creature merely gave us a 5-mile spin, made a
graceful curve, sailed back to the bar on which it
formerly grounded, and turned on its side. The
engineer made “siccar” this time by shoving Bft.
of red-hot “clinker slice” under its fins. It
panned-out nearly =£looo for us.

6001b.—Harry Morice Canon—height, sft. lOin. ;

It seems impossible to evade competition
in the rush of modern life. A lecturer at Sydney
University, who lately proceeded to work up some
mines in a spot in the East Indies, far removed, as
he thought, from civilisation, was surprised to be
met, when almost at his destination, by a letter
beginning “This Johnnie says his name is so and
so, and he can do all your washing,” and signed
by a former pupil, another Sydney man.
When the Princess Philip of Ooburg (in
family disgrace for her penchant for a commoner),,
lately cleared suddenly out of Nice, she left
behind 1000 dresses, 200 pairs of shoes, and 50
hats and bonnets—for which things and the rest
of her belongings there was a wild scramble of
creditors after the lady’s departure. And this
within cooey of august relative Victoria K.l. l

Wanted the address of the now historical
perjurer who said that Barton was a “ lazy man.”
~^i e rece nt N.S.W. Federal vote the
side that won lost, while the side that lost won.
Copyright paradox.
Q. Premier Byrnes is so terribly “ open
to conviction ’’ that he conveys the impression that
he has not a single established opinion or preiu-
dice on any earthly subject. The incarnation of
opportunism.
Ex-Premier Nelson, although relegated to
a sinecure, evidently fancies he is still in the
swim During his recent trip up North with
uov. Lannngton, he received deputations as in
the days of old, dispensed patronage, and pro-
mised redress and reform.
As an offset to the natural cleavage of
Billites versus Anti-Billites among N.S.W. poli-
ticians, half-a-dozen energetic underground
engineers are busy creating a new Bill-party out
of the anti-Bills. Then the cry will be New
Billites versus Old Billites.
Northern Democrats are, as a rule, too
desperately in earnest to develop their humorous
faculties, but occasionally they strike a patch
Recent joke by Labor M.L.A. : “Mr. Byrnes had
only two supporters in the town (Leyburn) but
next morning my horse’s tail was cut off.”
There is no payment of M’s.P. in Wes-
tralia, and already the poverty of Coolgardie’s
member, v osper of the Mane, the nearest ap-
proach the province has to a Labor-man, is said to
be hampering him sadly. The field would do
something ; but much of the field is busy just now
dodging the bailiff and the dun. 1
On polling-day the N.S.W. Government
allowed some of the Sydney trams, the people’s
property, to be decorated with placards calling’ on
the electors to vote against the Federal Bill. If
the public official who authorised this isn’t
promptly fired out of the service the people will
want to know the reason why, and want to know
it in a pretty loud voice also.
One Vic. politician who aid a lot in the
Federal campaign to revive a fading reputation
was Alfred Deakin. Deakin lacks constructive
ability, and when the Convention was slowly
building up the Bill he appeared to little advan-
tage-as compared with Isaacs and Turner and
Iren with. But he fought for it all through the
campaign that followed with never - flagging
energy and his eloquence on many occasions
swept the opposition off its legs. Wherever there
was a hostile feeling, and a chance of having water
or harder things aimed at him by the Maloney
crowd, Deakin was willing to face the audience
and in the end he generally came off best.
Somers Vine is
prime-mover of the Eng-,
lish party which has been,
day and night, urging
the British Government
to purchase Kaiser Wil-
helm Land from Ger-
many. That would give
another 68,000 square
miles of Papua to the
exploiters.

H. Young says the ques-
tion of old-age pensions
is “ fast coming within

this be a plagiarism of Tozer’s policy ? Horace
used to trot round helping to inaugurate the little
Gawspel-shop, presiding at Salvation Army festi-
vals, and so forth. And the policy pays.
One lie N.S.W. anti-Federalists had better
drop is the rivers cry. “Our rivers” consti-
tuencies number about 11—say, Albury, Barwon
Bourke, Condobolin, Hay, Hume, Murruinbidgee'
Murray, Wentworth and Wilcantiia. These
electorates expressed their willingness to accept
the Convention’s settlement of the problem of
their blessed rivers by 7800 to 2400 votes.
In N.S.W. last Friday, the electorates of
Spiite a number of the anti-Federal leaders voted
or Federation. Among others, Premier Reids
constituency gave the Bill 120 majority ; Lyne’s,
over.'GOO majority ; Ashton’s, about 500 majority ;
Cotton’s, 70 ; Gould’s, about 180 ; M'Gowen’s, 00 ;
Sydney Smith’s, 300; Ball’s, over 700. These
figures seem to suggest that sundry individuals
will be very much missing after the fast-approach-
inor funeral election.
Voting by States the Convention Bill was
carried in all four. Voting as Australia, by over
100,000 majority. As provinces, Victoria carried
it by 4to 1; Tasmania, 3to 1; S. Australia, 2 to
1; N.S. W., by 6000 majority, and by 69 elector-
ates to 56. If this is not majority rule, what is?
Thk Bulletin isn’t talking or thinking about
Sydney. It is talking and thinking of Australia.
Friday’s poll is another evidence that, if
N.S.\V. doesn’t federate, the ultimate destiny of
the Kaverina is to separate and throw in its lot
With Victoria. All the districts whose natural
outlet is Melbourne, but which are practically
compelled by the provincial system to trade with

Ihe fact that Riverina, which loses heavily by
provincialism, was manned down by the vote of
far away Sydney, whose gain is Riverina’s loss,
accentuates the difference of interests more
strongly than ever.
J. G. Drake (Q.), M.L.A., staunch Demo-
crat and one-time promising politician, is at pre-
sent puzzling his friends and baffling his enemies,
r ive years ago Drake was looked upon as a pro-
bable Premier of the immediate future; now
nobody knows whether he intends abandoning
politics or making a big political coup. He is a
Radical up to his_ hair-tips, an accepted and
sympathetic authority on Federation, and one of
the most popular and trusted men in his province ;
but he has obviously lost his way. With a strong
Socialistic-Labor faction in front, and a gang of
disreputable reactionaries in the rear, he stands
between the political devil and the political cess-
pool. Unless the coalition is burst up, Drake’s
time won t arrive before his epitaph is written.
The night before the Federal election, the
Australian Star came out with the magnificent
lie that the Federal Bill, which expressly provides
for absolute Freetrade inside the Commonwealth,
rendered all hope of intercolonial Freetrade
impossible. Also, it dished up the old perjury
that Victoria’s provincial bounties imposed before
June 30 were to be paid by the Commonwealth—
this despite the fact that there is no such pro-
vismn in the Bill, and that Premier Turner ex-
plicitly stated that Victoria would pay its own
bounties. And it also Asked the people to vote
at’q"w t * le because, under Federation,
iN.S. W. would not have the power to raise customs
revenue by a.separate provincial tariff of its own.
What kind of a Federation would it be wherb
each province could run its own separate tariff?
Rockhampton, headquarters of Central Q.
autonomy, has pronounced against Federation.
Rockhampton’s chief grievance is that clause of the
I ederal Constitution which requires the consent
of the Parliament of a province before that pro-
vince can be. subdivided. Rockhampton asserts
that this provision makes Separation an impossi-
bility, inasmuch as Q. Parliament never would
consent to such a sub-division. If Rockhampton
imagines Queensland will consent to partition if
she remains outside the Federal Union, that sultry
city is hugely mistaken ; and according to the
Colonial Boundaries Act the Imperial authorities
can t grant Separation in the teeth of the Q. Parlia-
ment. Charters Towers, however, the most im-
portant city in the North, is inclined to favor the
Federal movement, and the feeling in Southern
Q. is decidedly anti-provincial.

S.A. Parliament meets again June 23, and
the chief energies of the session will be devoted to
the passage of a Pastoral Bill, which will relieve
the squatters without prejudicing the interests of
the country.

AMATEUR HUMORIST: “ Yes, just across the way; but if you’ll slip round the
