S.A. anti-Billifces, just on the eve of the
Federal polling, published a circular asserting that
Federation would result in an “exodus of em-
ployers, workmen, and general workmen to the
big centres of Australia.” Within a week the
answer came in a big advt. in the dailies from all
the big manufacturers stating that Federation
would increase and extend industries, and denying
the anti-Billites’ assertions point-blank. The
advt. bore the signatures of over 150 of S.A.’s
leading producing firms.
One of the loud and persistent assertions
of theN.S. W. anti-Bill ious orators was that thecom-
bined “ armies ” of S. A., Westralia, and Tasmania
numbered only 70 men, therefore the absurdity of
N.S.W. federating with these provinces was quite
obvious. A day or two before the poll Major-
General French wrote to the papers pointing out
that the three “ armies ” were not 70 but 4390
strong. So, apparently, it was only another cram.
The literature of the honorable anti-Billious
gentlemen was mostly lies.
About the last wild cry of L. F. Heydon
before his provincial gang was licked by 5000
votes on Friday was reported as follows by his
admiring organ, the Telegraph :
If the progress continued at the same rate as duringthe
last four years, N.S.W. would, within a hundred years,
have a population of 56 million people, as compared with
14 million in all the other federating colonies. Why,
then, &c., &c. ?
Look at that, now ! In the last four years the
“ other federating colonies ” advanced from
1,740,384 to I,B72,9oB—increase 132,524. And
N.S.W. advanced from 1,223,370 to 1,323,400-
increase 100,030. And, if progress keeps on for a
century on the basis of these numbers N.S.W. will
have 3,824,150 people against the other provinces’
5,053,484. If it keeps increasing at the percentage
of the last four years, another century will see
Westralia with more people than all the other pro-
vincesputtogether. Which isanotherof the reasons
why The Bulletin regards Heydon as a highly-
respectable but grossly-ignorant bigot who talks
most awful rot through his hat.
W. J. Ryott- Maughan, the audacious
young man who lately captured for Labor the
Boodle wraith seat of Burnett (Q.), is in many
respects , a remarkable juvenile. During the
campaign he had to face a combination of obstacles
that would have dispirited many a strong Iran.
Although running in the Labor interest he was
vehemently opposed by the Worker, A.L.F.,
and Labor-leader Glassey; his opponent was
squatter-agent Dick Newton—an able man in
more ways than one. Maughan was a complete
stranger in the electorate, totally ignorant of its
riquirements, and probably—until recently—of its
geographical position ; then a well-meaning Inde-
pendent candidate of avowed advanced views
complicated matters. On top of all a writ for
.£450 was issued against Maughan the day after the
nomination ! This would have flattened out
most men, but it does not seem to have ruffled the
hyphenated one, who went straight ahead and
topped the poll. Whether the member will crush
the writ, or the writ the member, remains to be
seen. He deserves luck, anyhow.

During the recent electoral campaign in
the Burnett (Q.) the bigotry business was worked
for J 1 ] 1 it was worth. Ryott-Maughan, Labor
candidate, was denounced as having written a
bitter anti-Irish article in the now-defunct Bris-
bane Chronicle. On the other hand, Newton
(Govt, candidate) was assailed bv a priest who
fulminated against him from the altar. The latter
circumstance may account for T. J. Byrnes's re-
cent public protest at Townsville against “ eccle-
siastical coercion.’’ “ Ecclesiastical coercion " is,
of course, a fitting phrase when priestly influence
is wielded against the Govt, nominee, but when
sacerdotal lips bless the Ministerial candidate (as
they nearly always do in Bananaland) it is, of
course, a very different matter.

As a final, the Minister for Mines pointed out that the
°f coal was 3,278,328 tons in 1893, as against
4,383,591 tons in 1897. The recital of these figures was
greeted with cheers from a representative company.
In 1897 N.S.W. had, according to these figures, a
coal output of 340,000 tons more than any pre-
vious year’s record. If the pointing out of this
discrepancy is equivalent to calling any paper an
unscrupulous perverter of the truth it isn’t The
Bulletin’s fault.


How history does repeat itself ! A
leaky ecal-laden barque, from the inevitable
Newcastle (N.S.W.), has, it is cabled, put into
Whangaroa (M.L.), the crew having refused to
proceed on the voyage. The Bulletin hopes
that, in the interests of Proputty and Lawnorder,
these mutinous ruffians will expiate their crimes
in gaol. As the ship didn’t sink at her anchorage
in Whangaroa she was clearly fit to proceed to
San Francisco.
The old “wooden wall,” Nelson, lately
bought by a Sydneyite for .£2400 and now in Port
Jackson, was for years head quarters of the Vic.
naval forces. When launched in 1814 she was the
largest war-ship ever built in England, but in ’Bl
Vic. Govt, had her cut down to a single-decker.
The oak in the grand old hooker is said to be as
sound to-day as when she first swam.
If N.S.W. Premier Reid for once keeps
his word and opens new Federal negotiations with
the other provinces, he will probably be met with
a demand for a solid cash security (say, .£50,000)
that the N.S.W. Govt, will keep its promise this
time. When the other provinces went into the
last move mention N.S.W.’s invitation, they did
it on the written agreement, enacted by Parlia-
ment, and sanctioned by the Reid Govt., and en-
dorsed with the signature and the big red seal of
His Ex., that all that was wanted in N.S.W. was
50,000 votes and a majority. Then Reid and
Want and the rest of the political welshers repu-
diated that agreement as unscrupulously as any
gang of small bookmakers at a pony-trot. If
there are to be any more negotiations it will
probably be necessary that there must be a very
different kind of N.S.W. Government, or else
this one must find substantial bail. No other
Government is likely to take the mere promise of
the Reid Ministry again on any subject.

The Undeserving Poor.
At a meeting of Sydney City Mission, a few
days ago, the subject of Old Age Pensions
incidentally cropped up, and His Excellency
Gov. Hampton went so far out of the beaten
gubernatorial path as to slate that he didn’t
cuss strongly at such things with limitations.
In his own words, he “ should not at all object,
no matter where he resided, to contribute
towards a fund for the creation of Old Age
Pensions, but it would be on the condition that
the pensions should be paid only to those who
deserved them.” To put it in another way,
Gov. Hampden, like every other Orthodox and
Respectable Personage, objects to the Un-
deserving Poor having pensions or any other
decent provision or consideration for their old
age. And when one remembers all that the
world owes to the Undeserving Poor, and how
deeply the Respectable and Orthodox section of
the community is indebted to them, and how
much Gov. Hampden himself is dependent
upon them, The Bulletin regards this as most
ungrateful. In short, it desires to put in a
word for the Undeserving Poor, as a class which
has never been sufficiently appreciated.
* • « •
To begin with, the Hampden attitude rests
on a false arithmetical basis. It is not sug-
gested that the Governor should be called upon
to pay for the Old Age Pensions of the Un-
deserving Poor. That large and little appre-
ciated class will pay for itself if it gets a fair
show. The pensions will come out of the State
revenue, and to that revenue the Undeserving
Poor contribute more largely, in relation to
their means and often without any relation to
their means, than any other class. If all
the other sections of the community paid in
like proportion, there would be plenty of
revenue to provide old-age pensions for every-
body, but the lavish revenue contributions of
the Undeserving Poop are so much swallowed
up in paying the share of the public expenses
which should fall on Governor Hampden and
other deserving persons that there is nothing
left. Considering the nature of the situation,
the conduct of Governor Hampden and his kind
in kicking their great aggregate benefactor, the
Undeserving Poor, is an even worse kind of
ingratitude than The Bulletin fully recog-
nised a minute ago, when it commented on the
ingratitude of these people in the preceding
paragraph.
* * * •
The Undeserving Poor mostly drink ; their
tendency that way is the most prominent
feature in their undeservingness. Drink supplies
the biggest single item of taxation in almost
every Anglo-Saxon community, and out of every
shilling spent in drink about five times as
large a proportion goes to the State as out of
a shilling spent in any other way—save, per-
haps, in tobacco. And the Undeserving Poor
almost all smoke. Between these two items,
the person who is poor and thoroughly unde-
serving often contributes ten times as much to
the State revenue as the moderately affluent
person of shining merit. In N.S. W. particu-
larly, a-perfectly good individual who doesn’t
drink or smoke or take opium or use sugar in
his tea ; who has no land ; and whose income
is only ,£240 a year, or who successfully con-
ceals the fact that it is more, can live without
contributing more than a few coppers a year to
the Stale revenue. The Undeserving Poor
the individual who is thoroughly undeserving
in (he orthodox sense is always poor, or else
becomes so—help very largely to pay this meri-
torious person’s share of the interest on the
public debt and his share of the railway deficit
and the cost of police and military protection,
to maintain law-courts to protect his property,
and almost everything else—even for the con-
veyance and delivery of his newspaper. If
everybody was thoroughly deserving after the
Y.M.C.A. pattern, it would be compulsory
either to lump huge taxes on the necessaries of
life, or to put up the income-tax to about ss.
in the £ or something equally drastic —and that
would break the hearts of Gov. Hampden’s class.
In a thoroughly deserving community so many
of the mellow and fruity vices which now yield
large revenues would cease to yield anything
that some such desperate course would have to
be resorted to.
* * * *
In N.S. W. the Undeserving Poor drink and
smoke all day—provided their means hold out
—in a heroic effort to pay Gov. Hampden’s
salary and to save him from having his income-
tax doubled, or multiplied by four or more.
In Britain they smoke and drink all they can to
prevent the British Government being com-
pelled to put a huge land-tax on the Governor’s
estate ami to double his British income-tax.
They enlist in the army, the rank-and-file of
which is mostly composed of the Undeserving,
and get shot at a miserably low wage of next
to nothing per annum, in order to save his life
am! property. If it were not for the Unde-
serving, Britain would probably be reduced to
filling its ranks by conscription, anil then Gov.
Hampden might have to take a turn at carry-
ing a rifle himself. The existence of the very
wealthy class to which Gov. Hampden and his
associates belong, is almost solely owing to the
existence of the Undeserving. There is not
enough money, even in wealthy Britain, to let
everybody be so much as moderately rich—the
total wealth of the country which has a mort-
gage on half the globe only runs to about
.£260 per head, including everything. If every
man was Deserving, didn’t drink or smoke,
worked hard, saved his money carefully, and
invested it safely, and acquired an education
so that he could learn to strike out for himself
instead of being all his life the Undeserving
bond-slave of an employer, Britain would be-
come a land ot innumerable small capitalists,
but the big capitalist would become much less
plentiful. Money always keeps circulating,
audit is just because the Undeserving Poor don’t
grab their share of it hard enough as it goes by
that the plutocrat gets it in the end.
* » * *
There are always exceptional individuals who
rise in the world from the smallest beginnings,
and whose portraits and biographies infest the
Sunday-school literature of this weary world
for generations afterwards. They mostly owe
their rise to the Undeserving Poor. If all the
poor neither draijk nor smoked, were indus-
trious, self-educated and intelligent (because
their brains weren’t muddled by lh|Uor), then
the exceptional person who now rises because
he is all these things, would simply remain
down. He gets up because he is an exception
to his class. If his class wasn’t so Un-
deserving he would only be one of the crowd.
The more Deserving the poor get the less show
there will be for that exceptional person who
rises from the ranks, and comes to great afflu-
ence, and is an ungrateful and insufferable
bounder for ever after. If they only became
Deserving enough a day might come when a
man would need to know three languages before
he could get a fair show' at a job digging post-
holes. There was a time when a man who
could read and write with reasonable facility
was such a marvel that he had a good chance to
be an archbishop or a chancellor. Now, owing
to the increased deservingness of the great
mass of humanity, and without any less desert
on his own part, he is hardly good enough to
be a policeman. The improvement of the masses
is good for the masses, but bad for the excep-
tional individual. If the Undeserving Poor
would only wade in unanimously for a few
general ions with a view to becoming in every
w'ay Deserving, then it would be very bad for
Lord Hampden and his kind.
• * • •
Anyhow, as the Undeserving Person has
mostly contributed more than his share to the
State revenue, his pension is a matter, not of
charity, but of right. Gov. Hampden isn’t called
upon to pay it ; he pays for it himself. He also
pays for the Governor to a great extent. But
supposing he doesn’t get his pension when he is
old and past active service, what does Hamp-
den propose to do with him ? He must either
live by casual charity, or by organised charity,
or be supported in gaol—all of which cost about
as much as the pension would ; or he must die
in the streets, which is wretchedly unpietur-
esque; or he must be poleaxed. Is Gov.
Hampden prepared to poleaxe him personally,
or does he intend doing it by deputy ? And, if
he gets his pension, he will probably spend
most of it in drink, which is a perfectly lawful
proceeding sanctioned by Act of Parliament.
If he does so, the State will get most of the
money back in drink duties, so he will be a
far cheaper pensioner than the Deserving per-
son. Also the drink will probably kill him
off, and he will draw his pension for a much
shorter period than the Deserving person. And
if Gov. Hampden, whose salary is largely de-
rived from the proceeds of his drinking, desires
to object, then the ingratitude of that poten-
tate is beastlv to the last degree.
m * • •
Speaking personally, the writer doesn’t like
the Undeserving class. But he recognises
these facts : If the community was all Unde-
serving, it would be a very awful wreck
of a community indeed. If it was all De-
serving in the wide and orthodox sense,
the present scribe would have to know
a lot more than he knows now to keep
his billet. There are any number of people
who would he much better fitted for the billet
than he is, were it not that some of them got
no education to start with from their Unde-
serving parents, and some inherited drinking
and other tendencies which made them forget
what education they ever had, and some
wrecked their nerves through drink and other
things, and some never inherited the nerves
they might otherwise have possessed owing to
the Undeservingness of their forefathers, and
a general want of respectability has landed
others in the gaol or lunatic asylum or ceme-
tery. And the Uudeservingness of these people
has made life so much easier for him that he
can’t curse the Undeserving Poor as he ought
to do. Gov. Hampden, if he w asn’t so thought-
less or so shockingly ungrateful, would feel the
same way.

action, Hammond v. Hammond and Others, num-
bered 55 of 1897, with the approbation of the
Honorable Mr. Justice Cooper, by Messrs. B. D.
MO RE HEAD & CO., of Brisbane, Auctioneers,
in conjunction with Messrs. HILL, CLARK &
CO., of Sydney (the persons appointed by the said
Judge), at Mary-street, Brisbane, in the Colony of
Queensland, on the 21st Day of JUNE, 1898, at
Eleven O’Clock in the forenoon, in one lot,
TENHAM STATION
(Outside Tick-Infested Area),
Situated in Gregory South district of Queensland, con-
sisting of 276 square miles of country (more or less), of
which 190 square miles are Leasehold, having 17 years to
run from the Ist July, 1897, and 86 square miles qf country
held under Grazing Right, under “ Crown Lands Act of
1884.” Together with
6500 CATTLE (more or less), Fifty (50) HORSES (more
or less), STATION PLANT, STORES and IM-
PROVEMENTS thereon or belonging thereto.
The Country is situated on Cooper’s Creek and Kyabra
Creek, about 40 miles south of Windorah and about 230
miles west of Charleville railway station. It is very rich
fattening country for cattle, consisting of lightly timbered,
open, mulga country, with occasional black-soil plains,
abundance of salt-bush, blue-bush, cotton-bush, and other
edible shrubs. TENHAM is splendidly watered by the
main channel of the Cooper and its many branches, be-
sides springs. The Cattle are quiet, and have been well
worked. In the herd there are about 1000 head of Bul-
locks and Cows fit to start for market. The Improvements
are sufficient for the efficient working of the station.
Particulars and Conditions of Sale may be had gratis at
the office of Messrs. Macdonald-Paterson and Hawthorn,
Edward-street, Brisbane, Solicitors for the Plaintiff;
Messrs. J. F. Fitzgerald and Power, Queen-street, Brisbane,
Solicitors for the Defendants ; or at the offices of the said
B. D. Morehead .& Co., Brisbane ; Messrs. Hill, Clark &
Co., Sydney ; Messrs. Ryan, Hammond & Mates, Mel-
Doume ; and Messrs. Barker Bros., Adelaide, and at the
place of sale.
Dated this twenty-fifty day of April, 1898.
(L.s.) W. A. DOUGLAS,
Deputy Registrar.

The Melb. police make a unanimous guess
re the anarchist who planted his bomb. The Yio.
authorities somehow have always been very much
afraid of dynamite. An earnest student applied
at Melb. Public Library, not long: ago, for works
re the preparation of such explosives, but was
promptly fired out.
If Queen Victoria’s condolatory message
to Mrs. Gladstone was anything more than the
usual official second-hand sob, Her Gracious
didn't overload the old man’s memory with glory
when she referred to him as “ one of the most
distinguished statesmen of my reign.” This is
standing things on their heads with a vengeance ;
Victoria R. I. is a personage who happens to have
occupied the throne during Gladstone’s (intellectual)
reign.
Stock Owners N.S.W. and Queensland.—lt
is noticeable that in the year 1897 Messrs. Pitt,
Son and Badgery, Ltd., sold over 79,000 sheep
and over 5,000 cattle more than any other firm
through the Homebush Yards, so that any paper
professing to give a complete list of stock sales
without including the for going very important
operations must fall far short of giving the public
“complete information,” especially as this Com-
pany’s sales represent 30J, per cent, of the whole of
the sheep sold, and 20| per cent, of the whole of
the cattle sold in the year, and by 15 firms.*

Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“The champion Melbourne caligraphist, pony-
proprietor, and plunderer of Presbyterian parsons
(who hits still a long term of his original sentence
to serve in Pentridge), was visited last week by a
solicitor who announced to the gentleman the
decease of his grandfather and the gratifying fact
that the convict thereby inherits between £140,009
and .£45,000 sterling.
“ The unfortunate man whose insane wife re-
cently destroyed their three children and at-
tempted suicide, sold off his furniture and effects
by auction whilst the excitement over the affair
was strong, with the result that about 500 people,
principally women, attended and so overcrowded
the house that the auctioneer had to adjourn to a
waggonette outside, where a complete clearance
at big prices was effected. Some of the she-
ghouls were ready to tear each other’s eyes out
during the bidding.
“ Re the cruel surprise sprung on local spinsters
by the unlooked-for betrothal of that handsome
college-lecturer to a professedly love-proof heiress,
the latter hardly deserves all the blame of the
deception. Certainly she was aggressively pla-
tonic, but was that any more artful than osten-
tatiously pining and wearing willows, supposedly
for the sweet sake of Beryl Faber, otherwise
appropriated ?
“ Melbourne’s show bar-girl has at last met her
Waterloo. Hymen has captured her; and there
is great sorrow in the ranks of the monocle and
high-collar brigade.
“ The chances are that the Greek play to be
staged at the Melb. Town Hall, on ltith, will turn
out to be a hybrid mixture of Wagnerian music-
drama and the mildest form of Italian opera.
Marshall Hall has seized opportunity by the fore-
lock. For years past he has been trying to sur-
prise a Melb. audience with one of Wagner’s
operas. He doesn’t care an iota about Greek—
even if he knows any—but he’s going in for a big
musical sensation, and so “ Alcestis ” has come in
handy as an experiment. Turns out that the
chorus is to be hidden, like the concealed orches-
tra at Bayreuth. A Greek play without its chorus
to the front might just as well be Lohengrin. And
as if this were not anachronism enough, the
masterful Ormond Professor decrees that the
whole text is to be sung to Italian sounds, because
they go best with his music ; so there will be a
sort of “ Lucia di Lammermoor ’’ flavour about the
whole thing. The Greek play was lyric, and this
will be lyric—that will be about all the two things
will have in common. Dispirited Dr. Leeper, in
bedraggled raiment, fleeing before the imperious
demands of Marshall Hall, would fit in nicely as
a Greek episode, for what with one trial and
another the Doctor already looks as if he were
being homicided by the Fumenides.
“Stated in several papers that Madame Wie-
dermann must have found Professor Marshall
Hall’s death-scene music for ‘ Alcestis ’ very diffi-
cult to sing, but the lady herself says that she
found it not at all trying, although she sang it
with an intensity of dramatic passion the like of
which is not often seen on the concert platform.
The voice part was written with a view to the
possibilities of her register, and she ‘ threw it off
her chest’—literally—like the great singer she
really is. The Professor’s 1 Alcestis ’ death squall
is ‘ music ’ —as Gounod said of Weber’s —‘ that
one would not like to meet in the dark.’ It is
weird, creepy, haunting—like a peep into a black
abode of slimy reptiles.
“ At the Princess’, ‘ Thrums ’-folk—taking no
thought for their ‘ poor perishable bodies,’ beyond
a few parritch-and-whiskeys and their * Saw bath
blacks’ —seldom incite Pattie Browne to displays
of fine plumage. Masquerading as a gipsy lass,
she wears a skirt of hodden trey and faced red-
brown cloak and hood, with her small, smart feet
and ankles jimp and neat in white hose and high-
heeled leather shoon. Bright, saucy and crisp,
Miss Browne’s ‘ Babbie ’ is maybe rather a prosaic
edition of the will-o’-the-wisp ‘ Egyptian ’ enchan-
tress, weaver of spells for the sententious boy-
preacher. The same with Cecil Ward’s ‘Little
Minister.’ In the details of awkward arm-and-
leg-deportment, dowdy coat-tails, quaint high
hats and youthful pomposity, the stage version is
realistic. But ‘a man’s a man for a’that,’and Rev.
Gavin Disliart, prig though he be, besides being a
man (albeit a very young one), is a true knight and
something of a saint. Tosuitdramaticexigencies,
however, the clerical laddie is defrauded of his
naive enthusiasm, awe-struck consciousness of
sacred responsibilities, and bewildering blend of
sheepishness and grit. No ! The Little Minis-
ter, like Little Billee,- was never meant for the
boards.
“ Restored to the dignity of Lord Rintoul’s
daughter, the quondam Egyptian lays by some
of her youth with her skittish gipsy skirt, and
looms larger than aforetime in white brocade,
with a fillet of electric blue twisted round her
high-piled coiffure. En route , with unsuspecting
papa, to lasso the little minister on the grounds of
his ‘Scotch marriage,’she throws on an ermined
over all of apple-green moire and plumed, pink-
straw ‘lB3o’ bonnet. Thus arrayed, their new
shepherdess storms the hearts of the canny Auld
Licht congregation, exciting them to ‘ wail a por-
tion of joyous psalmody ” in their glee at the
inevitable jealousy of other kirks.
“ All sorts and conditions of Scottish accents
figure in stage ‘ Thrums,’ though Eloise Juno, as
old ‘ Nanny,’ boasts the most musical of the col-
lection.
“ Pa and ma Madden, two daughters and son-
in-law elect, helped to welcome back Pattie
Browne. Th& acting-Governor’s fourth daughter,
Leslie,. is to plunge into society next month, so
that wedding in September is, probably, as popu-
lar with the connection as are most similar
functions.
“ Any amount of fuzz and fizz at Wilson Barrett’s
farewell. They do say that W.B. wante I to
wind-up with ‘Virginius,’ as before, instead of
‘Othello,’ as requested this time. Probably
with an eye to farewell speeches, and so on, he
hankered after the old Roman’s white skirts and
silvery locks. Black face and woolly hair match
all right with frenzy of histrionic passion, but
they’re not so very appropriate to chastened real-
life adieux. Small blame, too, to Maud Jeffries
if she shied, as reported, at so many after-appear-
ances in Desdemona’s night-gown. Applaud as
they would, the audience coulcjn’t coax her on till
she’d thrown on her blue redingote. In his good-
bye speech W.B. somehow strayed into a belated
account of the Jubilee procession. ‘Horses
pranced, trumpets blared, cannon roared ’—here
the speaker jibbed uneasily, and changed the
subject just in time to save himself from ‘ women
screamed, dogs barked, children yelled.’
“lie a recent toney wedding, the bridegroom’s
kinsfolk, jointly and severally, possess all the
beauty-spots round about a favorite upland region.
A summer or two agone, so say truth-telling liill-
folk, the clan's widowed chieftain with 1 1 is one
fair daughter (since married) decided to shunt
house-keeping worries awhile by letting their
picturesque home for the summer and taking up
their own quarters with a married son, settled
thereabouts. When the news was broken to the
latter’s wife, she promptly put hubby up tofinding a
desirable tenant for their own woodland retreat. It
wasn’t the invaders themselves she objected to, so
site said, but the hordes of acquaintances bound
to follow in their wake. Anyhow, when both
abodes were snapped up in a hurry by unexcep-
tionable inmates, the evicted owners bore down
en masse on the hospitable halls of another brother,
a grass-widower, by the way, only to learn that, in
his loneliness, he, too, had successfully advertised
his little place ‘ to-let,’ confident of a warm corner
for himself in the home of Ins boyhood. In this
dilemma, the homeless band, bereft of furniture,
linen, and plate, along with their leased resi-
dences, put up a tent somewhere on the ancestral
acres, and had high times and lots of guests.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Cyprus the hurdler, who won again at Randwick
on Saturday, is a horse Sydney cannot afford to lose. He
registered a good performance in giving two and a-half
stone and a beating to a horse like Elisler, about whom
punters took even money. Cyprus has been sold for
Victoria, where all the good hurdle horses go.
* * * *
The steeplechase at Randwick last week was a
stirring race. Three third-class animals started, one of
which fell in the first half-mile, and was led in on three
legs. The jockeys on the other two rode leisurely round,
yarning to each other, until the last fence, where one
fell, leaving the other to canter in alone. Surely it would
pay to train a decent steeple horse in Sydney. The prizes
are good, and anything that can stand up generally wins.
* * ■* *•
Mr. Sam. Hordern has decided to keep on his
Randwick stables, for occupation by some youngsters
coming down from the stud-farm to be handled ; which
looks much as if his absence from “the turf ” will be but
temporary. Although it was understood that Mr. Hor-
dern's racing-stud was for sale without reserve, the pick
of the basket were not traded off, and it, could not have
broken their owner’s heart to part with those that did go.
* » * *
F. : At Blacktown (N.S.W.) races, on Queen’s
Birthda.v, saw the 13.3 handicap run—a miscellaneous
collection of gee-gees piloted through the treps by nonde-
script riders. One “ jock,” having 7.7 on the card against
his mount, jumped on the scales, and, finding himself
some lbs. short, stepped on to the railway-line, filled his
pockets with ballast from the track, returned to the scale,
and adjusted his “ ballast ” until the desired’weight was
reached. .Then buttoned up his coat, mounted his nag,
and rode a good race, though he didn’t win. Immediately
the weighing-out was got through, all hands crossed the
railway-line to the pub. opposite until time for the next
“ event."
* * * »
Owners of Sydney ponies now racing in Vic-
toria have just cause for grievance in the eleventh-hour
ruling of the V.R.C. committee against the iilliputians.
Aletallic and Aluminium have raced at Flemington more
than once without action being taken, and attention has
frequently been directed during the past year by the
press to the anomalous position of the governing body’,
with horses and owners, disqualified by the A.J.0.,
allowed to run on courses under its jurisdiction. Yet no
move was made till such time as the policy of drift
had lulled owners into a sense of security. Besides, with
the poor class of nags now racing on suburban courses,
Victoria would he benefited by the presence of some of
the smart Sydney “ fifteen-handers ’’—always providing
said ponies left their knowledge of betting tactics behind
them at the border.
* * * *
James Wilson, sen., just missed a nice stake, at
Flemington, on Queen's Birthday, through the boy aboard
Alabaculia being too weak to finish alongside Mo. Trahan,
on Cherry’. Several punters have been waiting for the
Queenseliff mare since one performance last year, when
the boy incautiously remarked, coming out of the weigh-
ing-room, “ I would ha’ won, sir, if she hadn’t stuinlljd at
the turn.” Judging by old Jimmy’s fiery look, that
youngster probably’ learned in due course" that a still
tongue doesn't make a sore head.

Racing matters are in a healthy condition in
Westrnlia, and good horses and men are continually being
drained from the other pro\ inces to supply the wants of
their younger rival. Thanks to the tote, the W.A.T.C.
has a “capital” balance of nearly £70,(100, and this
season, after paying all exes., its profits amount to over
£5OOO. The added money is co tinual y on the increase,
whilst cheeseparing tactics obtain in the Ikey Alo pro-
vinces.
* * • *
Westralian punters will rejoice to see First Mate
carrying Alec Forrest’s colors, for “ Bro. Alec,” though
credited with a two-handed grab-all policy in political
life, runs his horses straight. This is probably due as
much to his sporting qualities as to the ingrained Westra-
lian belief in the superiority of the “Forrest” article.
* » * *
As in N.S. Wales, where trotting-owners have
killed file game, so in Queensland ; the chief jockey club
has decided to leave trotting events off its programmes
in future. At the recent Canterbury (M.L.) trotting
meeting, the prize-money for eight races was 750sovs.
Had Sydney owners gone “straight,” they would now
have a trot for a good stake at each of the proprietary
courses. What a cut-throat game they played I
• * * ♦
Mr. Wonbobbie Buckland had quite a trium-
phal march with his trotters in Maoriland. Fritz, who
made buggy horses of their best, was cheered upon every
appearance, and on Friday last he had a go against
his Moonee Valley (Vic.) record of 2m. 14 4-ssec. He
trotted the mile in 2m. 14isec., creating a new Austral-
asian record. Good as Fritz undoubtedly’ is—in Aus-
tralia—his record shows how premature Dr. Slate was in
offering to back him against any trotter or pacer in
America. The doctor was perfectly safe in making the
offer, as it was not likely any inducement Australia
could hold out would persuade a Yankee owner to risk the
trip across with a crack horse. As for Fritz going to
America, if times goes for anything, horses able to beat,
the Australian champion are plentiful as blackberries. It
has been argued that our tracks are not conducive to a
horse showing at his best, but against this is the fact
that, as far hack as '93, a horse named Greenlander trotted
a mile in a skeleton waggon in 2m. I4Jsec.
• • • •
Wonbobbie Buckland’s Viva paid in Maoriland
one tote div. of £l7B 18s. Viva is full sister to Torrent,
with which Harry Knight won so jjpany trots about
Sydney.
* * * *
The appeal by Martin Taylor (M.L.) against
sentence of two months’gaol for “sporting-shop” keep-
ing is unique. Taylor had pleaded guilty, expecting a
fine, and, when convicted, appealed. Such an unheard-
of thing paralysed the lieak, who treated it as an excep-
tional case, and allowed leave to appeal. But he made
the security stiffer than the sentence —£1500.

fiock a fiver to hack a trotter bearing his name at a way-
side country show. The neddy paid the “ limit,’’ but
bookie disappeared. The saint and the sinner I

The performances of Newhaven and Maluma at
'Oine should make Australian owners pause ere venturing
their cracks on such a long and expensive trip. Judici-
ously placed, our horses would pay exes, all right, but
patriotism or “.biff head ” makes owners fly at too big
game.
* * * *
All the best racing blood is gradually being
drained from Australia. Having secured Trenton, Car-
nage, Carbine and Aurum—the pick of the Musket strain—
attention is now being paid to Yattendon ; Patron (Grand
Flaneur—Olga), one of our stoutest and most sterling
stayers having lately been purchased for service at ’Ome.
This is a poor outlook for future racing.
* * * •
Told of a certain diminutive horse-charmer, that
on the day following a gathering where he had romped in
short-weighted he received a cardboard box per post.
There being a probability of pigeons or black duck therein
he paid the double postage dues. Contents—two saddle-
leads. Pasted on one was an account of the sinful one's
race, with press observations. The other displayed un-
musical language of “colonial” manufacture, and a
Scriptural quotation re shortness of life, shortness of
weight, or something.

W. : “ Was yarning to-day to a tobacconist in
his shop near a Sydney pony course. Most of the cus-
tomers were jockeys, and the shopkeeper told me the
secret history of each. Eleven out of 17 mannikins were
then under disqualification-and several spoke exultingly
of their cronkness. Yet there are people who regularly
stake more than their pocket-money on ponies (and
jocks), but refuse to buy from (he local grocer because he
charges U)d. per lb. more for his butter than the leviathan
in town.”
* * • *
Harry Morant (“The Breaker ”) was last week
jumping his horse Cavalier over a 4ft. rail filling a gap in
the fence at the rear of the old Racecourse Hotel, Claren-
don (Windsor, N.S.VV. disl rict). The horse swerved and,
going too fast to stop, cleared the 7ft. palings! The
horse is a five-year-old gelding bred by Philip Charley,
by Freedom (imp.) out of Clara, an imported trotting
mare owned by the late Andrew Town, of Hobartville.
The jump was done in the presence of Mr. Kelly, of Clar-
en lon, and some half-dozen others ; and the measurement
is well-authenticated. The fence is a ti-tree stake one —
such as Chinamen usually erect to protect their gardens.
•» • * *
S. le W. : Speyed mares for racing could be
called speylings. To a layman in sport, a penalty of Ist.
extra would seem fairer than an allowance as to geldings.
There is no improvement in the breed of horses (the
original motif) to be gained by speying. The same does
not hold good with adding horses to the list. No one
thinks of starting a stud with equal numbers of stallions
arid mares. To hippophagists, however, the ovolithotomy-
is good.
» * • •
Randwick versus Wallaroo was the chief Sydney
football match on Saturday last, some 2000 people putting
in an appearance at the Agricultural Grounds. Play was
fast, and if anything the Wallaroos had the best of the
deal; but there was nothing in it, neither side scored, and
the game ended in a draw. Sydney and University played
a remarkably fine game, which also ended in a draw, each
side scoring 9 points. Pirates and Paddington, as antici-
pated, was a one-sided game, Pirates simply running over
their opponents with a score of 3(5 to 8. Casualties :
Bratind, a dislocated knee ; Duggan, a broken head;
Lane, a gashed cheek ; and Miller, a broken finger, all in
the Randvvick-Wallaroo set-to.
* » * •
Vic. lacrosse-players threatened to knock foot-
ball on the head this winter in the matter of public
patronage, hut, so far, public patronage has evinced a
steadfast resolution to abstain from “ patronizing” either
game. The lacrosse men have themselves to blame, for
public apathy has been encouraged by apathetic lacrosse
players. The game is clever, fast, spectacular enough to
appeal to most amusement-seekers, but has been ruined
so far by the spruce bank-clerk who scorns to remove his
picturesque figure and gorgeous costume from a position
of Vantage near the ladies’ stand. Let the ball come
within that radius and he performs wondrous pick-ups,
throws, dodging, &c., but under no circumstances will he
get out of range of Mabel's admii ing eyes.
* * »■ *
Lacrosse is struggling for a footing on the Wes-
tralian fields. But it has far to go yet to overtake foot-
ball. Even in Adelaide, where it is strongest, the
attendance at the big matches has not averaged one-sixth
of that at the big football matches on the same days. In
a few northern S.A. towns, though, it has practically
displaced football.
» • * *
Affiliation by the M.L. amateur A. A. with the
Cyclists’Alliance has proved an unprofitable partnership
for the pedestrian body, who think of quashing the agree-
ment. As pure and undefiled cycling is at a low ebb in
ALL., and an the Alliance is too high-toned to affiliate
with the cash-men, their future prospects are gloomy.
The M.L. A.A. has to programme events for the tin-pot
people in the different centres, but paucity of entries has
led to grumbling.
After the luncheon adjournment, a not-out bats-
man, indulging in a preliminary strike before resuming
his innings, complained that he saw three balls instead
of one ; whereupon a bowler, who had evidently been in

was clean-howled first hall. “ Why the devil didn’t you

The much-boomed Mdlle. Serpolette was rather
a frost, in Adelaide. The League officials refused to allow
her to scorch round the track in costume, and a cycle-
motor which she was advertised to ride broke down at the
last moment. It is beginning to be suspected that
Mdlle.’s Australian tour was projected at least partly in
the interests of certain brands of cycling costume.
» * » *
lie a ladies’ hike-race at Wellington, on Easter
Monday, a Maoriland paper writes : “It was the funniest
thing ever seen on the track. Only two starters, and one
of them was winning by about 100yds. As she neared the
post, the crowd began to applaud, so, thinking it was all
over, she turned off the track about 10yds. from home,
dismounted, and began to walk away ! An official drew
her attention to (he fact that she had not finished the
course, and led her back to the track. She attempted to
mount, turned the hike round the wrong way, rode
several figure B’s, and finally fell off ! Meanwhile, the
other damsel, urged on by the cries of the crowd, put on
a pound, and came toil'ng onward to the goal, but a kind-
hearted cavalier threw the leading lady on to her machine,
and a tangled mass of bike, skirt and iady forged past the
post a brilliant winner by four good inches.”

The downright lying in press advts. concerning
the unparalleled utility of worthless and unsafe low-grade
bikes is receiving attention in ’Murka. Liability to
criminal prosecution is suggested—a good idea that
should be extended to every-day commodities.
» * * •
Pedestrian Gilbert’s arrival in Melb. last week
was not the triumphal entry his friends had expected. A
small crowd of gaping office-boys and newspaper-sellers
followed him down Collins-street to a hair-dressing shop,
where the traveller sought refuge with a compatriot.
Gilbert is an undersized man of slight physique, whose
long red beard is in perfect harmony with his soiled suit
of khaki. Whatever be the opinions on the reported
£lO,OOO wager, or the methods of appealing to the hos-
pitable side of Australians by these pedestrians, it is un-
fair to insinuate doubts as to the loiia-fidcs of Gilbert’s
actual walking. His cheeks are abundant (a duplicate of
each being sent to Paris); and writer has testimony of
mounted trooper and linesman at Esperance (W. A.) as to
the Gaul having covered the distance from Bremer Bay
to Eucia per foot—as nasty a stretch to tramp solus as

The tremendous output of the Dunlop Co.,
Coventry (Eng.), may be gathered when it is stated that
1000 finished tyres are being turned out daily, or 300,000
tyres a year—from one factory alone. This should give
the lay mind a fair idea of the gigantic development of
the cycling industry in England.*

The new Commandant of the S.A. Army
left the post of Inspector of Distilleries to step
into the shoes of Col. Gordon. Some of the other
colonels feel so bad about the selection that they
threaten to resign. “Let ’em,” says Kingston—
who, as a full sergeant himself, knows all about
war.
Lady William Nevill, thus far, has visited
her delectable husband in gaol once a week regu-
larly, and, with the assistance of a recent legacy
(worth about £7OO a year), she puts a cheerful face
on her trouble.
The big German flyer, Kaiser Wilhelm der
Grosse, has again cut down the Atlantic record,
covering 3900 miles (3120 knots), Southampton to
New York, in 140 hours—an average of nearly 28
miles (22£ knots) per hour !
The economical side of the Melbourne
Bewerage system was casually revealed at last
weeks meeting of Met. Hospital committee.
Somebody threw up his hands in horror when it
me ntioiied that the cost of making the hospi-
tal decent in its sewage arrangements would be
*2OOO or thereabouts equal, at 5 per cent.,
to *lOO a year. Then the chairman reminded
the meeting that the City Council up to
now has charged the hospital .£4OO a year for
night-cartage ” of refuse that will presently be
washed away gratis. In fact, the management
V ... s^ ,ve ®bout £3OO a year for evermore by the
civilising of Smellbourne’s sanitary barbarism.
The Victorian Postal Department—the
most tired Postal Department in the Seven Pro-
vinces—decided that the work of fishing for the
mst mails that went down with the Manavvatu in
Port Phillip Bay, riy:ht at the Department’s back*
door, wasn t its funeral. The steamship co. said
that the work wasn’t its funeral either. The de-
partment neither sat up itself nor made the co.
Blt U 1 J ’ the mails were left in shallow water to
Out of 23 bags the kindly tide has, so far,
washed up eight. One containing 401 registered
letters, mostly addressed to “ Tattersall,” and
containing £lOOO or so, much of it in bank and
Postal notes, turned up lately along with the
placid dead dog, on the mud of Fisherman's
Bend, ihere are other valuable bags among the
lost mails, but the Vic. Postal Department isn’t
hustling after them. It is tired.
Solicitor Kennedy, leaving Scone (N.S.W.)
for fresh pastures, carries away with him an
illuminated valedictory, the ohief points of which
are that he is a sound lawyer, a zealous Sunday-
school teacher, an eloquent preacher and an adept
nigger minstrel. A good all-round man.
Baronet Arnott, the Cork millionaire, who
died lately leaving vast legacies to institutions
and persons in the south of Ireland, originated
vicariously, through marriage, Arnott House, the
well-known boarding establishment in Wynyard
Square, Sydney. One of the baronet s bequests
was J 240,000 to the staff of the Irish Times, a
newspaper he controlled for years.
S.A. rains are full of tricks. Lately they
have been dodging the Northern farms where
they were badly wanted, and have been pouring
themselves instead on the more Northern pastorai
country which has been abandoned in late years ;
so that it is proposed to shift farmers’ starving
stock to the country that has been thrown up »s
worthless.
Recently a junior clerkship examination,
in connection with Sydney Water arid Sewerage
Board, took place (says the local Telegraph;.
Amongst other things the candidates had to write
an essay. They were given three subjects to
choose from, one being “The pen is mightier than
the sword.” But one bright youth was un-
acquainted with this wise saying, and when tne
examiner called it out he wrote “ The pen ib
mightier than the saw.” Here is his essay :
A pen is mightier than the saw. A pen is much better
in every way than the saw ; the pen is much easier to use
than a saw. In the first place, for instance, a man sitting
in an office using his pen all day is by far better than a
man out in the sun and rain using a saw. The pen requires
much more art than the saw; a man with a saw has to
exert himself a great, deal. Is it not better to go to school
and learn to write than to stay about the streets and not
go to school. When it is time for a boy to go to worn,
then is the time that you are sorry you never went to
school ; he goes out to look for work, and he cannot
write, and he has to go and do hard work with the saw.
If you go into an office, and see a young fellow writing
you feel very disheartened that .you have to go out into
the rain and saw wood to build a house. The pen is a
very useful article. If you put the two together, the
pen and the saw, you find that the pen is by faY the
better. A man with a pen earns much more money than
a man with a saw. It requires much more brains and
care to use a pen than a saw. Therefore, for my part, l
think that the pen is a great deal better than the saw.

A Melbourne paper recalls a Grladstonian
story hailing from Clones, Vic, There was a
general election on, and two local celebrities, a
M‘Allister and a Murphy, fell out re the respective
merits of W. E. Gladstone and the R.C. candi-
date for Clunes. Murphy was made to smell
particular agony, and came up much battered out
of the mill. ‘‘Sure, I’m quoite convinced this
mahn Gladshtone is the bet.her man, but who the
divil is he, annyhow ? ” They told him that
Gladstone was the man who had been fighting all
Gehenna and over the way to secure Home Rule
for old Ireland. Then Murphy kicked himself,
and turned to his conqueror and wrung his hand.
“ It's meself that’s rale grateful to yez for the
father av a batin’ ye’ve given me ! ” he said.
At the little country church at Bugaboo,
the doorkeeper had long been annoyed by
cursory canines putting their noses inside the
sacred precincts during service. Armed himself
with a rock and waited. Shortly after, dog poked
in his nose and retreated, followed by a huge
gibber as he shot down the steps. . . . Door-
keeper, hearing strange noises, went out and found
local alderman desperately smitten in the abdo-
men, raising fearsome groans on the steps of the
temple, while a mangy cur peered curiously in
through the palings.
Particulars of qualifications and duties
required of the Assistant-Secretary (at =6300 a
year) for which Wellington (M.L.) Harbor Board
has been advertising in Sydney press. Must
state —
1. If surveyor’s, architect’s, or civil or mechanical en-
gineer's draughtsman. 2. Whether qualified as a L.S.
t If used to general office work and correspondence.
I. If can write shorthand and use typewriter. 5. Is he a
bookkeeper. 6. A Photographer. 7. If acquainted with
•seafaring matters. Duties : —Charge of Harbor Board’s
employees’ life assurance scheme. Checking monthly
v ouchers or payments. Charge of petty cash. Checking
and obliterating paid interest coupons. Charge of office
supplies. Checking customs payments to Board. Col-
lecting Sunday license-fees and rents, and preparation
and supervision of licenses (foreshores, watermen, hulks,
boats, etc.). Acting as draughtsman. Charge of plans.
Reproduction of plans by sun-printing. Checking of
specifications of new work. Attending to contractors.
Carrying out surveys, land and harbour. Taking levels
ind soundings. Laying out new works and checking
contractors’ books, etc. Successful applicant must also
take out an endowment policy to mature at 60, and also a
fidelity guarantee.
And they call this the age of the division of labor!
“ Mourambi,” to The Bulletin :
Re Kennedy platform exhibitions, alleged to have been
declared by medical men due to genuine hypnotic influ-
ence. I was present when, in ’95, Kennedy gave a gratis
exhibition, “ for medical men only,” at Sydney Criterion,
the bill of fare not materially differing from the stuff
usually dished-up to the public by Kennedy. The “ pro-
fessor’s ” victims may have been under mesmeric influ-
ence, hut, on the other hand, there was not one single
trick which might not possibly have been a barefaced im-
position managed simply by paying the subjects for
what they underwent. The one emphatic exception,
so far as my judgment goes, was the evidently genuine
cataleptic state into which the showman sent one of his
staff ; the same man who, in the Schools of Arts, nightly
had to sing his little song, “ Strolling round,” &c. Also,
it is true that on this occasion a few medical men
mounted the stage and attempted some feeble jokes of
their own; beyond which they contented themselves
with assuring the public that it was real kerosene which
one of the subjects was swallowing, and that it was
genuine castor-oil which another had eagerly accepted as
a present for his sister. The great majority of the audi-
ence, nevertheless, left in disappointment and disgust,
which, however, nobody made a point of publicly expres-
sing—a neglect of which the showman was of course
not slow in making capital. As for your statement
that even among practised hypnotists a swapping
“of subjects is seldom a satisfactory deal,” my
experience is that a real hypnotist deals best with an
entirely strange set of subjects at every performance.
Up till about a year before I witnessed Kennedy’s doings,
I had been a firm disbeliever in hypnotism, but I became
convinced of my error at a German University town
where, when the exhibitor called for subjects from the
audience, the stage was rushed by about 20 theological
students eager to expose the unholy fraud. The per-
former picked about half the number, and, although he
was dealing with subjects about whose hostility there
could be no possible doubt, he had them all helplessly
under his influence in 15 minutes —doing literally what
he liked with them. One hopped like a frog, another, on
all fours, barked like a dog, while his next neighbor
fended the dangerous animal off with a chair, and so on.
Hard to conceive anything more grotesque than the
sight of these 10 solemn men thus capering in their
priestly garments. Next night the stage was taken by
students of the free-and-easy beer-drinking sort, with
exactly the same result. One of the number required
two strong men to pull him off an imaginary' insulter,
whom he was striving to assassinate with a leaden
dagger. All the subjects were willing to give their word
of honor that they' found themselves absolutely compelled
to act as they did.

A contemporary refers to a law-suit, going
on in Switzerland about
“ the 520 fish out of the Sempach Lake driven
to the monks every year for the deliverance from
purgatory of the souls of the Austrian soldiers who were
slain in the Battle of Sempach. Those ecclesiastics have
eaten 254,800 of those fish, and they have been saying
masses for those souls for nearly 500 years, and yet the
wretched Austrians must be in purgatory still. And if in
Mexico they can get a soul out of purgatory in a
moment’s notice by the winning ticket in a raffle, how
comes it that in Switzerland five centuries of their inter-
cessions have not yet prevailed ?

In a Melb. witness-box, the other day, one
F. J. Minty stated that he had said of a certain
alleged misdemeanant, “It’s a monty he goes up,”
and Judge Hodges wanted to know what Minty
meant by “monty.” Counsel explained that a
“monty ’’meant a certainty. And “goes up? ”
asked his Honor. That means “ goes down, or
otherwise that he goes to gaol,” replied the lawyer.
“ Oh,” said J edge Hodges, “ then ‘ it’s a monty lie
goes up ’ means ‘ it’s a certainty he goes down,’ ”
and the His Honor entered the translation
among his notes.
The “ Delivery Charge ” on Wool. Under this
name a charge of ffad. per lb. is made by the Associated
Brokers of Sydney to the Wool-Boyers. Averag-
ing the number of Bales sold in the Sydney
markets at 3361b5., this %(\. is represented by
no less a sum than £75,0110. The CO-OPERA-

whatever to the Wool - Buyers. The only com-

per cent, to the Grower on large and small accounts
alike.*

SYDNEY, N.S.W.
At Narrabri (N.S.W.) bye-election, las
week, the Labor candidate (Ross) got in with 382
votes. The Protectionists mustered 555 votes,
but split them between two candidates, and were
left, though their foremost man was only 25 behind
the leader. The two Freetraders gathered in
227 between them. So the winner represents only
382 votes out of 1164 recorded ! When is the
second ballot coming along?

Re FURTHER ISSUE of SHARES.
Sir,—
As shareholders were informed at the half-yearly gene-
ral meeting of the company, held on the 27th inst., the
hoard of directors intend to make a further issue of 25,000
shares out of the present unissued shares in the capital of
the company, for the purposes explained at the general
meeting.
The new issue will consist of 25,000 shares, which will be
offered in the first instance at a premium of £3 per share
(that is to say, £6 a share) to all persons who are regis-
tered as members of the company on ttie 15th day of June
next, in the proportion of one share for every ten shares
then held by each member. For the purpose of ascertain-
ing the number of shares which each member will be en-
titled to apply for, the transfer-books of the company will
be closed from the 15th to the 17th of June.
Payment for the new shares will be required as follows :
£3 (being the premium) with the application, on or
before the 12th July next.
£1 on or before the 12th September next.
. £1 on or before the 12th October next.
£1 on or before the 12th November next.
In all cases where, by reason of the number of shares
held by any members, and application is made by them
as herein mentioned, such members shall be entitled to
fractional portions of shares, your directors have decided
to issue, in respect of such fractional portions of shares,
fractional certificates for each one-tenth part of a new
share to which members will be entitled ; these certifi-
cates to be transferable by delivery.
As soon as possible after the i7th day of June next a
form of application will be sent to each person who is
then a member, and a notice specifying the number of
fractional portions of shares which such member is
entitled to apply for. Such application form must be
duly filled in, signed, and returned to the company, to-
gether with the premium on all shares and fractional por-
tions of shares applied for, on or before the date to be
named in such notice ; otherwise such member will be
taken to decline to avail himself of any such offer as afore-
said in respect of sucli further issue. All shares and
fractional portions of shares not applied for will be dis-
posed of in such manner as to the directors may seem fit.
The directors, in deciding to issue fractional certificates,
are doing so in the interests of shareholders, as it is
thought that it will be more satisfactory if the power of
realising these fractious is left with the shareholders
themselves.
It will be one of the conditions attached to fractional
certificates that, on presentation at the office of the com-
pany of ten of these certificates at any time prior to the
twelfth day of December next, such ten certificates can
be exchanged for one full share (provided, of course, that
payment of the whole amount of £6 per share, as men-
tioned above, has been made in respect of the same).
Any shareholders desiring to realise their fractional por-
tions of shares without trouble may return them to the
’office of the company, when they will be placed in the
hands of the company’s brokers for disposal, and the net
proceeds transmitted to the vendors.
Unless these fractional certificates are presented at the
office of the company before the 12th day of December
next they will be regarded as void.
The company pays a dividend on the Ist October next,
and shareholders who shall have then paid the premium
of £3 a share and the first instalment of £l, payable on
or before the 12th September, as above stated, will re-
ceive one-third of the dividend, and those who shall then
have paid the premium of £3 and the first and second
instalments of £1 each will receive two-thirds thereof.
Shareholders have the option of paying up before the
12th September the whole amount of £(5 a share in
respect of their new shares, in which event they will be
entitled to receive payment of the full October dividend
thereon.
Scrip certificates for shares and fractional certificates
will he issued after the 12th of July next on payment of
the full amounts respectively due for shares or fractional
portions of shares.
By order of the board.
ALFRED MELLOR, Secretary.
39 Queen-street, Melbourne, 28th May, 1898.

“ Is it possible,” asks a Bulletin correspondent, “ that
they are going to break up the old wooden warship Nel-
son, without giving the Sydney public a chance of in-
specting her? The Nelson is built of oak, most of which
is as sound as when it was in the tree. What furniture
and house-fittings it will make ! ”
Ugo Biondi, Rickards’ coming engagement, is exceed-
ingly’ clever. He is of the school of Fregoli. One of his
best things is an imitation of a man entering a theatre,
saluting his friends watching a comedy (evidently a very
risqudone); then a very sad tragedy, which makes him
cry (he really cries). All this in pantomime. Again he
has a ladder fixed from the stage to the orchestra and
makes'up as different conductors-Donizetti, Sullivan,
Masoagni, Gounod, Liszt, Rubinstein, &c., conducting a
short selection from the works of each composer. The
changes are done with marvellous celerity, as Biondi only
puts down his head and up again. It is quite a new and
a very good “ turn.”
Not many would guess the agile Rickards’s
age as 62.
The Clitherows have had luck lately’. Firstly’, Mrs.
Clitherow and child went to England, with good pro-
spects ; then the two boys followed with Madame Albani ;
finally', W. F. Clitherow and daughter, Jessie, have gone,
under engagement to Wilson Barrett. All expenses are
paid, and little Jessie is to have a salary of 3us. a week
when not acting; £2 when acting.
“ T.D.” :Re Newbury and Orlando Harley. For years
I have been accustomed to hearing Harley cited against
the ex-Maorilanderas the best, tenor heard in Australia.
Yet, five or six years after his first visit he comes with a
voice still sweet and remarkably cultured, hut quite un-
reliable. These disheartening vocal failures help to drive
home the truth that singing voices, save the most
phenomenal, are only made to stand some 15 or 20 years’
wear. Then comes the vocalist’s chance to coin money by
teaching, but he lingers about the platform until
audiences no longer wish to hear him. The people who
can teach won’t teach, and those who can’t teach make a
tolerable living out of it.
“ Melb.” : In a Collins-street window is a Gladstone
photo., with an axe, draped in crape. Recalls the chorus
in “Tambour Major,” at London Alhambra, all wearing
crape on their arms when the news came of Offenbach's
death. A criticaster, “ Why not legs ? "
Two snappy lines from the latest Yankee song—“ When
a widow weeps : keep off the grass.”
“Theorbo”: Regina Nagel is in Paris, whereto
Bishopess Goe sent her, and recommended her to a sort
of Protestant conventual institution for board and lodg-
ings. Thus did Empress Eugenie shadow Patti. Regina
goes to Marchesi, with a special injunction to Madame
not to swear.
The Entr'acte, a little London weekly of the music-
hall persuasion, gets this sort of thing from its Australian
correspondent “Miss Maude Jefferys is almost as popu-
lar as is Mr. Barrett, which, considering how familiar a
Melb. audience is with the performance of Sarah Bern-
hardt, and other leading actresses, is say ing a great deal.
. . . Harry Rickards has scored a big success with
Charles Cartwright and ‘The Squire of Dames’ at Melb.
Bijou, and is in grave danger of becoming a bloated
millionaire.” To properly appreciate the truthfulness of
any’ Hinglish theatrical gossip in Australian papers one
must read what goes to Hingland from this side.
In one wet-k during the Adelaide season of the “French
Maid ” Ada Reeve sprained tier ankle, Bert Gilbert fell off
a wheel and so injured his face that he could not sing,
Carrie Moore was thrown from a horse and disabled, and
Alyce Leitoh was so ill from lung-inflammation that she
hail to “ &-ta.v home.” Yet the show went on, although
the house, followed the example of the two cycling princi-
pal and “ fell off.”
The m?ntal level of a Shakespearian audience does not
seem much in advance of a music-hall one. In the
“Hamlet” revival at Melb. Princess’s, Wilson Barrett’s
announcement “For England, for England!” in the
third act was solemnly swallowed by the great amphi-
theatre as a gag having reference to the “war-cloud,’
and a little patriotic demonstration was at once started
by a score of enthusiasts.
“No reference to the Jews or the Jewish people must
be made by the artists of this co.” Notice by Rickards in
the green-room of Melb. Opera House.

name. It is now the “ Pop ’’-shop.
“It arrive that I go to Melbourne on Thursday.”
“ During that I am here I will buy it the note papair.”
“ Nevaire I have seen something like to eat.” Who can

missed from Sydney art-circles?
Fitzgerald Brothers have some very strong admirers in
Maoriland. Some partisans were once discussing the
circus firm in the town of Ngatiwhackaruahoa, when one
said dogmatically, “Yes, they are undoubtedly the Bar-
nums of Australia.” “ Ga’ long.” said another enthu-
siast, “ Barnumsare the Fitzgerald Brothers of America !”
Ed. Bulletin. — Re Prof. Marshall Hall’s statement con-
cerning German musicians. On my asking Warwick
Williams, musical adviser to London County Council, how
his band of picked London musicians was so successful in
Germany during its extended tour, lie told me that there
are very few good bands in the Fatherland, that medi-
ocrity prevails— vide samples in the London streets.
Hence the success of the London band.— Ben Fuller,
Christchurch, M.L. 17/6/98.
Coppin's pageant of Melbourne old-time worthies at the
Old Colonists’ Carnival, the other day, included Miss
Louisa Swannell, and people asked who she was. Miss
Swannell, termed “ the Australian Nightingale,” and
aged 16, made a sensation at the concerts given in the
vestibule of Melb. Royal before the theatre was ready
in ’55.
This outting from a recent American programme shows
that the non-success of Nat Goodwin in Australia still
rankles in his heart:—
NAT C. GOODWIN.
“ An American Citizen,” the “ David Garrick ” of
the nineteenth century, who has no “ Rivals,”
who was treated to “The Silent System” by the
“Gilded Fools” of Australia, hut who “Turned
Up” with “ Nathan Hale” in America to find a
“ Gold Mine.”
Fitzgerald Bros.’ circus was to have visited Gisborne (of
M.L. massacre celebrity), but Wragge sent along a gale,
and the steamer had to put back to Auckland. Now,
most shows visiting Gisborne, instead of sending an ad-
vance, drop a parcel of printing to one Billy Miller, of
that town, who, in return, booms them for all they are
worth —and sometimes more—hut, if they are any good
at all, they do great biz. under his auspices. The Fitzs.’
were trumpeted for miles round, the whole district was
plastered with lions and tigers, and the Maoris came in
droves from all directions, and, when they found no
circus, proceeded to interview Billy. The spokesman—a
16 stoner, and well-moistened —to Miller : “ I shav, what
for no shircus? I link you te humbug. I come Kisborne,
I spend fi poun’ te pubricouse ; 1 lose ’em all te time,”
then, suddenly, “ Oh, Got tarn te shircus ! Cummanava-
trink ! ”
“ Mr. Wilson Barrett is frankly melodramatic in his
realisation of the r6le,” said Melb. Argus, the first time
he disappointed Melbourne with his Wilfred Denver. As
a matter of fact, Barrett doesn't realise any young man s
character, and, when he affects to be drunk, he isn't in
the least melodramatic. A sober actor must get away
from himself before he can seem the worse for liquor.
Barrett hardly ever gets away from himself and his
measured e locution.

A certain Yarra-bank agitator some time ago applied
for a billet as super, at a big theatre, but when his
identity became known he was “ choked off ” instanter.
He might have led to a revolt of the ballet.
The joke at London Lyceum is that Irving has 60 pairs
of specs, in use. He is always mislaying them. He has
to take them off every time he goes on ths stags.
The apparent stupidity of the Melb. police
as regards that bomb explosion grows less appa-
rent in the light of the suggestion that a thump-
ing reward should be offered for the capture of the
culprit.
Australian hospital-committees might profit
by the example of that of Hannelly, in Wales
(Britain). Over a weird dispute between matron,
head-nurses and house-doctors, the committee
divided ; and, finding sides equal and a big fight
in prospect, compromised, by each party sacri-
ficing its special proteges in the public interest.
The whole staff was therefore promptly sacked,
and replaced by another which knew nothing of
the dispute.
“ Here you are,” reproachfully exclaimed
Official Assignee Palmer to a bankrupt in Sydney
Court, “at the beginning of the year owing
.£l5O to money-lenders, and yet you go on
buying tobacco, cigars and cigarettes on credit ! ”
It was no doubt very wrong of that bankrupt to
behave as described, but how does it come that
the virtuous indignation of Court officials so seldom
assails the small luxuries of the bankrupt whose
schedule shows a deficit of £30,000 ?

“Ponte” on some tricks of the N.S.W.
licensed fisherman’s trade :
While the best of the men recognise that fish-con-
servation is to their own interests, there are others who
hold that laws are made to be broken, and who wait ana
watch for a week at a stretch for a chance of making a
haul in closed waters. A noted “ poacher,” having been
caught and fined, openly stated that he would haul there
again within a week. The inspector took up the overt
challenge, and a game of vigilance began. Within one
week the inspector, from his post of observation, saw tne
boat make for the prohibited waters, followed, watched
them “shoot” the net, and, just as he thought they were
about to “land” it, came up and took possession. He
found he had been tricked —there was no net—nothing
but the hauling-ropes. Meanwhile, the other hoat
had made a haul some little distance off, and got 30
baskets of fish. Another inspector could not understand
how it was he never caught the depredators, although
there was evidence that the thing was of common
occurrence. The men had a scout hidden in the in-
spector’s boatshed, and, as soon as the boat was launched,
he streaked off and flashed a signal to his mates. As the
inspector had to pull a mile or tw'o, there was ample tone
to get the net in and clear. The boy fell asleep one night
and so gave the game away. Another inspector was noted
for his alertness. He seemed to have the happy knack of
turning up at the right moment. An increase was
expected in his family, so the house was put under
espionage. One evening the scout reported that the
inspector had just ridden post-haste up the road. With
the whole night before them, the men took their time and
got more fish in one haul than they had done previ-
ouslv in a month.

Premier Turner has ukased that no more
people are to be sent to gaol for being hungry and
cold and knocked-out in a public place. People
of this class, gathered in “on the vag.,” are to be
taken before a P.M., who is empowered to ha.nd
“ deserving cases ” over to a charitable institution
to which the Govt, will pay 10s. per week for four
weeks. Strange to say, Panton, P.M. (who is
personally a most kindly and benevolent man),
officially snorts fiercely about the new order, and
declares, less in sorrow than in anger, that practi-
cally there are no “deserving” cases, and all who
have been brought before him during his magis-
terial career hlive been more or less undeserving.
There’s the trouble. If the “ case ” has to be
deserving, as Mr. Panton interprets the term, it
will be cleanly-washed and combed (like Mr.
Panton), and newly-shaven (like Mr. Panton) and
will wear an air of chastened suffering and a well-
brushed bell-topper (like Mr. Panton); it will
sing a bar of “ God Save the Queen ” every five
minutes* and manifest the deepest reverence for
Panton, P.M., all the time ; in addition to this, it
will have an unblemished record, a teetotal (or,
anyhow, a “ colonial wine ”) badge, and a written
“character” signed by nine parsons and a
Fatman. It seems to be much easier to get into
Heaven than to please Mr. Panton, P.M.
The same issue of the Argus that contained
Mr. P.’s righteous snort recorded the case of the
gaunt Angus M'Kechie found clinging to a fence
in Carlton, helpless from starvation. A thought-
ful policeman removed him, not to the lock-up,
but to the hospital. M‘Kechie had eaten little for
weeks, and nothing at all for three days, had
“slept out ” through three of the bitterest nights
known in Melbourne for 12 months, and at the
hospital looked like a specimen Cuban sufferer or
an Indian-famine photograph. It was just a
chance that he did not fall into the hands of a
constable of another sort, and be run in for being
an eyesore, and an obstruction, and a public
nuisance with no obvious means of support, and
be lectured next morning by the P.M. for not
doing his starving in a gentlemanly and unob-
trusive manner as becomes a citizen of that
glorious empire, &c.

flr. Williams’ PlNK PILLS FOR PALE PEOPL

Rheumatism,
A YEAR IN BED.
MARVELLOUS RECOVERY OF A
SYDNEY RESIDENT.
The cold days of Winter are near at hand, if
not already here. The unhappy sufferer from
rheumatism and such ailments dreads the return
of his or her complaint. There is one way, how-
ever, by which rheumatism may be positively and
permanently cured. In proof of this we append
the following statement
A reporter recently visited Mr. Walter Tye, of
33 M Arthur-street, Ultimo, Sydney, whose mar-
vellous recovery from a most severe attack of
acute rheumatism has been the subject of much
comment in that neighbourhood. Mr. Tye readily
volunteered to give the following statement of his
illness and recovery :
<7
:
~ V
I am a milkman by trade, and ride on the
cart when distributing milk. We serve a very
long round. You can readily see that the very
nature of my occupation compels me to be out in
all sorts of weather, for whatever happens we
must not disappoint our customers. It is some
eighteen months ago that, after being out in very
inclement weather, I commenced to suffer from
aching agonies in limbs and body, and flying
pains and stiffness in the joints ; and I also ex-
perienced sudden chills, generally succeeded by
heavy sweats. The pain I suffered was so severe
that sleep was an impossibility, and should I dose
off I would be suddenly aroused by spasms in the
muscles of the joints in which the rheumatism was
worst. I had the best medical advice, and the
premises of a well-known chemist were fairly ran-
sacked in search of a specific that would tend to
alleviate my agony. To make a long story short,
I was laid up for 12 months.
“ Relief, however, at last arrived, and from an
unexpected quarter.
“Boiler-maker Jarvis and his wife are near
neighbors of ours, and I had read of his wonderful
recovery from locomotor-ataxia by the agency of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Thinking they might
possibly have a beneficial effect in my own case, I
determined to try them, and did so. It was not
until after, the third box that I experienced any
decided change for the better, but you must bear
in mind that mine was a case of "long standing,
and, it was feared, one that might become chronic.
My health then improved by leaps and bounds,
and I am now, as yoti can see, strong and hearty.
I took, in all, eight boxes.
“ The nature of my business calls for the display
of considerable activity, and I am pleased to state
that I have no return of my old symptoms, and
I can face the coming winter without any anxiety.”
“ Have we your permission to publish these par-
ticulars, Mr. Tye? ”
“ Certainly you have.”
