HOTEL
Correggio Jones
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a recent Vic. Artists’ Society “smoke,” Dr. Springs
thorpe made some observations about local art which
paused his audience to writhe with suppressed rage. He
began by saying that Australians had inherited a nervous
temperament, which accounted for a predominance of
artistic feeling. In poetry, he said, there was a distinctly
Australian school, and the musical attainments of Aus-
tralians were wonderful in variety and excellence. Our
climatic conditions, which were largely those of Greece
and Borne (as though Australia were not a continent
containing all sorts of climatic conditions !) had much to
do with this. The art of painting, however, had lagged
behind, certainly in appreciation, possibly in performance
It required higher intelligence to appreciate music than
it did to appreciate painting. Here there were yells of
“No !” from the artists. The Aus. artist, continued Dr.
S., has.net yet struck a really Australian note. The key
of his color scheme was wrong. And so on. Which is all
true enough—in a way. But Dr. S. should know that he
who can merely diagnose a disease without suggesting a
remedy is a poor physician ; the doctor, no doubt, was all
the time fingering in his astral vest-pocket a visionary
box of Springthorpe’s Patent Color-Scheme Pills for Aus-
tralian Artists. . . . The trouble with the latter is that
they are too much dominated by the tyranny of the Old
World. In the matter of technique this despotism might
be called benevolent, but, in the matter of sentiment and
choice of subjects, it is maleficent.
Correggio Jones an artist was
Of pure Australian race,
But native subjects scorned because
They were too commonplace.
The Bush with all its secrets grim,
And solemn mystery,
No fascination had for him :
He had no eyes to see
The long sad spectral desert-march
Of brave Explorers dead,
Who perished—while the burning arch
Of blue laughed overhead;
The Solitary Man who stares
At the mirage so fair,
While Death steals on him unawares
And grasps him by the hair ;
The Lonely Tree that sadly stands,
With no green neighbor nigh,
And stretches forth its bleached, dead hands,
For pity, to the sky ;
The Grey Prospector, weird of dress,
And wearied overmuch,
Who dies amidst the wilderness —
With Fortune in his clutch ;
The figures of the heroes gone
Who stood forth undismayed,
And Freedom’s Flag shook forth upon
Eureka’s old stockade.
These subjects to Correggio Jones
No inspiration brought ;
He was an ass (in semi-tones)
And painted —as he thought.
“In all these things there’s no Romance,”
He muttered, with a sneer;
“ They’d never give C. Jones a chance
To make his genius clear ! ”
“ Grey gums,” he cried, “ and box-woods pale
They give my genius cramp —
But let me paint some Knights in Mail,
Or robbers in a camp.
“Now, look at those Old Masters-they
Had all the chances fine,
With churches dim, and ruins grey,
And castles on the Rhine,
“ And lady gay in minever,
And hairy-shirted saint,
And Doges in apparel fair —-
And things a man might paint!
“ And barons bold and pilgrims pale,
And battling Knight and King—
The blood-spots on their golden mail —•
And all that sort of thing !
“ Your Raphael and your Angelo
And Rubens, and such men, f
They simply had a splendid show.
Give me the same —and then ! ”
. . . • • *
So speaks Correggio Jones —yet sees,
When past is Night’s eclipse,
The Dawn come like Harpocrates,
A rose held to her lips.
The wondrous dawn that is so fair,
So young and bright and strong,
That e’en the rocks and stones to her
Sing a Memnonie song.
He will not see that our sky-hue
Old Italy’s outvies,
But still goes yearning for the blue
Of far Ausonian skies.
. . • . *
He yet is painting at full bat —
You’ll say, if him you see,
“ His body dwells on Gander Flat,”
His soul’s in Italy.
CREEVE ROE.
Katie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was always her eyes I feared. They were of
that steely-grey color, and, when they turned upon
me, seemed to think for themselves, and to ever
point me out as a naked soul exposed—eyes that,
once wronged, stared at me for all time, in the
darkness and the day, reproachful, seeking retri-
bution, compelling remorse.
I had 3een the same eyes in my mother, and, in
the madcap sins of my youth, people told me that
something was breaking her heart. I laughed at
the time; but I foolishly looked into her eyes.
And ever since, two grey spectres that seem to
moan in their pain move ever before me.
“ Dear girl,” I had said, “ I love you ! ”
And she believed it; nor was it the acting that
deceived her, for I swear before God that I spoke
the truth. It was the fatality of the time. I was
too ardent a lover to remain true long, and, when
my first attempt proved such an easy conquest, I
looked around for other faces. I found them.
Then, I suppose, I went to the dogs. Whatever
it was, it was while it lasted very pleasant life.
But my first sweet love came again with all its
tender regrets and memories, and the expres-
sion in Katie’s eyes when I told her I must
leave her had been with me all the long years,
and they looked at me unchanged when I chanced
to meet her in Sydney one day.
How altered she was! Her pretty face was
sobered by the look upon it that spoke the old,
old story.
“Katie,” I said; and, holding my hand, she
answered, “Jim.”
I asked her where was the child, and her eyes
that seemed too much in pain, too stricken to
weep, went, I thought, a trifle greyer. I grew
alarmed.
“ Not not ”
“He might be now ; will you come with me
and see?”
I went. We walked silently. Her last words
had agitated me strangely. Heath ! I had not
thought of that.
We entered a house and were soon beside a bed,
on which lay a small, wasted form. The face was
wan and very white.
“ Jimmie,” she said gently.
I started. It sounded so familiar. She was
speaking to the child.
The boy did not move or answer, and when she
knelt on the floor and took his hand in hers, we
both knew that the child was dead.
Her head had sunk in the bedclothes, and her
arms were thrown over the bed.
“Katie,” I said gently, “don’t cry.”
She lifted her head slowly and looked at me.
Her eyes were very cold and grey, but quite dry.
Instinctively I felt that I resented the want of
feeling.
“Katie,” I said bitterly, “the boy is dead.
And yet you do not weep.”
“Tears ! ” she answered vaguely.
“Forgive me,” I said, “ I forgot.”
I thought of the pain in her eyes.
I tried to comfort her, but she repelled me, still
kneeling, with her face in the bed-clothes.
“ No, no,” she murmured ; “ not novv.”
I tried to raise her head, to look her in the face,
but she resisted. Then I grew angry. I felt the
old love that never had died mastering me again ;
and I must share her grief.
“ Katie,” I said, “the child is mine as well as
yours. Give me a kiss.”
“ No, no ; your kiss would kill me. ”
Maddened, I grasped her in my arms, and
fought with her for a kiss. My utmost strength
just sufficed, and I kissed her passionately.
“ Help, help ! ” she screamed.
I rushed from the room, and out of the house,
into the street, and hurried away, away, with the
fire of her lips burning into my brain. Love had
come again to me, and all the sins I ever did
laughed in my ears “ Too late ! ”
I could not rest. For two days I roamed aim-
lessly about the city. On the third day, I could
not resist the impulse that brought me back to the
home where the child had died.
I knocked. An old woman answered the call.
I made enquiries.
“ The poor thing has gone,” was the answer;
“ she buried her little one yesterday.”
Did she know where the lady could be found ?
“ No; she went away sudden-like, without a
word to anyone.”
I turned away. I had reached the roadway,
when a sudden impulse stirred me, and I retraced
my steps.
“ Was the lady weeping ? ” I asked.
“ Bitterly, bitterly, sir; just as though her heart
was broke.”
“ Thank you,” I said.
J. J. O’M.
One-eyed Dogs-
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Knocking round the country,” said Mitchell,
in a sentimental mood, “knocking round the
country these times, a man often sees things that
hurt his heart; but I think one of the most—the
most —what’s the word ? ”
“ Pathetic ? ”
“Yes, pathetic—one of the most pathetic
things in the bush is a good sheep-dog that has
lost one eye—a one-eyed dog -or a wall-eyed one
watching you for orders or a bit to eat. I remem-
ber, when I was bush-felling down in Maorilatid
there was half-a-dozen dogs belonging to the
Maoris we had in camp, and three of those dogs
had lost an eye apiece—pig-hunting in the awful
supple-jack and bush-lawyer scrub round there ;
and, at first, I couldn’t get used to those dogs
nohow. And they soon got to know it. They'd
sit round in front of me at meal time and watch
me with their one eyes, for I couldn’t refuse them
a bit now and then. I couldn’t get any comfort
out of my tucker, anyway, while those dogs were
around—at least, not till I got used to them and
the novelty wore off a bit.
“ If that there old dog of mine was to lose one of
his eyes, after all these years I’ve had him, I’d
have to get rid of him, for I couldn’t stand him
sitting in front of me and watching me with one
eye. I had an old dog once—he vyas that there
old dog’s mate, too —and hS lost his eye one day
in the scrub; and he’d sit in front of me, with one
side of his face so bright and intelligent, and the
other so blind and helpless—and the pathetic,
pitiful way he’d turn the poor blind side of his
head round out of the road would make me feel
like I did once when a girl I was gone on told me
she was very sorry but she was sure she hadn’t-
given me any encouragement.
“ That old dog had come away from home with
me, and followed me for close up six years. He
followed ’til his other eye got bad, and one leg
went wrong, and he’d have followed still, with his;
nose at my heels, blind and on three legs, andl
there’d have been a wag in his tail for me as long
as ever that held out, but I couldn’t stand it; I.
had to get a chap to shoot him.”
And here Mitchell retired into a cloud of tobacco 1
smoke and silence and thought, perhaps, of other
dogs that had had their days.
“ Ah,” reflected Mitchell’s mate after a rather
long pause. “ Reminds ine of a blind dog I had:
once that useter walk backwards and feel his way
with his tail ”
But Mitchell snorted viciously, and his mate
wisely discontinued.
HENRY LAWSON.

Romance in the Market-place.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You stood beside the flowers,
Yourself a flower;
And on your face
The twilight stayed another hour,
It shone so pale ;
And all around men talked as in a market-
place.
I heard them talk, and felt
No interest stir
In what they said.
Roses were nigh you, and upon the air
The breath of love,
And all about the world moved on with
nervous tread.
I heard it not; far down
And round about
My soul you drew
The veils that shut the loud earth out,
And I and you
Were there alone, no one beside but I and
you !
What words were those we said ?
Old ones, perchance,
Pale with the pain
Of all who’ve kissed and talked romance,
And sighed farewell,
And mixed their tears and kissed, and sighed
farewell again.
We stood a sainted while,
And then your hand
Sought to be free,
And you were gone and all the land
Was under gloom,
And lamps were lit for other men, but none
for me.
I stood and watched you go,
And suddenly
The loud world grew,
Like some great-voiced, insetting sea;
And men went by,
Talking of trade and war and all but love and
you.
RODERIC QUINN.
Scotus : “ Notable that this year is the
jubilee of the memorable ’4B; in which year
icturning one day from school in Glasgow, I came
upon a confectioner’s stock scattered all about
the street. Further on was a jeweller’s stock in
the same plight, and close by a dray-load of flour
ripped from the bags and piled up in the middle
j This was the beginning of the
isread Riots, which it took the military three
days to quell. Similar riots occurred in various
other towns of Great Britain, and on the Continent
confusion was at its height in the French Revolu-
tion, while all over Europe Kings and Queens
were bolting, and thrones tottering. Is this year
of world-wide unrest to witness a repetition of
such scenes ? ”
On Our Selection.
THE OTHER WAY ABOUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It had been a dull, miserable day, and a cold
westerly was blowing. Dave and Joe were at the
barn finishing up for the day.
Dad was inside grunting and groaning with
toothache. He had had it a week, and was nearly
mad. For a while he sat by the fire, prodding
the tooth with his pocket-knife ; then he covered
his jaw with his hand and went out and walked
about the yard.
Joe asked him if he had seen the red heifer any-
where that day. He didn’t answer.
“Did y’ see the red heifer any place ter dav
Dad?” '
“ Damn the red heifer ! and Dad went inside
again.
He walked round and round the table and in
and out the back room till mother nearly cried
with pity. <
“Isn’t it any easier at all, father?” she said
coinmi serati ngly.
“ How the devil can it be easier ? . . Oh-h ! ”
The kangaroo-dog had coiled himself snugly' on
a a? be , fl ? re the tire ' Dad kicked him savagely
and told him to get out. The dog slunk sulkily
to the door, his tail between his legs, and his back
humped a,s if expecting another kick. He got it.
Dad sat in the ashes then, and groaned lament-
ably. Ihe dog walked in at the back door and
dropped on the bag again.
Joe came in to say that “ two coves out there
wanted somethink.”
Dad paid no attention.
The “ two coves a pressman, in new leggings,
and Canty, the storekeeper—came in. Mother
brought a light. Dad moaned, but didn’t look up.
Well, Mr. Ross,” the pressman commenced
L 6 “l’m from the
(something-or-other) office. I’m—er—after infor-
mation about the crops round here. I suppose—
Oh-h-h ! Dad groaned, opening his mouth
over the fire, and pressing the tooth hard with his
thumb.
The pressman stared at him for awhile ; then
grinned at the storekeeper, and made a derisive
face at Dad’s back. Then-“ What have you
got in this season, Mr. Ross ? Wheat ? ”
“ I don't know. .. . Oh-h—it’s awful! ”
Another silence.
“Didn’t think toothache so bad as that said
the man of news, airily, addressing mother.
.Never had it much myself, you see ! ”
He looked at Dad again ; then winked slyly at
and said to Dad, in an altered tone:
Whisky s a good thing for it, old man, if you’ve
got any.” ’ J
Nothing but a groan came from Dad, but
mother shook her head sadly in the negative.
Any oil of tar ? ”
Mother brightened up. “ There’s a little oil in
the house she said, “but I don’t know if we’ve
an J tar. Is there, Joe—in that old drum ? ”
Nurh.
The Press looked out the window. Dad com-
menced to butcher his gums with the pocket-knife
and threatened to put the fire out with blood and
Let s Have a look at the tooth, old man,” the
pressman said,- approaching Dad.
Dad submitted.
Pooh ! —lll take that out in one act!” .. .

Joe couldn’t find a piece of string, but produced
a kangaroo-tail sinew that had been tied round a
calf s neck.
The pressman was enthusiastic. He buzzed
about and talked dentistry in a most learned
manner. Then he had another squint at Dad’s
tooth.

Dad complied like a lamb.
Hold the light down here, missis—a little
lower. You gentlemen (to Canty and Dave) look
atter his legs and arms. Now, let your head come
back right back, and open your mouth —wide as

.Keep him down! ” and the others kept him
down.
. Dad’s neck was stretching like a gander’s, and
it looked as if his head would come off. The den-
tist threw his shoulders into it like a crack
oarsman—there was a crack, a rip, a tear, and,
like a young tree leaving the ground, two huge,
ugly old teeth left Dad’s jaw on the end of that
sinew.
Holy! ” cried the dentist, surprised, and we
stared. Little Bill made for the teeth ; so did
Joe, and there was a fight under the table.
Dad sat in a lump on the floor propping himself
up with his hands ; his head dropped forward and
he spat feebly on the floor.
The pressman laughed and slapped Dad on the
back, and asked how he felt—“ old boy ! ” Dad
shook his head and spat and spat. But presently
he wiped his eyes with his shirt-sleeve and looked
up. Ihe pressman told mother she ought to be
proud of Dad. Dad struggled to his feet then,
Pt < ? p u t smiling. The pressman shook hands
with him, and in no time Dad was laughing and
joking over the operation. A pleased look was in
mother s face; happiness filled the home again,
and we grew quite fond of that pressman—he was
so jolly and affable, and made himself so much at
home, mother said.
‘‘Now, sit over, and we’ll have supper,” said
Dad, proud of having some, fried steak to offer the
visitors. We had killed a cow the evening before
—one that was always getting bogged in the dam
and taking up much of Dad’s time dragging her
out and cutting greenstuff to keep her alive. The
visitors enjoyed her. The pressman wanted salt
None was on the table. Dad told Joe to run and
get some—to be quick. Joe went out, but in a
while returned. He stood at the door with the
hammer m his hand and said

-kilky was lickin' it this evenin’, Dave ? ”
Dave reached for the bread.
“Don’t bother—don’t bother about it,” said
the pressman. “ Sit down, youngster, and finish
your supper.”
“No bother at all,” Dad said; but Joe sat
down, and Dad scowled at him.
Then Dad got talking about wheat and walla-

Oh-h ! ”

we roared —all but the pressman.
Oh-h ! ” he said, and went to the fire. Dad

We ate on. The pressman continued to moan.
Dad turned on his seat. “ What paper, mister,
do you say you come from ? ”
‘‘Oh-h ! . . . Oh-h, Lord !”

Here,” continued Dad—“let’s have a look at

STEELE RUDD.
From a paper read by the Rev. A. Rivett
at the half-yearly meeting of the Victorian Con-
gregational Union
The church was not making-headway. The income of
the union was but one-third of what it was 10 years ago,
while the churches and mission stations were fewer in
number. The college, the new church sites fund, and
other projects were but memories. Some new church
polity was urgently needed to rescue the church. There
were but three courses open—Slow but decorous extinc-
birth’ amalganlatl0n with some other church ; or a new
Same wail with all the sectaries. The world is
obviously getting one eye open.
From “ In Memoriam ” columns of a Vic.
provincial paper :—
Many a lonely hour,
Many a sleepless night,
I think of you, my husband,
In the sad and lonely night.
I linger in my lonely home,
And think of former days,
Of all his kindly deeds and words,
And his many various ways.
And as before me seems to rise
His manly form and brow,
I grieve because what once was life
Is only memory now.
—Inserted by his sorrowing wife.
